The author of this book, having completed for you, my children, this book, which is a complete part of the science of medicine, and having reached the peak of clarity and explanation in it, saw fit to complete it for you with this chapter, which is a part of the practical application of the hand.
Because practical application of the hand is entirely lacking in our country and our time, so much so that its knowledge is nearly forgotten and its influence has ceased. However, only a few traces of it remain in the books of the ancients, which have been corrupted by hands, affected by errors and confusion, until their meanings became obscure and their benefit distanced.
Therefore, I saw fit to revive it and compose this chapter in the manner of explanation, clarification, and summarization, and to provide illustrations of cauterizing irons and other tools of the craft, since it adds to the clarity and encompasses what is necessary.
The reason there is no skilled practitioner with a skilled hand in our time is because the craft of medicine is lengthy, and its practitioner must first diligently study the anatomy, as described by Galen. So that he grasps the benefits of the organs, their arrangement, temperaments, connections, and separations; the knowledge of bones, nerves, muscles, their number and exits; arteries, veins, and their points of exit.
Therefore, Hippocrates said that physicians are many in name, but few in action, especially in the practical craft. We have mentioned a portion of this in the introduction to this book.
For whoever is not knowledgeable about the anatomy we have described will inevitably make mistakes that kill people, as I have witnessed many who claimed knowledge in this field without understanding or study.
I saw an ignorant physician who opened a scrofulous tumor on a woman's neck, then severed some of the neck's arteries, so the woman bled to death before his eyes.
I saw another physician who attempted to remove a stone from the bladder of an elderly man.  The stone was large, and he acted rashly, removing it with a piece of the bladder wall. The man died about three days later. I was called to remove it, and I saw the size of the stone and the patient's condition, and I judged that I could not have done it.
And I saw another physician who earned a living by practicing medicine among the merchants of our town.
A dark-skinned boy had a broken leg near his heel, along with a wound. The physician, in his ignorance, quickly bound the fracture to the wound with strong splints and bandages, leaving no room for the wound to breathe.
Then he indulged his desires, then left him for days, ordering him not to loosen the bandages until his leg and foot swelled up and he was close to death.
So I was called to him, and quickly loosened the bandages.  He gained relief and was freed from his pain, but the damage had already taken hold of the limb, and I was unable to stop it.
The damage continued to spread in the limb until he died.
I saw another physician treat a cancerous tumor.  After days, it ulcerated, and the affliction of the patient worsened.  This is because if the cancer is purely of black humor, it should not be treated with iron at all.
Unless it is in a limb that can be completely removed.
Therefore, my son, you should know that manual work is divided into two categories: work that is accompanied by safety, and work that, in most cases, leads to injury.
I have warned in every place in this book about the work that involves risk and fear.  You should avoid and reject it.
So that the ignorant may not find a way to speak ill and criticize, be firm and cautious, and gentle and steadfast in your care of patients.
Use the best path leading to safety and a good outcome, and avoid dangerous diseases difficult to cure.
Keep yourselves away from anything that you fear might bring doubt upon your religion and your worldly affairs.  This is the lasting safeguard for your reputation and the highest elevation of your status in this world and the next.
Galen said in some of his precepts: Do not treat a disease poorly, lest you become a poor physician.
This article has been divided into three chapters.
The first chapter.
In cauterization by fire and cauterization by sharp medicine, a well-ordered treatise from the heel to the foot, detailing instruments and cauterizing irons, and everything necessary for manual practice.
And the second chapter.
On incision, extraction, phlebotomy, cupping, surgeries, removing arrows, and similar procedures, all categorized and ordered, with illustrations of the instruments.
And the third chapter.
On fractures, dislocations, treatment of sprains, and similar conditions, categorized and ordered from the heel to the foot, with illustrations of the instruments.
The first chapter.
On cauterization.
Before describing the procedure, it is necessary to mention its benefits and drawbacks, and in what temperament it is used.
I say that discussing the benefits and drawbacks of cauterization, its precise science, and hidden secret, is a long discussion and a subtle knowledge, about which many wise men have spoken.
And they differed in their opinions; I have summarized the essentials of their discourse, for fear of being too verbose.
I say that cauterization is generally beneficial for all bad temperaments, whether with matter or without, except for two temperaments: a hot temperament without matter and a dry temperament without matter.
As for the dry temperament with matter, they differed in opinion. Some said that cauterization is beneficial in it, while others argued the opposite, that cauterization is not suitable for an illness arising from heat and dryness, because the nature of fire is heat and dryness.
It is impossible to be cured of a hot and dry illness with a hot and dry remedy.  The one who says otherwise asserts that cauterization with fire may benefit from a hot and dry illness occurring in people's bodies. Because when you add a person's body and its moisture to the temperament of fire, you make the person's body cold.
And I say as he says, because experience has shown me this many times, however, this matter should only be considered by someone who has trained extensively in the art of cauterization. And he must understand the diverse temperaments of people, and the nature of illnesses themselves, their causes, symptoms, and duration.
As for other temperaments, there is no danger from them, especially cold and moist diseases. All physicians agree on this and do not differ on the benefit of cauterization in these cases.
Know, my children, that the secret of cauterization with fire and its superiority over cauterization with a burning medicine lies in this: fire is a simple element whose effect is limited to the organ cauterized, and it harms a connected organ only slightly.
Cauterization with a burning medicine, however, may extend its effect to other organs and perhaps induce a difficult-to-treat illness or even death in the affected organ. Fire, by its nobility and the purity of its essence, does not cause this unless it is used excessively.
This has been made clear to us through experience, due to the length of our service and care in the craft, and through our understanding of the truth of the matter.
Therefore, I have dispensed with lengthy discussion.  Had it not been inappropriate for this book, I would have presented to you a profound secret about fire, its effect on the body, and its ability to dispel disease, using a rigorous and logical philosophical argument that might be beyond your understanding.
Know, my children, that there is disagreement concerning the time most suitable for cauterization, and they consider spring to be the best time.
I say that cauterization is suitable at all times, since the harm caused by time is overcome by the benefit derived from cauterization. Especially if the cauterization is for a strong, necessary affliction, a delay for which is intolerable, for fear that it may lead to a greater calamity than the slight harm caused by time.
Do not, my children, consider the misconception of the common people and ignorant physicians, that cauterization, which cures a disease, will never return, and you regard it as a bane.
This is not as you suppose, for cauterization is like a medicine that changes the temperament and dries the moisture, which is the cause of the afflictions.
However, cauterization is superior to medication because of its swift success, powerful effect, and intense influence.
The disease may return at some time, depending on the patient's temperament, the strength and severity of the illness, the accumulation of humors in the body, and neglect of acquiring proper nourishment, and similar causes.
Unless the disease for which cauterization is used is a mild one, affecting a part of the body with few humors and moisture. For example, cauterizing a tooth for pain, and similar cases, the pain may not return, and this is the least that can be expected.
And as for the common saying that cauterization is the final treatment, it is a correct statement, not in the way they believe, since they think that no treatment can benefit after cauterization, either medicinal or otherwise, but the matter is different.
The meaning of "cauterization is the final treatment" is that, when we have employed various treatments for a disease and those remedies prove ineffective, then if cauterization is ultimately successful, that is why cauterization is called the final treatment. Not in the sense understood by the common people and many ignorant physicians.
The ancients stated that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron.  They said this because of the moderation of gold and the nobility of its essence, and that the cauterized area does not fester.  However, this is not universally true.
For I have tested it and found that it only does this in some bodies and not others; and cauterization with gold is better and superior to iron, as they have said. However, if you heat the gold cautery in the fire, you will not discern when it has been heated to the desired degree for the redness of gold.
Because it is quickly chilled, and if you increase its heat in a fever, it melts in the fire and becomes deformed, so the artisan is occupied with this.  Therefore, cauterization with iron is faster and more practically sound for use, God willing.
I have organized this chapter on cauterization into sections, arranging them from head to foot, so that the student may readily find what he desires, God willing.
Chapter One.
In cauterizing the head, a single cauterization.
This cauterization is beneficial for the excess moisture and cold in the brain, which are causes of headaches, frequent colds descending from the head to the eyes and ears, excessive sleepiness, toothaches, and sore throats. And generally, for all illnesses resulting from cold, such as paralysis, epilepsy, and stupor, and similar ailments.
The procedure for this cauterization is that the patient should first be made to vomit with a purgative medicine that clears the head for three or four nights, according to the patient's strength, age, and habits.
Then, order him to shave his head with a razor, then seat him before you, squatting, with his hands on his chest.
Then, place the base of your palm on the base of his nose between his eyes.  Where your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then heat the olive-wood cautery, which is of this shape.
If the bone is exposed to the size of a thread or a carob seed, lift your hand. Otherwise, reheat your iron, or use another if it has cooled. Until you see the bone as I described.  Then take some salt, dissolve it in water, and soak a piece of cotton in it.  Place it on the affected area and leave it for three days.
Then apply a piece of cotton soaked in clarified butter and leave it until the inflammation from the burn subsides.  Then treat it with the four-part ointment until it heals, God willing.
And they say that the longer a wound remains open, the more pus it produces, and that this is better and more beneficial.
Some have said that the skin should be cauterized to the bone, and the cautery should be held until some of the bone's thickness is burned. Then the burned portion of the bone should be scraped off, and then treated.
Others said that the cauterization should be vigorous, affecting the bone strongly, so that it falls away like a small, granular or scaly fragment.  They claimed that this expels fumes from the head, and that the wound should be left open for a long time.
Then it is treated until it heals.  I do not at all consider these two types of cauterization suitable for everyone, but rather a matter of chance. I prefer to avoid it, and safety is best.  For if the head's natural connections are disrupted, it weakens, as we have observed in other parts of the body.
Especially if the patient's head is naturally weak. The first type of cauterization is safer and better in my opinion.  Use it, and God willing you will be safe.
Chapter Two.
On cauterization of the head as well.
If a chronic headache occurs throughout the head, and this persists in the patient, and if poultices, plasters, flogging, ointments, and dressings have been used, especially if the single cauterization we described has not helped, then examine. If the patient's head is strong in constitution and not weak, and he experiences severe cold, cauterize again, a little above the first cauterization. Then cauterize each horn of his head, until the thickness of the skin is gone and the amount of bone we described is exposed. Cauterize a spot at the back of his head, in the area known as the "axe" (or "the place of the axe").  Be gentle here; do not expose the bone, as the patient will experience intense pain, unlike the pain of the other head cauterizations.  I will describe this cauterization in its place.
The cautery used for the horns and back of the head should be gentler than the cautery used for the middle of the head.  This is its image (or form).
Chapter Three.
For cauterization of the non-chronic sister (or sibling).
If, in a head fissure, pain occurs with headache, and the pain extends to the eye, then the patient should be purged with medications that clear the head.  Employ all other treatments that I have mentioned in the classifications of diseases.  If this is unsuccessful, then cauterization is possible in two ways. Either by cauterization with a sharp, burning medicine, or by iron.
As for cauterization with the burning medicine, one takes a single clove of garlic, peels it, and cuts off its ends. Then, with a broad scalpel, one cuts open the painful area of the temple until a space beneath the skin is created, large enough to accommodate the clove.
Then, insert it under the skin until it disappears, then apply a firm pressure to it, and leave it for fifteen hours. Then, remove it and take out the garlic. Allow the wound to heal for two or three days, and then dress it.
Apply a cotton swab dipped in ghee until the area becomes suppurated. Then, treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If you wish, you can do this with some of the burning medicines that I have provided in the eighteenth chapter on burning medicines.
As for cauterizing it with iron, it is done in this manner: Heat the cautery iron, which is depicted as follows:
It is called the nail-shaped cautery because its head resembles a nail, with some grooves and a small protrusion in the middle.
Then place it on the painful area, holding your hand while slowly rotating the iron. The amount burned through the skin's thickness should be about half of it. Raise your hand so as not to burn the artery underneath and cause bleeding, then soak a cotton swab in salt water and place it on the area, leaving it for three days. Then wrap the cotton in oil, and treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.
If you wish, cauterize this abscess with the pointed knife-like part of the cautery, protecting yourself from cutting the artery in this particular abscess, especially if it is chronic.
Chapter Four.
Treating chronic abscesses.
If you have treated the abscess with the aforementioned treatment and the treatments mentioned in the sections on diseases, but the treatment has not been successful, and you observe a cause that does not respond to the initial cauterization with medicine or fire. Then you should heat the knife-like cautery until it whitens, after marking the area of pain with a line half a finger in length or similar, and you should withdraw your hand only once, while pulling it until the artery is cut and it reaches towards the bone.
However, you should be careful not to touch the jaw that moves during chewing, so as not to burn the muscle or nerve that moves it, and thus cause spasm.
And be cautious and careful of the bleeding from the artery you have cut, for there is danger in cutting it. Especially for one who does not know what to do and has not been a practiced practitioner, abandoning the work is better.
The treatment of accidental bleeding from the artery will be discussed in its place in the book, God willing, if you see an ailment that this cauterization cannot address.
If you see the patient's body able to tolerate it, cauterize a spot in the middle of the head, as we have described, and treat the wound until it heals, God willing.
If you wish, use the cauterization mentioned in the chapter on ligating the artery with the double-bladed cautery; it is a better and more effective cauterization than this.
Fifth Chapter.
On cauterizing earaches.
If an earache due to cold occurs and is treated with purgatives and other treatments mentioned in the classification, but the pain does not subside, cauterize the ear with the cautery, the so-called "point," depicted in this image.
Then, after heating it, apply it in a circling motion around the entire ear, or around both ears if the pain is in them. And move the cautery slightly away from the base of the ear after marking the area with ink. The cauterization should be about ten points to each ear or similar. Then, treat the affected areas until healed, God willing.
Chapter Six.
Concerning cauterization with a hot iron.
The affliction treated by cauterization is of the type that arises from phlegm, as I have mentioned in the classifications of diseases.
One should avoid cauterizing the type that arises from dryness and nerve tension.
If you have treated this type of affliction with poultices, massages, and gargles and your treatment has been unsuccessful, then the afflicted person should be cauterized three times. One at the base of the ear, the second slightly below the temple, and the third at the junction of the lips.
Make your cauterization on the opposite side of the affected area, because relaxation occurs on the side that appears healthy.
The cauterization should be performed with one cauterization in line with the upper part of the ear, slightly below the temple, and another in the temple, with a length equal to that of the thumb.
Apply the cauterization with your hand until you burn approximately half the thickness of the skin. This is the image of the cautery iron, which is a type of scalpel, whose image was previously presented, but it is slightly gentler than it, as you can see. The knife should have a slight excess thickness, then the affected area should be treated as previously mentioned, until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven.
On cauterization for chronic stroke.
If the stroke becomes chronic and treatment as described was ineffective, and the patient does not have a fever, cauterize four times. One cauterization on each temple, one cauterization in the middle of the head as previously mentioned, and one cauterization at the back of the head as described before.
Using the cautery method as previously stated, a cauterization may also be applied to the mouth of the stomach, which will be more effective. Then, treat according to the preceding method.
Chapter Eight.
On Cauterization for Forgetfulness.
The patient afflicted with phlegmatic forgetfulness should first be given large doses of emetics and purgatives that clear the brain.
Then, shave his entire head and apply a mustard poultice, as described in the chapter on dressings, repeatedly.  It is a form of cauterization.
And do this in the same manner as described there. If he is cured, then stop. Otherwise, apply three cauterizations to the back of his head, aligned from the top of the head to the bottom of the neck.
Place the thickness of a finger between each cauterization.  Then treat the cauterization as previously described. If you wish to increase the treatment and the patient can tolerate it, cauterize the middle cauterization.
If you wish to increase the treatment, cauterize the temples, then treat it until he is cured. The cauterization tool should be olive-shaped, as previously described.
Chapter Nine.
On cauterization for paralysis and general relaxation of the body.
First, purify the head with the applications mentioned.  Then shave the patient's head and cauterize one spot in the middle of the head, one on each temple, and one on the back of the head.
And three cauterizations on the cervical vertebrae.  If the illness of bodily relaxation requires more than that, and the patient is able to endure it and the illness is strong and entrenched, cauterize four spots on the vertebrae of the back. Cauterize deeply enough to burn most of the skin, then lift your hand. Then treat as previously described until he is healed, God willing.  Use an olive oil-based cautery.
Chapter Ten.
On cauterizing epilepsy.
Only the epileptic whose epilepsy is due to phlegm should be cauterized. First, his brain should be cleansed with large cupping glasses and with all the treatment we have mentioned in the section if the patient is large and able to take the medications.
However, if he is a child who cannot tolerate medication, then gargles and mouthwashes that cleanse the brain should be used many days before, along with improving his diet.
Then, shave his head and then cauterize with one cautery in the center of the head, as described previously, and another cautery at his back. And on each temple of his head, a cautery.  If the patient is strong and can tolerate it, cauterize with the cauteries mentioned in the section on paralysis and relaxation of the body, on the cervical and dorsal vertebrae.  The cautery should be olive-shaped, as described previously.
If the patient is a child, make the cautery gentle, in this manner.
Chapter Eleven.
Concerning the cauterization of melancholia.
If the cause of melancholia is corrupted humours and thick phlegm, cauterize with the cauteries we described in the section on paralysis.
If the cause of melancholia is an excess of black bile and the patient's body is moist, purge what will cleanse his brain, as was previously discussed in the division.
Then shave the patient's head, then make a tight cake of flax like a circle, then place it in the center of his head, and the patient sits upright holding on from each side. Then take one pound of aged sheep's fat.
Then heat it over the fire to a moderate heat, as much as the finger can tolerate if placed inside, then pour it into the circle on the center of his head and leave it until it cools. Do this with the patient once a week with the rest of his good treatment until he is cured, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize it with many small, scattered punctures without holding the cautery in one place, but rather by stroking. This type of cauterization moistens the brain moderately.
Then apply a cloth soaked in clarified butter or chicken fat to it.
Chapter twelve.
On cauterizing the water that flows from the eye.
If you initially determine that the water flowing from the eye displays the signs mentioned in the classification, then promptly treat the afflicted person.  Cleanse their head, protecting it from all moisture, and have them sweat in a bath on an empty stomach for several days.
Then shave their head and cauterize the center of the head, and cauterize the temples twice if the water is flowing from both eyes, or once on the affected temple if only one eye is involved.
Then, with the cautery, cut all the veins and arteries under the skin, ensuring the cauterizations extend lengthwise across the temples, and take precautions to prevent bleeding.
If you see something of it, cut it off at the point with any available remedy.  We will come to the wisdom of binding arteries and cutting them, and how to prevent bleeding.
Two strong cauterizations may be applied to the back of the head, beneath the bones.
Chapter Thirteen.
On cauterizing chronic tears.
If the tears of the eye are chronic and persistent, and originate from veins and arteries on the surface of the head, originating from outside the head, and you are certain that it is due to excess thick cold phlegm, then apply the cautery that I described at the beginning of the descending water. A cauterization in the middle of the head, two cauterizations on the temples, and two cauterizations at the back of the head below the bones; and if further cauterization is needed, then apply a cauterization on each side of the eye's corner at the edge of the eyebrow with a small cautery iron.
Chapter Fourteen.
On cauterizing foul odors of the nose.
If treatment with what we have mentioned in the classification is unsuccessful, then quickly apply cupping to the patient three nights, then shave his head and cauterize the middle area with the olive-oil cautery. Then cauterize with the nail cautery two points above the eyebrows, slightly beneath the hair, taking care to avoid the artery, not to cut it.  This is the image of the nail cautery.
Chapter Fifteen.
On cauterizing relaxation of the eyelid.
If the eyelid is relaxed due to illness or moisture, cauterize the eyelid once with this crescent-shaped cautery.
And if you wish, cauterize slightly above the eyebrows, two points on each side, spaced apart from the temples, and the length of each cautery should be the length of the eyebrow.
Do not overdo the cauterization, but only to the point where one-third of the skin is burned.  The shape of the cautery should be like this.
Chapter Sixteen.
On cauterizing the eyelid.
If the eyelashes turn inward and injure the eye, then the cauterization is of two types. Either by fire or by a burning medicine.  If cauterization is by fire, then instruct the patient beforehand to remove the eyelashes if he is the sort who plucks them, until they grow long and straight.
If it injured the eye while the eyelashes are growing, then bind his eyes with a bandage so that they do not move until they grow.  Once they have grown and straightened, place the patient's head on your lap, then mark the eyelid with ink in the shape of a leaf-like as.
Begin the mark near the eyelashes. Then place under the eyelid a cotton swab soaked in egg white or the saliva of the *basarqatun*.  Then heat a cautery iron, this is its image.
Then cauterize in the shape you have marked, little by little, several times, until the surface of the skin that is in the shape of the entire leaf-like as is completely burned.  A sign of successful work is to see that the eyelid has shrunk and the hair has risen from the eye's flesh.
Then remove your hand and leave him for three days. Then apply a cotton swab with ghee until the scab falls off. Then treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing. If any hair grows back later and the eyelid relaxes.
Then repeat the cauterization on that spot as you did at first. If the hair is in the lower eyelid, cauterize it until it returns to its natural position and does not injure the eye.  As for cauterization with a burning medicine, tell the patient to let the eyelashes grow long and straight.
Then make a shape of an as-leaf from paper. Then take from the usual soap and quicklime, one dirham or thereabouts from each, grind them both well, and do this quickly so that they do not dry. Then spread some of this mixture on the paper you have shaped like an as-leaf, and place it on the one or both eyelids. Place a cotton swab soaked in egg white under the eye, and hold the patient's head in your lap.
Place your index finger over the medicine and gently press it while moving it whenever the patient feels the medicine's sting, because he will feel a stinging sensation like fire. As long as he feels the sting, leave the medicine and move it with your finger.  Once the stinging subsides, remove the medicine and wash the eye with water.
And see if you see the eyelid has risen, as it rises when cauterized with fire or cut. Otherwise, repeat the application of the medicine to the area where it did not take effect and did not darken until you complete your work and the eye is cauterized. Then apply cotton with oil to remove the irritation of the burning.
Then treat it with the palm ointment or another, until it heals.  You should be extremely careful during the procedure to avoid any medicine falling into the eye. If the eyelid loosens after a few days, and you are afraid of something like hair getting into the eye.
Then reapply the medicine to the loosened area of the eyelid specifically, as you did at first, and treat it until it heals. This is an image of the as-leaf.
Know that people's eyes vary in size and age, therefore, your cauterization should be adjusted accordingly.  The correct method is not hidden from someone experienced in this craft.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cauterizing the fistula that appears in the eye's corner.
If you have treated the fistula as described in the sections on diseases, and your treatment has not been successful, then you should cauterize it in this manner.
Tell the patient to place their head in your lap, and have an assistant hold the head firmly, preventing any movement or shaking.
Then place a cotton ball soaked in egg white or in the juice of a prickly pear on their eyes, and then heat the cauterizing iron, which is depicted like this.
It should be hollow, like the shaft of a bird of prey's feather, on the side used for cauterization, and if you wish, the other side can be pierced, or if you wish, it can be solid like a rod.  However, this hollow form is better for your work, God willing.
Then, squeeze the fistula if it is open, and remove the matter from it and dry it, or if it is not open, then open it and remove the pus.  Then apply the very hot cauterizing iron to it, holding it with your hand until you reach the bone.
Move your hand slightly when cauterizing, from the eye towards the nose, so that your hand does not miss the mark or the patient become upset and the cautery falls on the eyelid, damaging it. If you reach the bone on the first cauterization, then do otherwise; if not, repeat the cauterization again if necessary.
Leave it for three days, then apply a cotton ball with oil and treat it with a drying ointment until it heals. If forty days pass and it does not heal, apply a strong, corrosive medicine until the bone is exposed, and treat it as described in the relevant chapter, God willing.
Another method of cauterizing the fistula, mentioned by some earlier authors, is to go to the location of the fistula, slit it open, and then place a thin funnel in the slit.  This is its form.
Pour into it an amount of molten lead equal to the weight of a dirham.  Hold the funnel firmly in your hand, tightly held, and the patient must not move at all so that the molten lead does not drip into his eye.  You should place a cotton ball soaked in egg white or water on the patient's eye.
The lead will burn the fistula and heal it in an amazing way. If the fistula heals with the cauterization and treatment described, then it is well. Otherwise, it is necessary to use nasal perforation and return the fistula to its nasal passage as will be explained in the specific section.
Chapter Eighteen.
On cauterizing the cleft lip.
A cleft often occurs in the lip, called the hair, especially in the lips of young boys.  If, after treating this cleft with the methods we mentioned in the classification, the treatment is ineffective, then heat a small, knife-shaped cautery in this manner.
Make its cavity as thin as a knife, then place it hot and quickly into the same cleft until the cauterization reaches the depth of the cleft, and then treat it with the Qiyaruti until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Nineteen.
On cauterizing the fistula occurring in the mouth.
If a tumor appears at the base of the gums, or in the palate, or at the roots of the teeth, then ulcerates and bursts, and from the flow of pus a fistula results, and if treatment fails, then a cautery should be heated to the size suitable for the fistula.
Then, insert it hot into the fistula's opening, and hold until the heated iron reaches its depth and end. Do this once or twice, then treat it afterward with the treatment we mentioned until it heals, God willing.
If the matter is severed and healed, then well and good. Otherwise, the area must be examined, and the corrupt bone must be removed, as will be explained in its chapter, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
Loose teeth and gums.
If the gums have become loose due to moisture and the teeth have moved, and treatment with medicines has been ineffective, place the patient's head in your lap, then heat the cautery iron, whose image will come after this, after placing the tube on the tooth.
Quickly insert the hot cautery iron into it, holding your hand for a little while until the patient feels the heat of the fire has reached the root of the tooth, then remove your hand, then repeat the cautery iron several times as desired. Afterwards, have the patient fill his mouth with salt water.
He should hold it for an hour and then spit it out.  The loose tooth will become fixed and the loose gums will tighten, and the putrid moisture will dry up.
Chapter Twenty-one.
On cauterizing toothache.
If the toothache is from cold or contains worms and medicine has not helped, cauterization can be done in two ways: either with oil or with fire.
As for cauterization with butter: take cow's butter, boil it in an iron spoon or shell. Then take a piece of cotton, wrap it around the end of a probe, dip it in the boiled butter, and place it on the aching tooth quickly, holding it until it cools.
Then repeat this several times until the heat reaches the root of the tooth. Alternatively, you can dip wool or cotton in cold butter and place it on the aching tooth, then place a heated iron on top to bring the heat to the base of the tooth.
As for cauterizing with fire: take a copper or iron tube, somewhat thick-walled, so that the heat of the fire does not reach the patient's mouth. Then heat a cautery instrument, place it on the same tooth, and hold it until it cools.  Repeat this several times.
The pain will then disappear either on that day or the next.  Following the cauterization, the patient should fill his mouth with good butter and hold it for an hour, then spit it out.  This is the image of the cautery instrument.
Cauterize with whichever end you prefer, as necessary.  This is the image of the tube.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On cauterizing swine.
If the swine are affected by phlegm and cold humors, and do not respond to ripening with medications, and you desire rapid ripening, heat the hollow cautery, the image of which is shown with both ends perforated.
To allow smoke to emerge from the opposite end during cauterization, apply it to the same tumor repeatedly, if necessary, until you reach the depth of the tumor. If the tumor is small, make the cautery the same size as the tumor.
Then leave it for three days and apply a cotton ball soaked in ghee to the area until what was burned by the fire is gone. Then treat it with ointment and sutures until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-three.
In cauterization for hoarseness of voice and shortness of breath.
If humidity predominates in the trachea, especially if accompanied by a cold temperament, the patient should first be made to vomit with laxative medications.  Then, a cautery should be applied to a point on the neck, near the base of the throat, at the low point.
Be careful not to reach the throat with the cautery, and only burn the skin to a half-degree.  Then cauterize another spot at the neck joint, just past the last bead of the neck.  The cautery iron should be nail-shaped, as described previously.
Then treat him with what has been mentioned previously until he is healed, God willing.
Chapter Twenty-four.
On cauterizing lung disease and coughing.
If the cough and lung disease are due to cold dampness, and the patient does not have a fever or consumption, and the illness is chronic, cauterize twice above the collarbones, in the soft, empty, low-lying areas, and once more in the middle of the chest between the breasts.
The cauterizing iron should be pointed, as previously described.  If you prefer, use a dripping cauterizing iron, the one called "the point," a design previously shown in the treatment of ear pain.  The number of drips should be between thirty and about that number.
Then treat him/her with what was previously mentioned until he/she is cured.  One may also use a three-pronged cauterizing iron, as depicted, for a quicker treatment as it allows three cauterizations in one application. This is the image of the iron.
Chapter Twenty-five.
Cauterization of the armpit.
If the head of the humerus has dislocated due to slippery moisture, or if, after returning it from dislocation, it does not remain fixed, so that it dislocates again at the slightest movement, as we have observed, then the joint must first be reduced. Then the patient lies on their back or on their right side, and the skin inside the armpit is pulled upward with the fingers of your left hand, if the joint has dislocated inward.
Then the double-pointed cautery, whose image is this, is heated.
Then, with it, the skin is cauterized until it penetrates to the other side. The shape of the cauterization is four cauterizations.  It may also be cauterized with a three-pointed cautery, in which case the shape of the cauterization is six cauterizations, and the points are according to the thickness of the tissue.
And this is the image of the three-pointed cautery.
And one more may be added to this number, making eight cauterizations. Then the beaten balls with salt are applied to the cauterization.  The patient should be given rest and not move the affected limb for a time until it strengthens.  If the dislocation is upward, it is rare.
Cauterize above the shoulder blade with one good, nail-like cauterization or many small cauterizations.  Then the joint will strengthen, the moisture will disappear, and the patient will recover.
Chapter Twenty-six.
In cauterizing the stomach.
If the stomach suffers from cold and excessive moisture, disrupting its temperament, and frequent discharges occur despite various treatments being ineffective, the patient should lie on their back, extend their legs and arms, and then receive three cauterizations. Cauterize beneath the breastbone, a distance equivalent to a finger's breadth, using a nail-like cautery iron.  Then cauterize with two additional cauteries, one each below the flanks of the first, so the cauteries form a triangle. Keep a space between them so that they do not unite when suppurating. The depth of the cauterization should be roughly two-thirds the thickness of the skin.  The shape of the cauteries should be [a particular form, implied].
In this way, and to this extent, no more.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a single large cautery in the center of the stomach, in this manner.
The cautery iron used for this single cauterization should be circular in shape, in this manner.
The stomach can also be cauterized by pricking, for those who are averse to this cauterization.  Mark the stomach with dots, the size you desire, using ink.  Then cauterize them using a pointed cautery iron.  Then treat it with the previously described treatment until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On cauterizing the cold liver.
If a painful condition arises in the liver due to coldness and dampness, or a hot wind that has caused it to deviate excessively from its natural temperament, and the treatment described in the classification proved ineffective. Then the patient should lie on their back, and three cauteries, of this exact shape and size, should be marked with ink on the liver, below the collarbones, where the human forearm ends.
Each cautery should be the thickness of a finger, and the cautery should be applied straight along the body, without pressing the iron with your hand.
Let the amount burned of the skin's thickness be about half, no more.  The patient should stand on their feet, or if unable, lie with legs extended and arms raised.  This is the shape of the cautery iron.
You may perform these cauteries with a knife-shaped cautery iron, if you possess skill and dexterity in the craft, and take care not to overdo it, lest you burn all the skin, thus burning the abdomen and reaching the intestines.  The skin there is thin; be aware of this.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
Treating a liver tumor with cautery.
If an abscess appears in the liver, and you wish to know whether the tumor is in the liver's flesh or its membrane, if it is in the liver's flesh, the patient will experience heaviness and pain, but not severe pain.
And if it is in the liver's membrane, there will be severe pain with intensity, and if you find that the physicians have exhausted all treatment options, then the patient should lie on their back, and you should mark the swollen area with ink.
Then, heat the cautery in the fire; it is a cautery resembling a file, and this is its form. And cauterize with a single cauterization, until the entire skin is burned and the cauterization reaches the membrane, so that all the matter is expelled, then treat it with abscess treatment until it heals.
This type of cauterization should only be performed by someone who has extensive experience in medicine and has treated such diseases repeatedly through experience. Only then should one undertake such a procedure; to leave it to someone else is preferable.
The twenty-ninth chapter.
On cauterizing the fistula.
The ancients recommended cauterizing cold fistulas with the roots of the zarawand plant in this way: take a single, longest, dried zarawand root, as thick as a finger, then dip it in oil and hold it in the fire. Then cauterize once between the collarbone and neck, and two small cauterizations slightly off the veins, slanted towards the area under the chin, and two large cauterizations above the breasts, between the third and fourth ribs.
And two more cauterizations between the fifth and sixth ribs, slightly slanted backward, another cauterization in the middle of the chest, and another above the stomach, and three cauterizations from behind, one between the shoulders and two on the sides of the lower back, below the cauterization between the shoulder blades.
One should not probe deeply with the cautery, but it should be superficial, on the surface of the skin.  Some of the ancients reported that some people used a heated iron rod, similar to a spatula, heating it and inserting it between the ribs until it reached the tumor itself.
The pus will discharge, as we mentioned in the case of a liver tumor, and in this condition, cauterization may lead to the patient's death immediately, or a fistula will develop that cannot be healed.
Chapter Thirty.
On cauterizing the spleen.
If you have treated spleen disease with the therapies described in the classification and your treatment is unsuccessful, cauterization is possible in three ways, each correct: one is to cauterize the spleen three or four times, in a row along its length.
In the shape of the liver cauterizations previously described, and there should be a space between each cauterization the thickness of a finger or a little more. The cauterization implement should have the same properties as that described for liver cauterization. Do not dig deep with the cauterization tool. The patient should be lying on their back.
The other way is at the elbow; lift the skin opposite the spleen where the patient's left elbow ends. Lift the skin across the width of the body so that the cauterizations are along the body.
Then, very carefully insert the cautery probes until they penetrate the skin from the opposite side. Then, remove the cauterizing tool, and the cauterizations will be four.  If desired, use the cautery tool with three probes.
Then treat the cauterization site after it has been allowed to discharge pus for several days; this is more effective than any of the previous treatments.
Chapter Thirty-one.
On cauterizing hydrops.
Cauterization is only beneficial in ascites, specifically. If, after treating the patient with the types of treatments we mentioned in the classification, your treatment proves ineffective, you should cauterize four times around the navel and once on the stomach.
Another cauterization on the liver and one on the spleen, and two behind his back, one opposite his chest and the other opposite his stomach. The depth of the cauterization should be close to the thickness of the skin. Then leave the cauterization open to discharge pus for a long time.
Do not neglect the patient with aftercare following the cauterization, as necessary for the affected areas to heal quickly, ensuring the proper recovery of the patient. The shape of the cautery iron used for the back is pointed, as previously described, and that used for the back is olive-shaped.
Chapter Thirty-two.
On cauterizing the feet and legs.
If the feet swell and fill with yellow water in dropsy, then it is necessary to cauterize the back of the foot at the point between the little finger and the ring finger.
Hold your hand with the cautery iron upright and do not bend it. Then raise your hand and do not lower it again, for the yellow water will drain, and the cautery iron should be of this shape.
Then cauterize the legs twice on each leg; the cauterization should be done with the sharp tip of the cautery iron, along the length of the leg, one below the knee, and another below that, towards the middle of the leg, and on each thigh cauterize twice.
Leave the cauterization open without treatment for a long time to allow the water to drain, then treat it with the rest of the methods we mentioned.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On the cauterization of diarrhea.

Apply one or two large cauterizations to the cotton above the coccyx.

Chapter Thirty-Four.

And cauterize under the navel with a width of two fingers. And another on the spleen with a nail-shaped cautery. Leave the cauterization open for a time, then treat him until he is healed, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-five.
On cauterizing warts.
After cutting it, if the wart is cut, heat the cautery, which resembles a stylus, then insert it hot into the same cut wart and press your hand firmly until the cautery reaches the mouth of the vein from which blood flows. Do this once or twice.

Chapter Thirty-six.
On cauterizing the fistula that occurs in the perineum and its surrounding areas.
If the patient does not respond to the incision and the procedure we described in its place, and avoids it, he may heal with cauterization. If a fistula develops in someone and has existed for a long time, pus and corrupted moisture flow from it, the first thing you should do is measure it with a thin probe.
Then, determine its depth with the probe. Then, heat the cautery iron, which resembles a needle, and insert it hot into the same fistula, aligning it with the depth of the fistula and the amount the probe went in. Repeat the cauterization until all the corrupted material is burned, once, twice, or three times as needed.
And be careful not to burn a nerve, if there is one, or a large vein, if the fistula opens into the bladder or the intestine.
And be careful of all these locations.  You should only do this if the fistula is in a fleshy area and you judge that it is not penetrating. Then treat the area until it heals. If the area is sealed and the materials have stopped flowing from it and remains so for a while, know that it has healed completely.
If the discharge does not stop, know that it is penetrating or that there is corrupted bone within its depth, or something similar, which will be discussed in its place.

Chapter Forty.
On cauterizing a dislocated hip.
False humors may gather in the socket of the hip, causing dislocation.  A sign is that one leg will be longer than the other when compared, and you will find an empty space at the site of the dislocation.  It is necessary to cauterize the afflicted area, around the socket of the hip itself, with a roughly circular cauterization.
After applying the cautery around the socket, the socket should lie in the center of the circle.  The cautery shown in the example of cauterizing the stomach should be used. If this cautery is unavailable, then use three cauterizations with a large olive-shaped cautery.

Chapter Forty-One.
In cauterizing sciatica.
If pain occurs in the hip joint due to cold and dampness, and the treatment mentioned in the classification proves ineffective after a period of time, then the patient should be relieved of thick humors through the use of rotten seeds or prickly pear seeds, or similar remedies.
Then cauterize.  Cauterization has two forms: either with burning medicines, or with fire.  Cauterization with fire has various methods, one of which is to cauterize the hip joint itself with three triangular cauterizations in this manner.
This will produce a beneficial depth, with the interval between each cauterization equal to the thickness of a finger.  The cauterizing agent should be olive-colored.  One may cauterize in the center, on the head of the hip itself, for a total of four cauterizations.  Or, if desired, cauterize with a circular motion, as previously described in treating hip dislocation, with a single cauterization.
The circle should encompass the entire hip, burning the full thickness of the skin. This is the method of cauterization for sciatica if the pain is localized to the hip itself, and does not extend to the thigh and leg.
If the pain extends to the thigh and leg, cauterize twice on the thigh, at the location the patient indicates as painful.  Cauterize once above the heel, about four fingers to the outer side.  Use a scalpel-like cauterizing agent, and make the depth of the cauterization only the thickness of the skin.
If the patient indicates that the pain extends towards the toes, cauterize at the indicated location with the pinpoint cautery three, four, or more times, if necessary.  If the pain is below the knee, towards the leg, cauterize once with a scalpel-like cautery.
In all your cauterizations, be careful not to burn a major nerve or artery, lest you cause the patient a serious or lasting injury.
I have observed cases of cauterization above the heel, where excessive cauterization caused gangrene of the leg, extending to the foot and causing its complete necrosis, ruining the entire leg, and followed by diarrhea and death. If the pain is in both areas, cauterize them in exactly the same manner.
Some knowledgeable scholars have described a specific cauterization method for the hip.  A cup-like object is made of iron, with a diameter of half a span.  Its opening is the thickness of a date pit, or slightly smaller.  Inside this cup are another cup, and a third cup.
There is a space of about the width of a thumb's breadth between each cup, and the cups are open on both sides. Their height is approximately one or two spans.  A handle of iron is firmly attached to the cups.  This is its description.
Then, it is heated in the fire until it glows red and sparks, and then placed on the affected hip, with the patient resting on the healthy side.  Three circular cauterizations are made in one instance.  Then, it is left for three days, and bandaged with ghee, leaving the wound open for several days.
Then it is treated with ointment until healed. The author of this book states that this type of cauterization is rarely used due to its unpleasantness and horrific appearance, and few can tolerate it. However, it is a good cauterization for those who can endure it and who suffer from the affected area.
As for cauterization with burning medicines, it is done by creating two cups resembling the rings where the gate-bolt of a door goes, made of copper or iron.
Their height will be about two spans, or thereabouts. The distance between them will be about the thickness of a finger.  The bottoms and tops will be open.  They are fastened together in this manner.
Then, place them on the affected hip of the patient lying on his/her healthy side. Hold your hand gently. Then pour a little hot, sharp water between the circles (cups).  Hold it for a while, like an hourglass. The patient endures the burning and stinging, which is like fire.
Until the stinging subsides. Then remove the instrument, and wash the entire hip with fresh water.  Leave it for three days.  Apply ghee until the blackness of the burning disappears.  Leave it for a few days, allowing pus to drain, as it is most beneficial.
Then treat it with ointments until it heals. If the pain extends to the thigh or leg, make a mold for him/her, holding the water in the same way as done for the hip.  This is the description of sharp water, and it has been proven in the treatise on the improvement of medicines.
Take one part each of lye and quicklime. Grind them and place them in a new pot with a single small hole at the bottom, just large enough for the pestle to enter. Place another glazed pot under the bottom of the first pot.
Pour enough fresh water over the lye and lime to cover them with a finger's breadth after you have thoroughly crushed them with your hand.  Let the pot sit until the sharp water descends to the bottom glazed pot.  Collect all the water and pour it onto fresh lime and lye, also ground.
It will then be extremely sharp, effective in many medical procedures and cauterizing other parts of the body, for it acts like fire itself.  Some medicines, such as thafsīya, balaذر honey, and lime mixed with soap, can also cauterize the hip when ground.
Galen recounted a story of an ancient physician's treatment for hip pain and sciatica, greatly praising it.  He claimed that no other treatment is needed and that it heals in one application.
Even, it is said, that a patient could be carried into the bath and emerge healed.  This is achieved by taking fresh green shiṭraj, or if no green is available, fresh dried shiṭraj, grinding it with some fat, and applying it to the hip where the pain is, or to the leg or thigh.
Apply the compress and leave it on for three hours, or as long as the patient feels the burning subside.  Then, have him enter the bath.  When his body is moist, place him in a basin.  The pain will disappear and he will be healed, God willing. If not, reapply the compress after ten days.
He will be healed, God willing.  And know that no one should use these cauterizing remedies until the body is emptied.  Dioscorides mentioned that goat's dung, if used for cauterizing sciatica, is beneficial.
The cauterization should be performed as follows: Take wool, soak it in old oil, and place it on the deep area between the thumb and the elbow, closer to the elbow.  Then take dry goat's dung.
Heat it in the fire until it becomes glowing embers, then place it on the wool and let it cool. Continue doing this until the sensation extends from the middle of the forearm to the hip, and the pain subsides, God willing. This type of cauterization is called cauterization with goat dung.
The Forty-second Chapter.
On cauterizing back pain.
Pain in the back may arise from various causes, such as falls, blows, excessive vomiting, and the like.  It results from the effusion of cold, moist matter.  Cauterization is applied only in this type of case.
After the patient has vomited, and after applying remedies like rotten herbs, the back where the pain is should be cauterized in three rows, each the width of a table, after marking the area with ink.  In each row, five cauterizations or more should be applied, as determined by the patient's tolerance and strength.
The cauterization should be done with a point cautery, or if desired, with a medium-sized nail cautery in three or four applications, in this manner.
Chapter Forty-Three.
On cauterizing the beginning of a swelling.
This ailment often affects young children.  A sign of its early stage in a child is shortness of breath when standing or moving, and at the end of the spinal column, a prominent bead appears, protruding above the other beads.
If you see this and wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery, in this manner. Apply the cautery to each side of the vertebra evenly.  If desired, cauterize around the vertebra with a pinpoint cautery, in two or three rows.  Let the points be close together.  Then treat the area until healed, as described.
Take care to use cautery on swellings caused by nerve spasms.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On cauterizing gout and joint pains.
If joint pains are caused by cold dampness flowing to any part of the body, and if the pains occur in the legs, physicians customarily call it gout, specifically.
If the cold gout, the cause, is treated with the methods we mentioned in the classification, and the pain does not subside, then cauterization will relieve it. This involves applying numerous cauterizations around the joint of the legs after inducing vomiting. The cauterizing instrument should be olive-oil based and of medium heat, in this manner.
And if necessary, apply dots on the leg with the pointed cauterizing instrument. If the pain ascends to the knees or to other joints, which frequently occurs, cauterize each knee with three or four cauterizations on each side using the same olive-oil based cauterizing instrument.
If more cauterization is needed, apply it, but do not dig your instrument deep into the skin; cauterize only to the thickness of the skin. If the pain ascends to the hips or back, use the cauterization method described in its appropriate section.
If the pain is only in the hands, apply dots around the wrists, in a row on both sides.  If the pain remains in the fingers, apply a dot to each knuckle and on the palm of the hand. If, after a few days, the pain ascends to the elbows or shoulders,
Cauterize them [the elbows or shoulders] on both sides.  The patient should not neglect good treatment and taking medication.  If he improves his diet and expels phlegm, he will recover with this cauterization, God willing.
Chapter Forty-five.
On cauterizing hernias.
If a hernia appears in the groin, and some intestines and fat have descended to the testicle, and this is a recent occurrence, the patient should be instructed to abstain from eating that day and to use laxatives to expel stool from his body.
Then, he should lie on his back before you, and you should instruct him to restrain himself until the fat or intestines emerge.  Then, you should push them back with your finger, and mark the area under the hernia on the pubic bone with ink, a mark resembling a semicircle with its ends pointing upwards towards the body.
Then, heat a cautery, which is pictured like this.
Until it glows white, emitting sparks.  Then, return the intestines or fat to the abdomen.  Then, have an attendant place their hand on the area so that the intestines do not protrude again. The patient's legs should be spread apart, and a cushion placed underneath them, with another attendant holding his legs.
And another attendant should hold his hands on his chest.  Then, bring the cautery down on the same mark.  Your hand should hold the cautery upright until it reaches the bone, or repeat the process if it did not reach the bone on the first attempt.
And carefully restrain the intestines from protruding while cauterizing, lest you burn the patient, causing either death or a serious illness.  Know that if the cauterization does not reach the bone, your work will not succeed.  The cautery should be appropriate in size for children and adults.
Then, treat the cauterized area with ghee after three days to remove the harshness of the burn, then treat it with other ointments until it heals.  Keep the patient lying on their back for forty days until the wound closes.
His food during the treatment should be such as to soften his bowels so that the intestines do not protrude during bowel movements.  Then, if he wishes to stand after forty days, a tight bandage should be used and kept on for another forty days.  Reduce exertion, overeating, drinking, and loud shouting.
If he follows this treatment, he will be completely healed, God willing.  I will describe the treatment of hernias by incision in its appropriate section, God willing.  Regarding a hernia that occurs in the rest of the abdomen, if you wish to prevent its growth, cauterize a circular area corresponding to the size of the hernia. Let the skin burned be about a third of its thickness, then treat it as described.  It will not grow, God willing.  Some early physicians mentioned cauterizing the hernia with a triangular cautery in this manner.
After marking the hernia with ink, and making a line across the upper part of the hernia and another line to the bottom, and placing your hand in the middle with a single, nail-like cauterization, the initial cauterization is easier and better, God willing.
Chapter Forty-six.
On cauterizing the fistula.
If, in some limb, a fall or blow causes pain that persists for a long time, and medication is ineffective, then it is necessary to cauterize with a pinpoint cautery.  The cauterization of each limb should be adjusted according to its size, smallness, weakness, and strength.
The pain and fistula will be subdued by the cauterization; if the initial cauterization heals him, then it is well and good.  If not, repeat the cauterization, for it is the nature of these ailments to spread from the affected limb to its surroundings.  Thus, cauterization must be followed up until the patient is healed.
Chapter Forty-seven.
On the cauterization of leprosy.
Lepers may benefit greatly from cauterization, especially those whose leprosy originates from the putrefaction of phlegm and black bile.
If you wish to cauterize him, observe. If the leprosy is in its initial stages and you have treated it as described in the section on classification, without deterioration or cessation, and you fear the corruption might spread to the patient's entire constitution, cauterize his head five times. One in the customary middle of the head, the second below it toward the forehead, at the end of the hair; two on the temples, and one at the back of the neck, at the nape; exert your cautery slightly, so that it affects the bone to a slight degree.
Scales will peel off, facilitating the expulsion of thick vapors from it; and cauterize the spleen also, as previously stated.  However, if the leprosy has persisted and become clearly evident on the patient, it is necessary to cauterize with the described methods on the head. One cauterization on the tip of the nose, two on each cheek, two on the cervical vertebrae, six on the thoracic vertebrae, one large one on the coccyx at the base of the tail, another above it on the same plane, and two on each thigh, one on each. Two on the knees, one on each knee, two on the shoulders, two on the elbows, two on the ribs of the chest, and cauterize each joint of his fingers and toes; and each heel of his feet and each wrist of his hands.
Avoid burning the nerves behind the heels.  A cauterization may be applied to the pubic bone, another to the stomach opening, and another to the liver.  Know that the more cauterizations you apply, the more beneficial and effective they will be.
Know that the patient experiences less pain from the cauterizations than a healthy person, because their body is numb.  The cauterization size—large or small—should be determined by the organs and joints, as described previously in the characteristics of the instruments.
Then treat the cauterization with fine powdered carrageen, honey, and other treatments until he is healed, God willing.
Chapter Forty-eight.
Concerning cauterization for numbness: If a part of the body has been numb and treated with medications, ointments, and dressings, but still has not healed, cauterize the numb part with cauterizations corresponding to the size of the affected bone.  Make your cauterization slightly deeper in the skin's thickness.
Then treat it with ointment until it heals.  Cauterization may also be used for some numbness that occurs in the hand or foot, arising from the spinal vertebrae at the point where the nerve that moves the affected limb emerges; this will alleviate the numbness.
Only those who are knowledgeable in the anatomy of the limbs and the origins of the nerves that move the body should undertake this.
Chapter Forty-nine.
On cauterizing leprosy.
If leprosy persists despite all medical remedies, cauterize it with a moderate depth, corresponding to the thickness of the skin, so that the whiteness disappears and the color changes.
Then treat it with lentil flour, rose ointment,  horsetail leaves, and dove's blood or hawk's blood, one part of each. Mix them all and apply to a cloth, and bind it to the affected area until it heals.
Chapter Fifty.
On cauterizing cancer.
If the cancer is in its early stages and you wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular cautery around it, as it circles. Some physicians have suggested a severe cauterization in the center, but I do not advise this, as I anticipate ulceration, having witnessed it on several occasions.
So the correct approach is to cauterize around it in a circle, as we have said, or with numerous small cauterizations.
Chapter Fifty-one.
On cauterizing the fistula.
If a fistula has developed and is slow to mature, either due to the agent causing it, the patient's age (if the patient is an elderly person with low blood), or time, and you wish to hasten its maturation, cauterize around it with many small, dotted cauterizations.
Then leave it; this will hasten maturation. If you wish to cover it with a cauterization, heat the cautery that has this form.
Place it in the center of the fistula until it penetrates the skin.  Let the cauterization be positioned downwards so that the pus drains easily. Then treat it as appropriate until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-Two.
On cauterizing ulcers.
Ulcers are a corruption that spreads within the tissue, consuming it like fire consumes dry wood. If you see an ulcer in a location suitable for cauterization with fire, heat numerous small and large cautery irons, according to what is suitable for the location of the ulcer.
Then cauterize it from all sides until all the corruption is eradicated and nothing remains. Then leave it for three days and apply powdered sulfur mixed with oil to the cauterized areas, to remove all the scab and corruption.
Then treat it with healing ointments. If after three days you see that the flesh grows soundly without any corruption, then leave it alone. Otherwise, repeat the cauterization on any remaining corrupted areas. Ulcers can also be treated with strong medicine.
For it serves the purpose of fire, offering quicker benefit.  The treatment of ulcers with strong medicine was mentioned in the division; take it from there when needed.
Chapter Fifty-three.
On cauterizing inverted and non-inverted nails.
This affliction, a rough, painful condition, often occurs on the soles of the feet.  Cauterization is performed in two ways: either by fire or by strong water.
As for cauterization by fire, heat a hollowed-out iron implement, resembling an eagle's beak, the size of the nail's circumference, with thin edges. Then, apply it, hot, to the nail.  Rotate the implement around the nail until it penetrates to the nail's depth.
Leave it for three days to form pus.  Then, apply a poultice of crushed wild barley mixed with salt, and leave it on overnight.  This will remove the nail from its roots.  Then, treat the wound with a wound-healing ointment until it heals; this is for non-inverted nails.
It often occurs on the surface of the body, especially the hands and feet.  Therefore, take a tube of copper, iron, or eagle's beak and apply it to the nail or wart. Then pour a small amount of strong water into the tube.
Hold the tube and gently rotate it with your hand so that the edges of the tube affect the nail's base, and the strong water penetrates to the nail's base.  The patient should endure the stinging of the strong water for a while.
Then leave it, as the nail will detach from its roots. Do this to each one until you have dealt with all of them on the body.  Then treat the areas, after removal, with ointments that promote healing of the flesh.
Chapter Forty-five.
On the treatment of scabies.
If someone has scabies caused by cold in the nerve, or fever, or other conditions, you should cauterize four or five times on the back, between each cauterization on the back, and one on the chest. And cauterize the abdomen with an olive oil cautery, as this will relieve the scabies and hasten the resolution of the cold disease.
Chapter Fifty-five.
On cauterizing a wound occurring on the body.
Ugly boils may arise on the body due to thick, corrupt cold materials.  Upon their first appearance, a gentle cauterization should be applied to the head of each boil with a burning stick, the tip of which has been ignited with fire or with the long root of the zarawand, or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And boils, in their initial eruption, should be cauterized in this manner.  The active principle will not increase, and the patient will recover, but this must follow bloodletting.
Chapter Fifty-six.
On cauterizing bleeding occurring from a severed artery.
Bleeding frequently occurs from a severed artery in a wound from the outside, or when incising a tumor or cauterizing a limb, or similar situations, and it is difficult to stop.
If this happens to someone, quickly place your index finger over the artery's opening and press firmly until the blood is contained beneath your finger and nothing escapes.  Then, take several small and large olive-oil cauteries and heat them in the fire until extremely hot.
Then, take one, either small or large, depending on the wound and the location where the artery has ruptured, and apply the cauterium to the same vein after quickly removing your finger and holding the cauterium until the bleeding stops.
If the bleeding surges again when you remove your finger from the artery's opening and the cauterium has cooled, quickly take another cauterium from the heated ones and continue doing this one after another until the bleeding stops.
And be cautious not to burn a nerve, lest you cause further injury to the patient.  Know that if an artery is bleeding, it cannot be cut, especially if it is large, except in one of four ways: either by cauterization, as we have said, or by amputation if it has not already been severed. If it is severed, its ends will contract and the bleeding will stop, or it can be tied tightly with threads, or medications that will stop the bleeding and tightly constrict it can be applied.
As for those who attempt to stop the bleeding by tying it with a bandage, or by constricting it with cloth, or applying burning objects and similar methods, the bleeding will not stop thereby except rarely.  If someone in such a situation is not attended to by a physician or does not have medicine, then let them quickly place the index finger over the opening of the wound as we described.
He should press it firmly until the blood is contained and flows out above the wound and on the artery and finger.  The finger should not be removed from the wound with repeatedly applied very cold water until the blood coagulates and thickens and stops flowing.  During this time, consider what cauterization or medication is needed.
The Second Chapter.
Concerning incisions, lancinations, phlebotomy, and other surgical procedures.
In the previous chapter, we discussed every disease in which cauterization with fire and burning medicaments is appropriate, along with its causes, reasons, and tools, and the various types of cauterization procedures, presenting this information as chapters from the head to the foot.
I will follow the same method in this chapter, to make the material easier for the student to grasp.
Before beginning, you must know, my children, that this chapter contains greater uncertainty than the first chapter on cauterization.
Therefore, caution must be greater here, as actions in this chapter often result in excessive blood loss, the lifeblood, when opening a vein or making an incision on a tumor, lancing an abscess, treating a surgical wound, removing an arrow, making an incision on a stone, or similar matters.  All these are accompanied by uncertainty and fear, and death often results.
I advise you to avoid doubtful actions.  Those in this profession will encounter various types of sick people, some of whom are weary of their illness and have little fear of death.
Because of the severity of their illness and the prolonged suffering, some show signs of impending death; others offer you their wealth in the hope of recovery, yet their illness is fatal. You should not treat those whose condition is such.
Let your caution be greater than your desire and eagerness.  Do not undertake any of these actions without certain knowledge of a favorable outcome.  Use knowledge and warnings about the consequences of health or sickness in all treatment of your patients.
This will aid you in acquiring praise, glory, remembrance, and thanks. May God guide you, my children, with wisdom, and deprive you not of truth and success.  Indeed, this is in His power, there is no god but Him.
I have arranged this chapter into sections, as was done in the chapter on cautery, from the crown to the foot, so that you may find what you seek more easily, God willing.
Chapter One.
On the treatment of water that collects in the heads of children.
This ailment often affects infants at birth if the midwife applies pressure to the infant's head without gentleness. It may also arise from a hidden cause that is unknown.
I have not seen this ailment in anyone other than infants. All those I have seen who had it died quickly. Therefore, I have decided to abandon treatment for it. I have observed an infant whose head filled with water, and the head grew larger each day. Until the infant could no longer sit up due to the enlargement of the head, and the moisture increased until death occurred.
This moisture either collects between the skin and bone, or under the bone on the peritoneum. If the moisture is between the skin and bone and the swelling is small, one should make a single horizontal incision in the middle of the head, about two spans long, to allow the moisture to drain.  This is a depiction of the lancet.
If the moisture is greater and the swelling larger, make two intersecting incisions in this manner.
If the moisture is under the bone, and the sign is that you see the sutures of the head open on all sides, and the water recedes when pressed by your hand inwards (this should be clear to you), then three incisions should be made in the middle of the head in this manner.
After the incision, all the moisture will come out. Then, the incisions are closed with bandages and splints.  Afterwards, the wound is treated with poultices, and oil, until the fifth day. Then, the bandage is removed, and the wound is treated with lint and ointments.  The head should not be bound tightly, and the patient should be fed with all dry foods that are low in moisture.
Until the limb strengthens and heals. Another method of incision is to look where the bone of the swelling and the accumulation of water appears, as it may be more prevalent at the back of the head, or the front, or to the right, or to the left.
Thus, with the incision, aim for the location where the swelling and accumulation of water appear.  Make the incision as best you can, and be careful not to cut an artery, causing bleeding, which could lead to the patient's death from such bleeding while also evacuating the moisture.
The Second Chapter.
Severing the two arteries behind the ears, known as the "hissings.
When someone suffers from a severe affliction of the eyes or chest, and the remedies of medicine prove ineffective, then the most effective treatment is the severing of these two arteries.
If you wish to sever them, you should first shave the patient's head with a razor, then rub the area with a rough cloth to reveal the artery.  Then, tighten the patient's neck with the aid of the patient's own clothing.  Then, look for where the pulse beats and the two locations, the two recessed areas behind the ears.  These are rarely hidden, except in some people.
Then, mark them with ink. Then, sever them with a dissecting scalpel, cutting them through to the bone.  This will be across the width of the head.  Or, if you wish, insert the scalpel beneath the artery and cut it upwards.  The length of the cut should be about two clenched fingers.
Then, when the artery is severed, the blood will flow out in a pulsating, forward, continuous stream.  If the artery is not visible to the touch, then you should estimate from the ear by about three fingers' width, mark it with ink, and cut through to the bone.  And what is necessary...
About six ounces of blood will be released immediately, more or less, depending on the strength of the patient and the fullness of their arteries. Then, look at the wound. If any part of the peritoneum remains on the bone, cut it off.
So that a hot swelling does not arise. Then, bind the wound with a linen thread, and treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter Three.
Of the arteries in the temples.
If a person suffers from chronic headache or acute attacks caused by sharp humors and heat in the muscles of the temples, or from chronic severe headaches, and similar conditions, and if treatment with various medical remedies has been unsuccessful, we have experimented with severing the arteries of the temples, or cauterizing them, as we have described.
The procedure for severing them is to shave the patient's hair on the temples, and to locate the superficial artery in the temple.  It can be identified by its pulse, and it rarely goes unseen, except perhaps in exceptional cases.
Or, if the artery is hidden by intense cold, the patient should tighten his neck with his garment, then you should rub the area with a cloth, or apply warm water until the artery becomes clearly visible. Then take the lancet, which has this image. Then, gently dissect the skin with the lancet until you reach the artery.  Then insert a hook into it and pull it upward to bring it out from the skin and free it from the membranes beneath, on all sides. If the artery is thin, twist it with the hook's end. Then cut off a portion so that its ends are separated and constricted, and no bleeding occurs.  If it is not severed and cut, the blood will not be separated at all.  Then drain between six and three ounces of blood.
If the artery is large, it should be tied in two places with a strong doubled thread, whether of silk or of bowstrings, so that putrefaction does not quickly reach it before the wound heals, causing bleeding.
Then cut the excess between the ligatures, do this either at that moment or after some time.  If you wish, cauterize the area down to the bone with a small, knife-like cauterizing iron until the ends are severed, and this will do the same work, or even better, as we said.
