However, if the patient has a fever or is of a hot temperament, cauterization will aid in eliminating the humidity, making it more beneficial.  After dissecting the arteries, the area should be packed with old cotton and covered with firm bandages.  After the dressing is removed, treat with dry wound healing medicines and compresses until healed, God willing.
If, during your work, bleeding from the artery occurs, immediately cut it off, either by cauterization or by filling the area with alum and pressing firmly until the blood stops. If nothing of that is available, use your finger to stop the blood.
Apply very cold water to the area to reduce the inflammation and tighten it as appropriate.  A simpler and easier method than dissecting the artery is to cauterize the blood vessel with this double-bladed cauterizing iron after marking the two locations with ink.
Apply the cauterization very intensely until it reaches the bone and severs the blood vessel in two places, so that the distance between the ends is increased.  It will not heal with this type of cauterization.  This is an illustration of the cautery instrument.
The instrument has two sharp blades, similar to small knives, but much less sharp than a knife. If they were as sharp as a knife, the cold would reach them quickly, and the flesh would not sever quickly.  Therefore, if they have some thickness, the heat of the fire will be retained.
It quickly cuts through the flesh.  This method is the best, easiest, and simplest, with the distance between the blades being about the thickness of a finger.
Chapter Four.
For the treatment of persistent, hot, watery discharge from the eyes.
If the discharge is persistent, coming from the veins on the skull's exterior, and no medicinal treatment is effective, and you see the patient's face has become red. And if he feels a crawling sensation in his forehead like ants crawling, and his eyes are sunken and moist, their edges eroded, and their eyelids glued together from the intensity of the tears, the earliest treatments resorted to this method, calling it "treatment with the sword."  It involves ordering the patient to shave the hair from his forehead.
Then three parallel incisions are made on the forehead, each about two fingers long, aligned with the length of the nose in the center of the forehead, the second slightly distant from the movement of the muscles in the temple, and the third on the opposite side.
And be careful not to cut the arteries on the sides, and keep your hand away from touching the jaws.  After each incision, allow a space of three fingers closed together.  Have ready sponges or many dry cloths to absorb the blood.
Then insert the sharp-ended scalpel, which is of this form. From the incision next to the temple to the middle incision, use it to peel away all the skin between the incisions, along with the periosteum on the bone.  Then do the same from the middle incision to the third incision.  Then remove this scalpel. And insert into the first incision another instrument, called a scalpel, sharp on one side and smooth and blunt on the other. This is its image.
Turn its sharp side towards the flesh attached to the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone.  Push it until it reaches the middle incision, and cut all the vessels that descend from the head to the eyes, without reaching the surface of the skin.
Then do this from the middle incision to the other incision.  After a moderate amount of blood has flowed, compress the areas of solidified blood. Then, in each incision, place a cotton wick, and put on it a compress soaked in a mixture of medicated liquid and oil, or vinegar and oil.
So that a hot swelling does not occur. On the third day, dissolve the ligaments.  Use abundant tepid water compresses, then treat with basil ointment mixed with rose oil and other remedies for wounds until healed, God willing.
Chapter Five.
On treating tears and discharge from the eyes from within the head.
When someone has a severe, persistent, and copious discharge, and the eyes are sunken and small, and their sight is weakened, and the eyelids are ulcerated, and the eyelashes are falling out. And there is intense, painful pain deep within the head, and repeated sneezing.  Know, from these symptoms, that these materials and discharges come from numerous deep veins. The best treatment is this: order the afflicted to shave their forehead, then make a single incision in the middle of the forehead.
And there is intense, painful pain deep within the head, and repeated sneezing.  Know, from these symptoms, that these materials and discharges come from numerous deep veins. The best treatment is this: order the afflicted to shave their forehead, then make a single incision in the middle of the forehead. Or raise it slightly transversely, beginning the incision from the left temple to the right temple.  The incision should be to the bone, and keep your hand away from the mobile masseter muscles when chewing.  Until the bone is exposed and all blood is wiped away with a sponge.
Separate the edges of the incision with used cotton or flax thread. Then, tie it together from above with bandages, and soak the bandages with medication and oil to prevent a hot swelling.  Once you have resolved it, and see that the hot swelling has subsided, you should rub the bone until flesh begins to grow there.
Then treat it with a desiccated preparation that promotes flesh growth, such as taking two parts wheat flour and four parts of calphonium, preparing a salve from them, and using it for flesh growth in wounds of this type.
Chapter Six.
For treating what falls into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a metallic or semi-metallic stone, like iron or glass; or a vegetable seed, such as chickpeas, nuts, and similar things; or a liquid, like water and vinegar, and similar substances.
Or, if an animal, such as a pebble or a similar small stone, falls into the ear and does not grow in the ear, face the ear towards the sun.  If you see the stone, drop a little violet or cerucean oil into it, then try to remove it by moving the head or inducing a sneeze with a censer. And close the nostrils when the sneeze comes, after placing a bandage of cloth or wool around the ear and stretching the ear upwards.  Often, this treatment will remove it.  If it does not, try removing it with gentle cotton wool, of which this is a picture.
If it comes out with the cotton wool, otherwise try to remove it with a gentle, slightly curved, blunt hook. If it doesn't come out with that, then make a copper tube and insert the end of the tube into the ear canal.
Seal the area around the tube with softened pitch mixed with oil, so that the air has no route other than through the tube, then pull it out with a strong pull.  It often comes out this way.  If it doesn't come out by the methods described, then take a small amount of Nabataean gum or prepared gum with which birds are caught and place it on the end of the rod.
After wrapping it tightly with cotton, gently insert it into the ear canal after the ear is dry. If it doesn't come out with all the methods described, then proceed to incision before the hot swelling or hardening occurs.
The method of incision is to first bleed the patient from the temples or forehead, to the extent of their strength.  Then, place the patient before you, turn their ear upward, and make a small incision at the base of the ear, near the cartilage, at its lowest point.
The incision should be crescent-shaped until you reach the stone, then remove it with whatever instruments you can. Then quickly stitch the incision and treat it until it heals.
If the object lodged in the ear is one of the grains that grow and swell, attempt to remove it with the methods described. If that doesn't work, then take a thin, delicate lancet of this form.
Try to cut that type of fallen grain in the ear with it, but only do so if you are certain that the grain has moistened with earwax to make it into many small pieces, then remove them with a blunt hook, a soft cloth, or by suction.
As mentioned, it is easier to remove it.  As for water that has entered the ear, the patient should first use a bellows-like device for sneezing, and have filled their ear with some worn cotton while lying on their affected ear.  If it comes out that way.
Otherwise, take several small, smooth stones, warm them slightly by fire, and have the patient insert one into the ear hole.  The patient should then stand on one leg, on the same side as the affected ear, and strike another stone against the stone in the ear.
He should continue doing this with stone after stone until all the water is removed.  Water might also be removed by taking a piece of papyrus or feather, inserting one end into the ear, and heating the other end in a fire until most of it burns; then, a different piece of feather is used.
Repeat this process until all the water is removed, or use the tube as described for the stone removal.  Regarding removing an animal that has entered the ear, if it is small, such as a flea or similar, treat it as described in the classification.  As for...
If its body is large enough to be felt, try removing it with lint and forceps.  This is easier than anything that can occur in the ear.  As for removing worms that have developed in the ear, if you have treated it according to what we have mentioned in the chapter on the various types of infection but your treatment was not effective.
Then you should look into the ear in the sunlight. If you see any worms, remove them with lint or fine forceps. If you don't see anything, take a tube—this is its shape.
The tube is narrow at the bottom and wide at the top. Insert the thin end into the ear as far as the patient can tolerate, then suck strongly with it. Repeat this several times until all the worms are expelled. If that doesn't work, seal around the tube with wax, as I have described for removing stones.
If it is not removed with the methods mentioned, use the catheters I have described.  The ancients tested these for killing worms, and you will find this in the chapter on catheters.  You would apply ointments and medications to the ear with this instrument.  Here is its image.
As you see, you make it from silver or copper. Narrow at the bottom, with a small hole, and wide at the top.  If you prefer, the projectile within the tube can be made of strong copper.  Or, you may take a cord and tightly wrap cotton around its end.
Then place the ointment, or juice, or whatever medicine you want, into the tube while it is in the ear.  Then, insert the cord with cotton on top and gently squeeze with your hand until the ointment flows into the ear and produces a sensation.
Have the patient remain inside while applying it, and make sure the substance instilled into the ear has been slightly warmed in fire.  Be careful not to use something that is extremely cold or extremely hot, as the ear cannot tolerate that.
Chapter Seven.
Concerning the treatment of ear obstructions.
Some children may be born with their ear canals unperforated.
Some people may also experience an obstruction in the ear canal due to a wound or overgrown tissue. This obstruction may occur deep within the ear canal, and be invisible.
Sometimes it may present slightly outside the canal, and be visible.  Obstructions deep within the ear, not visible, are often difficult to cure.  Those visible should be placed in the sun. If the obstruction is visible, then open it with a gentle scalpel; this is its image/form.
Its tip should have a slightly limited protrusion, and the rest of the scalpel should be smooth on both sides so as not to injure the ear. If the obstruction is due to tissue growth within the ear canal, grasp it with a gentle forceps and cut it away with great care until all tissue is removed.
If the obstruction is deep within the ear, take a smooth, slender probe, warm it slightly in the fire, then insert it into the ear canal.  If you feel the obstruction, along with the patient's difficulty hearing, then remove it gently.
Care should be taken not to injure the ear drum.  Then, place a wick into the ear, sized to fit the canal, smeared with Egyptian ointment.  Do this for several days until the wound is healed.  Or, take a wick, soak it in water, and sprinkle powdered zaga on it.
Use this.  If you see that the ear has swollen with a hot tumor, then remove the wick and replace it with another one smeared with ointment made from rose oil, in order to reduce the hot swelling. Then treat it until it heals.
If bleeding occurs, dip a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear. Use the other treatments for stopping bleeding mentioned in the chapter on cauterization.
Chapter Eight.
On treating warts that appear on the eyelids.
These warts that appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry.  They should be grasped with a forceps or needle and cut away from their roots with a scalpel. Then, powdered alum should be applied to the area.
If bleeding occurs, cauterize it with a gentle, lentil-shaped cautery iron.  Cauterization is preferable after cutting.  It often returns if any part of the root remains.  If cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter Nine.
On treating cold affecting the eyelids.
A condition resembling severe cold often appears on the eyelids.
Therefore, it is called cold, being the accumulation of thick moisture in the upper and lower eyelids.  If the cold is prominent on the surface of the eyelid, moving in every direction, then it is easy to treat.  Cut it across.
Then carefully scrape it from every side until it comes off.  Then hang it by a hook and cut it.  If cutting it does not become possible until the eyelid is pierced by the cut, this will not harm the patient. If the cut is large, sew it up and treat it until it heals.
And if it is small, there is no problem.  The ointment will heal and join it. If the cold is sloping towards the inner surface of the eyelid, turn the eyelid inside out, hang the cold by a hook without needing a cut, and trim it from every side.
If the eyelid is pierced by the cut, this will not harm anything. Then wash the eye with salt water after cutting the cold and treat the area with something that heals it until the patient is well.
Chapter Ten.
On treating the chalazion that appears in the eyelid.
A chalazion is a swelling that forms in the layers of the upper eyelid.  It occurs most often in children.  It weighs down their eyes and causes them to have colds in the early morning hours, and they cannot look at sunlight. Because tears rush to them.
Therefore, you see them always leaning on their faces, and they sleep on them. Their eyelids beneath the eyebrows are moist, possibly swollen.  The chalazion is clearly visible.  If you press the area with your fingers, you feel the chalazion between them.  The method of treatment is for the patient to place their head in your lap.
Then take a linen cloth, prepare a wick from it, and make a circle from that wick to encompass the chalazion on all sides.  Then place it on top and press with your fingers on all sides to gather the chalazion into the center of the circle.
Then, with the lancet, cut through the center of that moisture with a transverse cut.  The cut should not be larger than that made in a venesection.  As for depth, you should cut through the entire skin until you reach the chalazion.
In most cases, the chalazion will protrude from the cut as a white piece of fat.  Pull it out with a cloth wrapped around your fingers, moving your hand back and forth until it heals.  Be careful not to enlarge the cut, lest the lancet injure the eye.
If the chalazion does not appear in the initial cut, you should gently enlarge the cut a little more until the chalazion emerges.  Then pull it out as we said.  Then, dip a cloth in vinegar and water, place it on the area, and bind it firmly.  Some people crush salt.
And place it in the opening of the cut to dissolve any remaining moisture.  Then treat it until it heals. If a hot swelling occurs at the site, treat it with cooling compresses until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On the types of eye dressings.
If extra eyelashes grow on the eyelid, outside the natural course, under the normal lashes and thicken, they harm the eye and cause various illnesses, such as constant tearing, eyelid relaxation, white spots, and thickening, until this becomes a cause of eye impairment.
And eye dressing has four aspects: either by cauterization with fire, or by a sharp medicine as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, or the dressing may be by cutting and stitching, or by using a reed, as I will describe.
You should place the patient's head in your lap, then turn the eyelid with your left hand. If it turns, then insert a needle with thread from underneath the eyelid, and pass the needle and thread from above, close to the eyelash itself, and pull the thread upward through the eyelid.
Then turn it with a spatula, and cut the excess hair from the inner eyelid with a lancet from the larger to the smaller incision, then pull the thread, and place a small pad of cotton or cloth under the eyelid, and then secure the eyelid with medication.
Like the shape of a rue leaf, but the shape should be according to the amount of elevation needed for the eyelid, as this may differ among people. Some may require a specific amount of eyelid excision in proportion to the eyelid's relaxation.
Some require a smaller incision.  All this is according to the relaxation of the eyelid.  Then, with the lancet, make incisions along the lines you marked, starting from the larger incision to the smaller, and make each incision near the natural lashes, about the thickness of a spatula.
Then, insert the hook into one corner of the skin, then peel the entire skin off.  Then sew the two edges together with a fine wool needle and thread, wiping away the blood.  Stick any remaining threads to the eyebrows with something adhesive.
You may do this, or you may not bother.  The stitches and threads remain for about three or four days, then treat it.  If you prefer, leave the wound unstitched and treat it with something that dries and contracts, for the eyelid will elevate when the wound closes and comes together. Stitching is better.
This method of incision, the ancients described it, but it requires effort for the patient. It is a good procedure, without danger. Another method of incision exists, also, in which you shape the eyelid like a rue leaf, as we described. Then raise the eyelid with three hooks, separated or grouped in this manner:
Then cut off any excess eyelid with a small scissors in this manner, cutting evenly.
If you are unable to hold the hooks in place, or if the procedure is not successful, take a needle with thread and insert it into the center of the shape. Insert another thread near the larger incision and a third near the smaller incision.  Hold the threads evenly with your fingers, then raise your hand steadily.
Raise the marked skin entirely as we described, then close the wound edges with stitches and treat it until it heals.  If a hot swelling appears after the incision or cut, reduce the swelling with a poultice or similar soothing ointment.
The lower eyelid may also have its edges turn inwards, so it is necessary to use the cutting, stitching, and cauterization methods we mentioned.  Cauterization with fire or sharp medicine is better and gentler on the patient than cutting and stitching. Cutting and stitching are more complex in these cases.
Cauterization with a reed is done in this manner: turn the eyelid over, make an incision on the inside as described, then make two thin reeds or pieces of wood the length of the eyelid, and less than the width of a scalpel.
Nibble the ends of each reed on both sides where the threads adhere, then gently bring them together over the remaining part of the eyelid.  Tightly fasten the reeds from both sides, and leave it for several days.  The tightened skin will die, turn black, and decay.
Until it falls off on its own.  If it takes too long, trim it with scissors, then treat it until healed.  Once it is joined, the eyelid will rise and the lashes will no longer irritate the eye.  This is the shape and size of the two reeds, the length and width should be precisely as shown.
Chapter Twelve.
For removing irritating hairs from the eye with a needle.
If it is one or two hairs, the procedure is to take a thin needle, thread it with a smooth, fine silk thread, tie the ends with a very gentle knot, and make the thread about a span long.
Then, thread another thin, shorter thread onto the tweezers.  Tie its ends if you wish.  The patient should place their head on your lap, close to a light source to clearly see the work, as it is a delicate procedure.  Then, using the needle on the tweezers, insert it into the root of the extra hair.
And gently pierce the eyelid, until it emerges from above the natural hair. Then pull the thread upward through the tweezers, and extract the hair(s) - one, two, or three at most - into the tweezers.
Then, pull with your hand until the hair comes out of the eyelid with the tweezers, along with the natural hair.  If pulling the tweezers doesn't remove the hair, pull the tweezers downward using the thread you'd previously attached, until the tweezers exit the upper opening.
Then, return the hair to the tweezers and pull them out. Repeat this until the hair is elevated in the upper opening and clearly visible.  At that point, remove the tweezers and thread, close the eye, and keep it closed for two or three days, until the hair is firmly in place and the flesh has grown over it.
If the hair is short, either add a longer strand of natural hair to elevate it with it, or allow it to grow longer and then lift it.
Chapter Thirteen.
On treating the stye that occurs in the upper eyelid of the eye.
This stye, which is called "arnybiyyah," can be either natural or accidental.  An accidental stye results from an inflammation of a wound, a cut, a burn, or similar conditions.
The procedure involves cutting the inflammation, separating its edges, inserting a linen thread between them, and tying it until it heals.  Things that dry and constrict should not be used in its treatment.
If you do that, the stye will return even worse. Instead, use softening substances like fenugreek, licorice root steeped in rose water, flaxseed, and a diacodion ointment mixed with some unguents. Apply this mixture to the thread and treat it with this.
The key to treating it is to avoid any re-adhesion in its original form.  This method, as described, is merely a part of correcting the stye, not a complete restoration to its original state.
Chapter Fourteen.
On treating styes in the lower eyelid.
This stye, which forms from below, is the true stye, and is either natural or accidental.  The accidental stye results from a wound, cut, burn, or similar causes.
The method involves taking a needle with a doubled thread, inserting it into the flesh, and passing it from the inner to the outer eyelid until the thread is on both sides of the flesh. Then, pulling the flesh upwards with the needle, cut it with a broad scalpel.
If the eyelid returns to its proper shape, well and good.  Otherwise, take a spatula and place it over the cut.  Turn the eyelid over and make two incisions in the inner side of the eyelid, the edges of these incisions meeting at the angle of the previous cut, forming an angle. So that when joined, the shape will resemble this shape.
Then, remove the flesh to the extent that the sharp side of it is below the eyelid.  Then, rejoin the separated parts with two stitches, sewing with a wool thread.  Then, treat it with the relaxing and tightening remedies that we mentioned, until it heals.
If the ulceration occurred from a cut, suture, or cauterization, a simple incision should be made, also without touching the eyelashes, as previously mentioned. Then, the lips should be separated with a tightening method, as described.
In short, for treating an ulceration, whether it is on top or bottom, conduct the treatment based on the appearance of the ulceration.  For it can vary greatly in its presentation, and the surgeon must devise a method by any means possible.
Until the shape returns to, or approximates, its natural state. The condition itself will indicate the appropriate action and tools in most cases.
Chapter Fifteen.
Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion may affect many people, causing the upper eyelid to adhere to the white of the eye, hindering its free movement and its natural functions.  A blunt instrument should be inserted underneath the eyelid.
Then lift it upwards or stretch it with a hook, and sever the adhesion with a gentle scalpel, one not as sharp as surgical blades, but rather somewhat dull, so as not to cause distress to the patient during the procedure, lest the scalpel slip and injure the eye with its sharpness. Your incision should be like peeling away the adhesion.
And wait until the eyelid returns to its normal position and the entire adhesion heals. Then, instill saline solution into the eye, or apply a solution of zinjari (a type of mineral substance) diluted in water, using a dropper, and separate the eyelid from the eye with a linen thread.
Place wet cotton wool soaked in egg white over the eye. After three days, use poultices until healed.
Chapter Sixteen.
In cutting the nail and deep fleshy growths.
The nail is of two types: either nervous, resembling a tough, thin membrane, or non-nervous, resembling a solid, white moisture. If touched by iron or attempted to be grasped with a hook, it breaks.
A hook does not stick to either type.  Both types begin in the largest part of the eye, gradually spreading until they cover the pupil and obstruct the light and movement of the eye.  The procedure for cutting them is for the patient to place their head on a stone.
Then, the patient opens their eyes and you raise the eyelid with your hand.  Then, you grasp the nail with a slightly bent hook and pull it upwards.  Then, take a needle, thread it with a horsehair or cowhair and a strong thread, bend the needle tip slightly, and insert it into the center of the nail.
Pierce it with the needle, tie the nail with the thread, pull it upwards, and peel away with the hair the side of the nail adjacent to the pupil, as if spreading it with the hair to its end. Then, cut the remaining portion at the root of the eye with a gentle scalpel or small scissors, and leave the natural tissue of the eye.
So that a continuous flow of tears does not result from cutting it.  The nail can be pulled with the hook alone or with the thread, as we said, then the smooth scalpel is used to peel the end, which is pictured as follows:
Be careful that the scalpel does not touch the cornea, causing a rupture in the pupil.  After the cutting, place some powdered salt or powdered green vitriol into the eye, and leave it until the next day, then treat it as necessary until healed.
If the nail is not sensitive and you cannot insert a needle or secure a hook, this type of nail is often treated with medications, healing as described in the classification. If you wish to cut it, open the patient's eye.
Take a gentle, smooth, blunt scalpel, like this picture. Peel the nail gently from above with the scalpel. If you see that it is crumbling and only the peel is affected, put a drop of powdered green vitriol or red vitriol or powdered salt into the eye, and compress the eye until the next day.
Then, repeat the procedure until all of it is gone, unless a hot abscess appears in the eye.  Leave it alone and treat the hot abscess until it is healed, then resume peeling until healed.  And as for a fleshy outgrowth...
If the outgrowth is causing a severe injury to the eye, attach the outgrowth to the hook and cut a portion of it off, but do not cut excessively lest it cause tear leakage.  Then, apply red or green vitriol to the depths until healed.
Chapter Seventeen.
On the excision of the hordeolum and any excess flesh that grows in the eye.
In some people's eyes, red, accumulated flesh may grow until it covers the pupil or nearly covers or spills over the eyelids, and sometimes the eyelids turn outward, resembling a rose.
The treatment involves placing the patient's head in your lap, then opening their eyes, and using appropriate hooks to remove all of the red flesh.
Or hold it with a scalpel or forceps, and then cut the flesh, piece by piece, until it is all removed.  Protect the eye from injury during the procedure.  Use either the scalpel described earlier for excising the toenail, or a small pair of scissors, for the excision. Like the method used to pick out splinters, as will be shown in the following chapter.
If the excision is complete and all the fleshy growth is gone, then fill the eye with powdered salt or drop in red or similar corrosive remedies. Apply a bandage of egg white to the outside of the eye to prevent inflammation.
If any fleshy growth remains, and bleeding is excessive, and you fear inflammation, leave the eye and treat it with something to reduce inflammation. Then return to the procedure until healed.  Do the same for any excess flesh that appears in the eye of this type.
However, you should avoid treating any weak eye through natural means or by surgical intervention with iron, and do not insert your hand into any of these procedures until you see that the organ is capable of withstanding the procedure.
Chapter Eighteen.
On the extraction of foreign objects from the eye.
Foreign bodies are red veins that weave over the eye, preventing sight and weakening the eye over time.  First, you should examine it. If the eye with the foreign bodies is strong, and has no other ailment besides the foreign bodies...
Then, the extraction is to instruct the patient to place their head on your lap, and to grasp those veins with a single hook, or two, depending on your skill.  The hooks should be subtly curved in this manner:.
or they can be two hooks joined together in a single instrument, like this:.
Then, gently extract the veins with a fine pair of scissors, wiping away the blood occasionally until you see the eye is free from the veins and the blood has drained.  Protect the eye; do not injure it with the scissors' edges.  Work in the middle of the day, in the sunlight.
Be very precise in your work to avoid cutting anything other than the veins.  Upon finishing, instill green or red antimony into the eye, to consume the remaining foreign bodies.  If you cannot remove all the foreign bodies at that time, then bandage the eye to relieve its pain.
Leave it for several days until the pain subsides and the inflammation abates.  Then, repeat the procedure in exactly the same way, until it is healed.  This is a depiction of the scissors used for this procedure.
Leave it [and let the patient's head rest on the student's lap, while another dries the tears and examines the front and back of the eye]. The proper procedure is to open the eye with fixed or triangular openings, held by a student.
Concentrate; the patient's head should rest on the student's lap, and another should wipe away tears and examine the front and back of the eye.  You have the choice of beginning the extraction from the upper or lower part of the eye.  It is preferable to dry two thread-like structures (sonar) in the upper part, one at the end of the crown and the other at its beginning.
Hold it [the eye] between the index and middle fingers.  Then hang the threads from underneath them.  Then hold it between the ring and pinky fingers, and gently remove your hand so that you see all the foreign bodies have risen.  Cut them from the smaller side, either with scissors or a delicate scalpel, if you wish.
Insert a needle, and carefully remove all the foreign matter until it rises to the surface.  Do not cut the drying threads, as tears and blood will flow profusely onto you.
Then cut with scissors what remains of the threads, both above and below the crown, so that nothing remains protruding except within the cornea. If possible, lift it from the cornea with a needle; that is better, and may prove suitable for some people.
If you cut it, the area will be painful for a few days.  The pain will subside and the inflamed swelling will be avoided, and then the procedure can be repeated.
Chapter Nineteen.
Regarding returning the rib to the nose.
Doctors call the rib a "nasus," if, after treating it with cauterization or a harsh, burning medication as previously described, it does not heal, then the only recourse is to incise the tumor when it is ripe and remove all the moisture or pus within it.
Until the bone is exposed. If the bone is exposed and you see damage or blackness in it, scrape it with an instrument, this is its form:.
And the instrument is called the "khashna al-ras," made of Indian iron, and its head is round like a seed. It is engraved, like a file or a scraper, with a fine engraving. You place it on the damaged area of the bone, then rotate it between your fingers while slightly squeezing your hand until you know that the damage has been removed.
And you do this repeatedly, then bandage the area with drying, astringent medications. If the area is sealed, flesh grows in it, the discharge ceases, and forty days pass without recurrence, or pus formation, or any incident.
Know then that it is healed, otherwise there is no remedy except to return the nasus to the nasal opening in this manner: expose the bone again with iron or a caustic medication.  Once the bone is exposed, take a probe of this shape:
The end of the iron instrument should be triangular, and its shaft tapered towards the tip, as you see. Then place it on the bone itself, close to the nostril, and move your hand slightly away from the eye.  Then, rotate your hand with the probe until it penetrates the bone and the patient feels it.
By finding that air comes out from it when he holds his hand over his nose.  At that point, bandage the area with astringent, drying medications, as I have taught you.  If the flesh grows and the area hardens, the matter that was protruding outward will return to the nose, making it easier on the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
In returning the protruded eye.
If the entire eye has protruded, and there is no damage or loss of vision, then ease the patient, then place it (the eye) in a sling. Then place a cupping glass on the back of the neck without restrictions, and apply gentle suction.
Then apply a poultice made of acacia, aloe, frankincense, and spikenard to the eye.  Then gently tie the eye over the bandage with numerous bandages. The recipe for the bandages is to take many soft strips of cloth, fold each strip into four layers, and use many of these.
Then place them one on top of the other as much as the eye needs, then tie them tightly with a firm cloth. Replace the cupping glass with suction, without restrictions, then remove the cupping glass and leave the eye tied for a day and night.
If the ligament relaxes during this time, it should be tightened again.  Then remove the bandage after one day and one night, as we said.  If you see the eye has returned, then stop. Otherwise, repeat the bandage, poultices, tightening, and cupping until it returns.
Chapter Twenty-one.
On the excision of the conjunctiva.
If a rupture occurs in the conjunctiva, then protrudes and emerges outside the eyelids, resembling the conjunctiva and spoiling the person's appearance, and you wish to excise it, then the procedure is as I describe.
To do this, insert a needle into the base of the conjunctiva from below upwards, then insert another needle with a doubled thread from the opposite side and pierce through it, leaving the first needle as it is.
Then, cut the doubled thread at the point of its bend, tie a portion of the conjunctiva around the needle with it, tightening it firmly.  Then, remove the needle and apply soaked wool dipped in egg white to the eye, leaving it until the threads and conjunctiva fall out.  Then, treat the eye with something that strengthens it.
Until the wound heals, the conjunctiva may be tightened from all sides with a circular piece of cloth, so that the eye protrudes more forcefully. Then, with a thin scalpel, reach the depth of the eye so that the whitish moisture flows out and the eye descends and withers in place, then tighten it until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On the treatment of abscesses.
This affliction, called an abscess, is a swelling that collects in the eye, resembling descending water but without its properties.  The method of treatment involves having the patient sit upright on a chair, then taking their head in both hands.
and move it until you see the swelling descend with your eyes, and then hold it still so it does not move, and the light will be released and the patient will see things as they used to.  If it does not descend, we know it is water. If the swelling does not descend with the method we described,
Otherwise, have the patient sit before you, then take a slender scalpel and make an incision just above the cornea where the inflamed area meets the cornea at the limbus, until the material is discharged.
Then, after the discharge, instill warm water into the eye, mixed with honey, or water in which you have boiled licorice root and honey. Then treat with the rest of the treatment until healed.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
We have already discussed the types of water in the classification, and which are suitable for instillation, with detailed explanations.  Take these from there for accuracy.  Then, the patient should sit before you, crouched, facing the light near the sun.
Tie and firmly hold the healthy eye, then raise the eyelid with your left hand if it is the left eye that has the water, or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then, place the tip of the cupping instrument near the limbus, with the thickness of a date pit, in the same white part of the eye, near the smaller eyelid corner.  Then, push the cupping instrument firmly, while rotating it with your hand, until it penetrates the white of the eye and you feel the cupping instrument has reached an empty space.
The cupping instrument should penetrate to a depth equal to the distance from the child to the edge of the iris (the limbus). The copper can be seen by the person looking, in the same way the eye sees, because of the clarity of the cornea. Then, move the cupping instrument upward to the location where the water is.
Then press it downward repeatedly. If the water comes out at once, the patient will see what has been opened to his sight immediately; the cupping instrument will be in his eye. Then he rests for a while. If the water rises, lower it again without removing the cupping instrument.
If it settles and does not rise, remove the cupping instrument gently, twisting your hand slightly. Then dissolve some clear, fine salt in the water, and wash the inside of the eye with it. Then place a moistened cloth or wool over the entire outer eye.
With rose ointment and egg white, bind the healthy eye.  As for us and the people of our time, we place crushed cumin with egg white on it.  If the cupping instrument will not enter the eye due to its hardness, because some people have very hard eyes. One should take the scalpel called the *bayrid*, which is of this form:
Pierce only the adhered tissue with it, do not make the hole deep.  Rather, make a gentle opening for the cupping instrument, then insert the cupping instrument as described above. When your work is complete, prepare a secure bed for the patient, in a dark room, with the patient lying on their back.
He must avoid all movement and coughing.  His food should be easy to digest. He must not move his head right or left at all. The bandage should remain in place for three days.  Then, in that dark room, remove the bandage and test his eyesight.
Show him objects, then replace the bandage for seven days.  You should not do this during the treatment period or after cupping.  Rather, you should avoid it before the water rises quickly with excessive staring.
If a hot swelling appears, then you should remove the bandage before the seventh day and treat the swelling with what will calm it. Once it has calmed, then release his sight. Place a veil over his face to train his eyesight from beneath it for several days while he is in that dark room.  Then...
He is gradually released from the room and allowed to move about. Know that a learner cannot dispense with observing cupping multiple times before undertaking the procedure.  I have heard from some Iraqis that they make a suction cupping instrument in Iraq that draws out water.
I have not seen anyone in our country do this, nor have I read of it in any of the earlier books. It may be a recent invention.  Here is a depiction of the various types of cupping instruments, to illustrate the different possibilities.
They are made of copper, specifically, and their tip is precisely this shape; triangular and sharp-pointed.
Chapter Twenty-four.
In the treatment of flesh growing in the nose.
Various superfluous growths may sprout in the nose, some resembling scorpions with many legs, others cancerous, hard, and dark-colored, and others soft and not dark-colored.
For those growths that are soft, not malignant or cancerous, the patient should sit facing the sun, their nostril opened, and the snare applied to the flesh.  Then it should be drawn out, and what is caught should be cut away with a gentle, sharp scalpel, on one side only.
To ensure that all the flesh is gone, if any remains uncut, remove the remainder gently with a suitable instrument, so that nothing remains; otherwise, bleeding or a hot swelling may ensue.
If it is a malignant tumor, then cauterize it until the bleeding stops and all the flesh disappears.  Then, after cutting, apply vinegar and water or other drink to the nose, so that the nose opens and moisture drains into the throat.
Know then that it has healed, but if the moisture does not drain properly, know that there is growing flesh inside the porous bones, which the cutting instrument did not reach.  In this case, a thick linen thread should be taken.
Tie many knots in it, making the space between each knot the width of a finger or less.  The patient should use a smooth instrument or something similar to a button to insert one end of the thread into their nose and then pull it along until it reaches the turbinate bone.
It will emerge at the throat.  Children often do this in schools.  It is easy for anyone who desires it.  Then, pull together the two ends of the thread, the one that has come out of the mouth and the other that remains in the nose.
Then use the knots in the thread to separate the flesh, continuing until you know the flesh has been separated by the thread's knots.  Then remove the thread.  After cleaning the blood from the nose, insert a thread soaked in Egyptian ointment into the nose. Do this for three days or more.
Until the ointment has consumed all remaining flesh.  Finally, a lead tube is placed in the nose for a period of days until healed.  If further drying treatment is needed, use that method.
This is a picture of the instrument used to instill ointments and medicines into the nose.
A small, open-topped, ointment-containing vessel, made of silver or brass, similar to a small lamp, is used.  Its passageway is also of this nature.
If desired, the tube can be made closed, like a cane, and the ointment applicator's surface flat.
It has a handle at its end, as you see, to hold when heating the ointment or any other liquid substance within it.
Chapter Twenty-five.
On warts growing on the tip of the nose.
Warts often grow on the tip of the nose, increasing in size and number over time until they disfigure the appearance.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut it off at its first appearance and remove it completely, then apply either cauterization or a burning medicine that serves as a cauterization. If cutting it off was delayed until it grew large, then examine it; if it is hard, dense, dark-colored, and insensitive,
do not use iron on it, for it is a cancerous tumor; I have often seen that cutting this tumor resulted in great suffering for the patient. If the tumor feels soft, is not dark-colored, and you see that it can be entirely cut out, drain the affected area and cut it out without hesitation or delay, and treat the area with what dries and contracts until healed.
Chapter Twenty-six.
On the suturing of the nose, lip, and ear. If its connection is separated by a wound or the like, know that when a separation of connection occurs in one of these cartilages, rarely does treatment succeed except in some people.
Therefore, when one of these things occurs, examine if the wound is fresh with blood. If so, bring together the edges of the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals. If the separation of connection has separated its edges and each gap is sound, then one must remove each gap's visible skin thickness until it bleeds.
Then, bring together the edges with sutures, tighten them, and dust them with powdered gypsum and frankincense. Place a plaster of palm ointment, or another suitable ointment, on top of the dusting, and leave it tightened for two or three days, then remove it.
Then change the medication and leave it until the threads break by themselves, then treat it with ointment until it heals.  The method of suturing is to bring together the separated connection, either with needles as described in abdominal suturing, or with thread, as previously explained.
Chapter Twenty-Seven.
On removing the knots that appear on the edges.
Many people develop small, hard tumors inside their lips, some resembling carbuncles, others smaller or larger.  One should turn back the lip and cut open each knot, and remove it with tweezers.
Cut it from all sides, then fill the wound with powdered alum until the bleeding stops.  Then rinse with vinegar and salt, and treat the affected areas with something astringent until the wounds heal.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
In cutting excess flesh on the gums.
Excess flesh often grows on the gums, which the ancients called *apulus*.  It should be grasped with tweezers or a pick, cut at its root, and allowed to bleed.  Then, powdered alum should be placed on the area.
Or one of the astringent, dried substances. If the flesh returns after treatment, it often does. Cut it again, and cauterize it, for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
In cleaning the teeth with iron.
Rough, unsightly scales may collect on the surfaces of the teeth, both inside and outside, and between the gums.  They may turn black, yellow, or green, leading to decay of the gums and an unsightly appearance of the teeth.
The patient should be seated before you, head resting on your lap.  Carefully clean the tooth or teeth exhibiting scales and sand-like substances until nothing remains.  Do the same for any black, green, or yellow discoloration, until clean.
If the cleaning is complete on the first attempt, proceed. Otherwise, repeat the cleaning process another day, and again on a third day, until the desired result is achieved.  Know that different shaped cleaning instruments, many in form, are required depending on the task.
Because the instrument used to clean the inside of a tooth is different from the one used for the outside, and another still for cleaning between the teeth.  You should have a variety of these instruments prepared.
Chapter Thirty.
On the extraction of teeth.
One should attempt to treat a toothache by all means before resorting to extraction, for there is no remedy after extraction, as it is a noble substance.  Only when extraction is absolutely necessary should it be performed.
Therefore, if the patient insists on extraction, one must be certain the toothache is healed. Often, the patient is deceived by the pain, thinking it is in a healthy tooth, and has it extracted, only for the pain to persist until the diseased tooth is also extracted.  We have observed this repeatedly in the actions of barbers.
If the painful tooth is clearly identified, then one must carefully use a strong forceps to detach the gum on all sides and then gently rock the tooth back and forth, bit by bit, with fingers or fine forceps, until it loosens.
Then, firmly grasp the tooth with large forceps, with the patient's head between your knees, ensuring no movement.  Pull the tooth straight to avoid breaking it.  If it doesn't come out, take one of the instruments and carefully insert it underneath the tooth from all sides, gently rocking it as before.
If the tooth is hollow or decayed, it is necessary to fill the cavity with a cloth and firmly seal it with a thin, smooth strip, so it does not break apart when you grip it with forceps.  Carefully detach the gum from all sides, thoroughly.
Use caution to avoid breaking the tooth, as a piece left behind will cause the patient greater suffering than the original pain.  Do not act like the unskilled barbers who extract teeth without the methods we have described.
They often inflict great harm on people, the least of which is breaking the tooth and leaving the roots, either all or in part. Or, as we have witnessed repeatedly, it may pull out part of the jawbone.  After extraction, the patient should rinse with a drink, vinegar and salt. If bleeding occurs, which frequently happens, crush some alum and apply it to the area.
Otherwise, if the alum is ineffective, use the delicate forceps, the ones that first move the tooth.  Their handles should be short, and the tips long and thick, so they do not bend when you grip the tooth.  This is a picture of the larger forceps.
The handles should be thick, so when you grasp them, they do not yield or bend.  The tips should be short.  They should be made of Indian steel or tempered, strong steel.  On the tip, there should be teeth which interlock securely.
The tips can also be made like a file, making them very strong and precise.
The Thirty-First Chapter.
On the extraction of tooth roots and the removal of fractured jawbones.
If, after removing a tooth, a root remains broken, you should apply a piece of cloth soaked in ghee for a day or two to relax the area.  Then, use forceps or pliers. Which resemble the beak of a bird called a 'balrajah' and this is a picture of the forceps.
If it is not possible to extract it with these forceps, then you must drill into the root and expose all the flesh with a scalpel, then insert the tool resembling a small lever, of which this is an image.
Short, slightly thick at the end, and not sharp, lest it break.  If the root comes out with this, otherwise, use the other tools, of which this is an image; the first one is triangular at the end with some thickness.
Another delicate triangular image.
We can also use this instrument with two branches, of which this is also an image.
And with other tools and instruments, which were previously mentioned in the inventory of the teeth, and we may also use this instrument resembling a large hook, of which this is an image.
Triangular, curved at the end, with a little thickness to prevent breakage, and not sharp. And know that dental instruments are numerous, and similarly, other instruments are almost countless.  A skillful craftsman, through his practice, may invent tools as indicated by the work and the diseases themselves.
For some diseases, the ancients did not describe instruments, because of the differences in their types. If a bone of the jaw or one of the bones of the mouth breaks or decays, investigate it in its place with a suitable tool from among these instruments and forceps, mentioned in removing the roots.
And we use the forceps, this is its image.
It should have some slight thickness to grip the bone firmly so it does not slip until the bone is extracted, and we treat the area with appropriate medications. If the bone has a slight decay, remove the decay and blackness until it is clean, then treat it until it heals.
Thirty-second chapter.
In the eruption of teeth that sprout over others.
Teeth that erupt out of their natural course disfigure the appearance, especially in women and slaves.  One must look closely. If a tooth has erupted behind another tooth,
If it cannot be moved or retracted, remove it. If it is attached to another tooth, cut it with this instrument, whose image is this:.
It resembles a small beak, made of sharp Indian steel.  Cut it over many days due to the tooth's hardness and to avoid disturbing other teeth. If it is protruding and firmly placed, cool it with an Indian file, of this form.
It should be entirely of Indian steel, with very fine engravings, resembling the file used to make needles.  Cool the tooth gradually over many days, gently, to avoid dislodging it and causing it to fall out.  Then, smooth it afterwards and scrape it with certain scrapers.
If a tooth has broken and causes pain to the tongue while speaking, it should also be cooled until the roughness of the fracture disappears and it becomes smooth, not hurting the tongue nor impairing speech.
Chapter Thirty-three.
In fastening loose teeth with silver or gold thread.
If older teeth become loose and displaced due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot eat anything for fear of their falling out, and treatment with astringent remedies has been unsuccessful.
The solution is to fasten them with gold or silver thread; gold is better because silver tarnishes and becomes foul after days, whereas gold remains unchanged. The thread should be of medium thickness and thinness, appropriate to fit between the teeth.
The method of fastening is to take the thread and insert its fold between the healthy teeth, then weave the ends of the thread through the loose teeth, one or more, until you reach the healthy tooth on the other side.
Then weave back to the starting side, gently and wisely tightening your grip so the teeth do not move.  Tighten the thread at the roots of the teeth to prevent it from slipping.  Then cut the excess ends of the thread with scissors, gather them, twist them together with a tool, and hide them between the healthy tooth and the loose tooth.
To avoid injuring the tongue.  Then leave it tightened as long as it remains; if it loosens or breaks, tighten it again with another thread, and the patient may enjoy it thus for a lifetime.  This is the method for fastening teeth, with two healthy and two loose teeth, as shown.
A single or two teeth may be re-inserted into their place and fastened as described, and remain. This is the work of a skilled craftsman.  He may also carve bone from a cow's bone, shaping it like a tooth and placing it in the space where the tooth was lost. And tightens it, as we said, so it remains and is enjoyed.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On severing the ligament that lies under the tongue and prevents speech.
This ligament that lies under the tongue may be either natural, present at birth, or accidental, resulting from a healed wound.
The procedure is to open the patient's mouth, place their head in your lap, and lift their tongue.  Then, cut the ligament transversely until the tongue is released from its restraint. If it is somewhat hard and entangled, if it is from a healed wound, use a probe to split it transversely until the ligament is severed and the knots are loosened.
Be careful not to cut too deep into the flesh, severing an artery and causing bleeding.  Then, rinse the cut area with rose water or diluted vinegar afterward.  Place a linen compress under the tongue for the patient to hold in place each night.
To prevent re-adhesion.  If bleeding occurs, apply powdered alum to the area.  If the bleeding is severe, cauterize the site with a lentil-shaped cautery iron. Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-five.
For removing a toad that has developed under the tongue.
A swelling resembling a small toad may form under the tongue, hindering its normal function.  It may even enlarge to fill the mouth.  Treatment involves opening the patient's mouth facing the sun and examining the tumor.
If it appears dark-colored or black, hard, and the patient feels no sensation in it, do not attempt to treat it, as it is cancer. If it leans towards whiteness and contains moisture, insert a probe, and carefully cut it open with a scalpel, removing it from all sides.
If blood flows excessively while working, apply pulverized alum until the bleeding stops; then return to your procedure until complete removal, and finally have the patient gargle with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with all other appropriate remedies until healed.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
In the treatment of tonsil tumors.
And any tumors growing in the throat, which may present glands resembling those appearing externally, are called tonsils. If these have been treated as described in the preceding sections but have not healed, ...
If the tumor is hard, dark-colored, and insensitive, do not use a knife. If it is red and thick, also do not use a knife for fear of bleeding, but leave it until it matures; either it will soften or burst of its own accord.
If it is white, round, and thin, this is what should be cut. Before doing so, check if the inflammation has completely subsided or if some of the inflammation has decreased.
Then, have the patient sit facing the sun, head resting on your lap, mouth open, and a helper by your side who will press the patient's tongue downwards with an instrument, which looks like this:
Make one of silver or brass, as thin as a knife.  When you have pressed the tongue down and the tumor is visible, take a hook and insert it into the tonsil, pulling it outwards as far as possible, without pulling any of the membranes along.
Then cut it with an instrument, which looks like a pair of scissors, but with the tips bent and very sharp, made of Indian iron or Damascus steel.
If this instrument is not available, cut it with a scalpel, which looks like this:
Sharp on one side and blunt on the other.  After cutting one tonsil, cut the other in the same manner.  Then, after cutting, the patient should gargle with cold water or vinegar and water.
If bleeding occurs, gargle with water in which pomegranate peels or rue leaves, or similar astringents, have been boiled, until the bleeding stops. Then treat it until it heals. Other tumors may grow in the throat besides the tonsils, and these are to be cut in the same way as the tonsils.
I treated a woman with a tumor that had grown inside her throat, resembling a small, poorly sensitive lump, which almost blocked her throat.  The woman was breathing through a narrow passage and had been unable to eat or drink.
She was close to death if she had lived another day or two. The tumor had grown two branches that extended to her nostrils. I acted quickly, inserting a hook into one of them, and pulled out a piece.
Then I cut it where it emerged from the nostril, and did the same with the other nostril. Then I opened her mouth, pressed down her tongue, inserted the hook into the tumor itself, and cut off a portion of it, with only a small amount of blood flowing.
The woman's throat opened up, and she immediately drank water and ate.  We repeatedly cut away at that tumor over a long period of time, and it kept growing back in place of what was cut away, and this went on for a long time, involving both me and her.
I devised a method and cauterized the tumor within her throat, causing it to stop growing.  Then I left the area, and I do not know what happened to her after I departed.
Chapter Thirty-seven.
On the removal of a goiter.
If a tumor, called an *unba*, descends to the throat and swells, being white and oblong, it is called a *amood*. If it is thick at the bottom and round, it is called an *unba*. If treatment as described in our classification is ineffective.
If the hot tumor has subsided and is thin, it should be cut.  If it is a compacted, round mass with no length, and is the color of blood, or dark or lacks sensitivity, it should be avoided.
If it presents the described characteristics of whiteness and length, the patient should sit facing the sun, and the tongue should be pressed with the instrument described before, then the snare should be inserted into the *unba*.
Pull it downward and cut it with one of the two instruments mentioned in the section on tonsil removal.  Only the excess beyond the natural amount should be cut, for cutting more will harm the voice and speech.
Then, after cutting, use the methods described for tonsil removal and treat it until healed. If the patient is hesitant to have it cut, use cauterization without fear or caution. The method of cauterization is with a sharp medicine, and the patient's head should be placed on your lap.
Then, press the tongue with the instrument mentioned before, take the sharp water mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, mix it with quicklime, neither thick nor thin, fill the indentation of this instrument, and this is its form.
The tip where you place the medicine has a depression like the depression of a mard spoon.  Place the instrument with the medicine on the uvula itself, with the patient lying on their side, so that saliva will drain from the mouth from inside the throat, so nothing will go down into the throat and harm him.
Then, hold the medicine in your hand and press it against the uvula for about half an hour until you see it blackened and the burning sensation of the medicine subsides.  If you wish, take a piece of cotton, wrap it around the end of a mard spoon, and moisten the cotton with the medicine.
Insert the spoon with the cotton into the tube from above until the cotton adheres to the uvula.  Repeat this several times until you have achieved the desired cauterization of the uvula. Then leave it; it will wither and fall off after three or four days. If you need to reapply the medicine, do so.
After the cauterization, wipe around the uvula with cotton soaked in ghee, and wipe away any medicine around it.  Then, the patient gargles with cold water and is treated externally with poultices and internally with gargles until healed. This type of treatment is safer than cutting and further from fear.
The uvula can also be treated with something milder than cauterization or cutting, by using incense in this way:  Take frankincense, saffron, sage, squill, wormwood, chamomile, and rue, or similar herbs. Combine them all, or some of them, in a pot and soak them in vinegar.
And it is boiled, while the pot is covered with earthenware, and there is a hole in the middle of the lid on which a hollow instrument is placed. This instrument is made of silver or copper.
The end of the device with the pomegranate is inserted into the patient's mouth, so that the steam rises up to the uvula through the tube until the uvula shrinks.  Then, repeat the process several times until it withers.  Be careful not to perform this treatment at the very onset of the swelling.
For it often increases the swelling.  Rather, it should be done when the hot swelling has subsided.  If you do not have this device, take a reed and fit a hard-boiled egg shell to its end, so that the patient's mouth does not burn.
Because the egg shell prevents the heat of the steam from burning the mouth. This is a good and safe treatment.
Chapter Thirty-eight.
On the removal of thorns and other foreign objects lodged in the throat.
Thorns, fish bones, or other objects often lodge in the throat.  It is necessary to remove any visible ones that can be seen, after pressing the tongue with the instrument under the light.
To ascertain what is in the throat; if something is hidden and not visible, the patient should vomit before the food has digested in the stomach.  Perhaps the foreign object will come out with the vomit, or the patient will swallow a piece of a twig or the root of a plant.
Or he swallows a piece of dry bread, or takes a piece of soft sea sponge, ties it to a thread, then swallows it.  When it reaches the location of the thorn, pull the thread quickly, repeating this several times.
Often the thorn or bone adheres to it and comes out. If it does not come out by the methods mentioned, then use a lead instrument in this manner:
It should be slightly thicker than a lead rod.  At its tip there is a hook. The patient inserts it into their throat gently, while raising their head upward and taking care not to touch their throat to avoid coughing, and thereby dislodging the bone or thorn.  Or the physician inserts it with their hand.
Having the patient insert it is better, as they know the location of the foreign object, and pushing it downward, or pulling their hand with the instrument upward, should be done to the extent possible until it comes out.
Chapter Thirty-nine.
Removing a leech lodged in the throat.
If, having treated the leech with the remedies mentioned in the section on medicinal treatment, it proves ineffective, then examine the patient's throat in the light of the sun, after pressing down the tongue with the instrument that I have described to you.
If you see the leech, pull it out with a small hook or a gentle, firm forceps.  If that is not possible, take a hollow tube, insert it into the patient's throat near the leech, and then insert a heated iron into the tube's interior.
Do this several times. The patient should abstain from water all day.  Then, he should take a jar filled with cool water, open his mouth over it, gargle, without swallowing any of it.  He should move the water with his hand from time to time.
If the leech feels the water, it will fall off. If it does not come out using the methods described, fumigate the throat with urine and with hellebore using the instrument described for fumigation of the throat. Do this several times and it will fall off.
The method for fumigation is to take a vessel containing embers heated by fire. The vessel is covered with a lid having a hole in the middle.  Place the end of the instrument in this hole, then put the fumigation material into the vessel.  The patient should place their mouth on the end of the tube and close their mouth tightly so that the smoke does not escape until it is certain that the smoke has reached the throat.
If the leech falls off at that point, good.  If it does not fall off, the fumigation is repeated several times.  The patient should endure thirst and eat salty foods and garlic, but not drink any water.  The leech must come out with this treatment.  This is an image of an instrument used to pull the leech from the throat when it is spotted.
It resembles pincers, as you see, except that it has this bend which enters the throat, and its tips are like a bird's beak.  There is the roughness of a file on them; if they grasp something, they do not let go.
Another instrument is used to press the tongue.  It is a wide ring of iron, as wide as the mouth, placed above the tongue. It is inserted into the mouth upright on its side, so that the mouth remains open involuntarily.  The patient cannot remove it at all until it is removed.
Description of the instrument used to open the mouth in this exact manner.
Chapter Forty.
In it, there is discussion of the incision and lancing of tumors.
Tumors are numerous and varied, as will be described one by one in this book.  Their incision and lancing differ in two ways: one, from the nature of the tumor itself and the humours it contains.
The second way is due to the locations in the body where they occur, for a tumor arising in the head differs from one arising in the buttocks, and a tumor arising in fleshy tissue differs from one arising in a joint.  Each has its own treatment.
Some tumors should not be incised until the pus has matured and is fully formed; others should be incised while still raw and not fully matured, such as tumors close to the joints.
Because if a tumor arises near a joint and continues to fester until it rots the surrounding tissue, it may damage the ligaments or nerves of that joint, causing paralysis of that limb, or the tumor may be near a vital organ.
Because if you delay incision until it matures, you will harm the vital organ, or if it is near the buttocks, you should incise it raw.  It is necessary to incise a raw, incompletely matured tumor near the buttocks, lest the decay spread inwards and reach the anus and become a fistula.
It will become incurable.  You should know when to incise fully matured tumors, which is when the tumor pain subsides, the fever abates, the redness and throbbing decrease, the head of the tumor softens, and other symptoms disappear.
The incision should be made at the lowest point of the tumor, if possible, to allow the discharge to flow downwards; or at the thinnest, most prominent part of the tumor.  If the tumors are on the arms or legs, the incision should be made lengthwise along the limb.
The incision should be made over muscle, tendons, nerves, and arteries, generally in all straight, non-bending areas.  For those that bend, the incision should follow the natural contour of the area.  If the tumor is in a fleshy area, it is best to leave it alone.
Until its maturity is complete, as we have stated.  If you incise it before then, the pus will flow excessively, causing much harm and dirtiness, and possibly hardening the edges and the wound. Some tumors may require an incision across the body, if necessary, or in accordance with the needs of the affected limb.
Small tumors require a single incision, while large tumors require a broad incision or many incisions, depending on the size of the tumor. Some tumors may require the skin to be incised and cut away if it has become like a tattered cloth.
It has become, in some cases, like dead tissue, as often seen in many tumors and abscesses.  Some are incised with a three-angled cut, and some are cut like a rue leaf, like a tumor of the groin.
Some require a circular or crescent-shaped incision, and similar types of incisions.  Tumors that do not have a head, such as flat, smooth tumors, should only be incised with a simple incision. If the tumor is large, has existed for a long time, and has been incised, do not rush to drain all the pus at once.
Instead, drain some of it, then compress the tumor until the next day, and drain some more pus. Repeat this gradually until all the pus is removed.
Especially if the patient is weak, a pregnant woman, a young child, or an elderly person, the vital spirit often weakens with the sudden expulsion of pus.  The patient might die without you realizing it.  Beware of this matter greatly.
After incising these tumors, you should clean the wound and examine it.  If the tumor wound is small or the incision is simple, use a bandage of linen or worn cotton.  If the tumor is large and the incisions are numerous,
Then you should insert a thread into each incision until they meet.  If part of the skin has been severed or eroded by the tumor, you should fill it with worn cotton or linen fibers without moisture and compress it until the third day.
Then remove it and treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If bleeding occurs during your work, use cold water and vinegar, after soaking a linen cloth in them, and apply it to the bleeding area repeatedly.
If the bleeding persists, you should use the methods and treatments, and the cauterization techniques, that we have described in many places in this book and in the classifications. If it is winter and the tumor area is highly sensitive, you should soak the compresses in warm drinks and oil.
And place them on the area. If it is summer and the areas are fleshy, you should apply compresses soaked in water and oil, or in cool drinks and oil, until, as we said, the third day, when you should remove the tumor and clean it.
And use similar treatments until it heals. This is what you need to know about treating tumors in general.  As for the detailed approach, each tumor's treatment is described in a summarized and categorized manner.
Chapter Forty-One.
On the treatment of tumors appearing on the scalp.
Small tumors appear on the scalp, a type of pustule contained within membranes, resembling the characteristics of a hen's egg.  Their types are numerous, including fatty ones and those containing a moisture like mud.
Some contain moisture like porridge and soup, and similar substances. Some are stony and hard.  None are dangerous to cut open and remove, unless you encounter a blood vessel while cutting.
The procedure for cutting them is to first probe them with the instrument, whose image will come after this, called a probe, so you know what they contain. If they contain moisture, cut them lengthwise with a simple incision in this manner:
Begin the incision from line ~b~ to line ~c~. If the moisture is discharged, peel away the sac that contained it and cut it entirely.  Leave absolutely nothing behind, as it often returns if any part remains.
Then dip cotton in the resinous ointment, if available, or in salt water, and fill the wound with it. Leave it for another day, for it will consume any remaining sac.  Then apply the cotton with the resinous ointment again, and a third time if necessary.
Until you are certain that nothing of the sac remains. Then treat the wound with ointments until healed. If the swelling contains fatty matter, cut it in this manner:
Make a firm incision, and place forceps on the edges of the wound. Peel it away from every side, and exert all your effort in removing the membrane that contains it. If an artery obstructs you, do what was previously described to you.  Similarly, do this with the swelling, if it is hardened from the incision.
The treatment is the same as previously described. The incision on a hardened swelling is easier, because it has little blood and moisture. Some of these swellings in the head in some people may have no moisture at all.  This is because I incised a swelling in...
the head of an old woman, and found the swelling as hard as stone, rough, white; one could not break it even if one threw it, and it would only chip.  Other swellings occurring on the head, such as those which appear on children's heads, were not like this.
At the roots of the ears, make a simple incision along their entire length, and create a slope from the lower portion so that the material can drain downwards.  Then treat it with the appropriate therapy.
Chapter Forty-two.
On the incision of swine-like swellings which often appear in the neck.  These swellings often appear in the neck and under the armpits and in the groin.  They may appear elsewhere on the body.  The one which appears in the neck may be single or multiple, and some arise from others.
Each such swelling lies within its own special membrane, as do tumors and swellings of the head, as we have described.  There are many types of these swellings, some hard, some containing moisture, and some malignant, which do not respond to treatment.
Of those I have seen, some felt good and their appearance was close to the color of the skin.  They moved in all directions and were not attached to the neck's nerve, vein, or artery, nor were they sunken. Then it is necessary to incise it simply from top to bottom of the body in this manner, from line ~J~ to line ~B~.
One should dissect it from all sides, and stretch the skin flaps with a hook or two, or many hooks if needed, as we said regarding swellings of the head.  Remove it little by little, taking care not to sever a vein or nerve.  The scalpel should not be excessively sharp.
So that your hand does not cause more cutting or cause distress to the patient, leading to the severing of what is not necessary to cut.  If you cut a vein or artery, preventing you from continuing, then apply powdered za'aj or some of the substances which stop bleeding to the wound.
Then tighten the wound and leave it until the swelling subsides, the wound relaxes, and putrefaction begins.  At that point the bleeding will cease, so return to your work until you are finished. Then, use your index finger to check if there are any remaining, then smaller, splinters and cut them and remove them.
If a large vein is at the base of the splinter, you should not cut the splinter at its base.  Instead, you should tie it with a doubled, strong thread and let it fall out on its own without causing harm.  Then fill the wound with used cotton soaked in Egyptian ointment, and then treat it.
If you have cut the splinter entirely, you should bring together the edges of the wound and stitch it immediately, after making sure there is absolutely no remaining splinter.  If you see that a splinter of the bone remains, however.
You should aim to cut at the root and base of the splinter, and use stitching as we described.  For splinters containing moisture, apply a simple compress where their point of ripening appears, placing the compress as we said, below.
Then, after the compress, apply a bandage with Egyptian ointment, or similar, to consume any remaining corruption.  Once you see the wound is clean, treat it with healing ointments until it heals.
Chapter Forty-Three.
On opening the throat for a tumor occurring within the throat.
The ancients mentioned this opening of the throat, but I have seen no one in our country perform it.  This agrees with their account.  As for those with difficulty breathing, it is necessary to avoid opening the throat, as it will not benefit them.
If all the tumors and lungs are diseased, and those with a hot tumor in the mouth, throat, or tonsils, if there is no disease in the trachea, then opening the throat is necessary to escape the harm of suffocation.
If we wish to do this, then the throat should be opened under three rings of the trachea, or four small transverse incisions between two rings, as far as the incision should be in the pleura and not in the cartilage.  This place is suitable for the incision.
Because it lacks flesh and the blood vessels are distant from it, if the surgeon is timid, then the throat's skin should be stretched with a hook, and then the skin should be cut until, when it reaches the trachea, he should avoid the blood vessels if he sees any.
Then, he should cut the pleura, which we have described, and the incision of the trachea is shown by the phlegm that comes out of it with the things that are being discharged, and from the cessation of the voice, and the wound is left open for a time, and when the time that was feared for suffocation has passed, he should bring together the lips of the wound from the skin.
Then, its line is alone, without cartilage, and then the medications that produce flesh are used until it heals.  The author of this book said: The explanation of this speech that we have recounted is only if they see the patient's throat blocked by one of these tumors that they mentioned.
When the patient was close to death and was about to die, they went to cut the throat so that the patient would breathe through the wound, and be saved from death.  However, they were ordered to leave the wound open until the disease's course was completed, and the course of the disease is about three days or so.
Then they ordered the wound to be sutured and treated until it healed.  What I saw with my own eyes was a servant who took a knife and slashed her throat, cutting part of her lung's trachea.  I was called to treat her, and I found her weakening as a slaughtered animal weakens, so I examined the wound.
I found the blood that came out of the wound to be little, so I was certain that she had not cut an artery or a vein.  Air was coming out of the wound. I quickly sutured the wound and treated it until it healed.  The servant was not harmed, except for some hoarseness in her voice, nothing more.
