And after a few days, she returned to her best condition.  From this, we say that cutting the throat has no danger.
Chapter Forty-Four.
On the incision of the tumor that appears on the larynx from the outside, called the pharyngeal tumor.
This tumor, called a pharyngeal tumor, is a large tumor the color of the body. It is common in women and has two types.
Either it is natural or accidental. The natural one is incurable.  The accidental one comes in two types: one similar to fatty tumors, and the other similar to tumors that arise from the complexity of the artery; there is danger in cutting it. Therefore, one should not use the knife at all, except for those that are small.  If, upon probing and examining it with a probe, it is found to resemble a fatty tumor and not connected to any vessels, cut it like you would cut a tumor. And remove it with whatever sac it is contained in, if it is in a sac. Otherwise, remove the whole thing and then treat the area with the appropriate treatment.
Chapter forty-five.
On cutting various tumors.
Tumors have many types, and all their types have been mentioned in the classification.  It is necessary here to explain the difference between a tumor and an abscess, as this is problematic.  I say that an abscess is accompanied by heat, fever, and frightening pains until the boiling of the inflammation subsides. The putrid material then develops, and the fever and intensity subside.  A tumor is not accompanied by heat, fever, or pain. It is contained within a serous sac, which is a special covering for it. It is the same color as the body, and it begins as a small lump, growing into something larger or smaller.
They are of two types, either fatty or containing moisture.  The colors of moisture are numerous, as mentioned in the classification.  Therefore, when treating a tumor, you must first probe and examine it with an instrument called a "meds," as its image will appear in the following chapter.
Assess the size of the tumor, then withdraw the meds and observe what comes out afterward. If a flowing moisture of any color emerges, then make a small incision, as described in other tumors. If no moisture emerges after using the meds, know that it is fatty.
Incise it with a firm incision, in this manner, as I have taught you.  Hang it with hooks and carefully detach the skin from every side, and if possible, remove the sac containing the tumor intact.
If the sac breaks during the procedure, and you are unable to remove it intact, as often happens, remove it piece by piece, so that nothing remains.  If any part remains, large or small, the tumor will likely recur.
If you are overcome and only a small part remains, cauterize the wound with some sharp cauterizing substances when finished, and suture the wound. Apply a remedy to calm the hot tumor and treat it with other remedies until it heals. If the tumor is large, suture its edges and treat it with remedies to close the wound.
If a bleeding vein, or other vein, interferes and causes bleeding, immediately cauterize the affected area with powdered alum. Leave it compressed for two or three days until the wound becomes infected and the blood ceases to boil, then proceed to remove any remaining parts of the tumor.

Chapter forty-seven.
On the treatment of the male breast.
Some people's breasts may swell to resemble women's breasts during puberty, remaining swollen and unsightly.  If one dislikes this, one should make a crescent-shaped incision on the breast in this shape.
From line ~b~ to line ~c~. Then remove all the fat, then fill the wound with the medicated ointment, then stitch together the wound's edges and treat it until it heals.  If the breast sags and relaxes due to the weakening of its structure, as happens in women.
One should make two crescent-shaped incisions on its upper sides, connecting at their ends, so that the larger incision encircles the smaller one in this manner. From line ~b~ to line ~c~, then the skin between the incisions is removed, the fat is extracted, and the previously mentioned stitching, sutures, and necessary treatments are applied until healed.
If you are unable to remove everything that should be removed, either because of the patient's distress or excessive bleeding, then the wound should be packed with cotton soaked in corrosive ointment, left until the remaining fat is consumed, then treated until healed.
Chapter Forty-eight.
On the treatment of tumors that appear under the armpit.
These tumors that appear under the armpit are of the swine-like variety, hard and contained within a sac. Some contain moisture.  Those containing moisture should be incised with a crescent-shaped cut, like this.
From line ~b~ to line ~c~.  If all the matter in the tumor has been discharged, pack it with used cotton and leave it until the next day, then remove the cotton.
And treat it with the appropriate ointment until it heals. If the tumor is of the "swine's knot" type, cut it as we described for swine tumors.  If the wound becomes purulent, use cauterization as previously described.
Chapter forty-nine.
In the incision of a tumor appearing near an artery or vein, called an aneurysm.
If an artery is wounded and the skin above it adheres, a tumor often results.  Similarly, a vein can also develop swelling and a tumor.
The signs distinguishing whether a swelling is arterial or venous:  If from an artery, the swelling is oblong, clustered deep within the body, and when you press it with your finger, you feel a crackling sensation.
A venous swelling is round and on the surface of the body.  Incisions on these tumors are dangerous, especially in the armpit, groin, neck, and many other areas of the body.  Large tumors should be avoided when treated with iron.
Also, those in the limbs or head should be avoided.  For those that have an arterial swelling, make a lengthwise incision in the skin, then widen the cut with hooks, and then separate and free the artery from the membranes.
Until it is exposed.  Then insert a needle beneath it, and pierce it to the other side.  Tie the artery with a double thread in two places, as I have shown you for the arteries in the neck.  Then pierce the area between the ligaments with a lancet until all the blood is released.
The swelling will subside.  Then use the treatment that produces pus until the ligaments fall off.  Then treat with the appropriate ointments until it heals.
If the swelling is from a vein rupture, you should grasp the swelling with the skin as much as possible.  Then insert a needle below the area you grasped, and pierce it with a double thread until it comes out on the other side. Then tie the swelling firmly, in the manner I described for tying the aneurysm on both sides of the thread. If you fear the threads will slip, insert another needle with another thread under the entire swelling, where the first needle crosses it.
Tie your threads in four places. Then cut the swelling open in the middle, until what is inside comes out. Cut off any excess skin, and leave what was tied. Then place a bandage on it, dipped in a mixture of liquid and oil. Then use the treatment of twisting and ointments until it heals.
Chapter Fifty.
In the swelling that arises from nerve twisting.
Just as swelling occurs in the artery and vein, so too does swelling occur in the nerve if it is struck or caused by excessive exertion, or similar things.  This is more common in the wrists and ankles.
And in every place where the joints move, it is a hard swelling that resembles the color of the rest of the body.  Most often, it is painless.  If pressed firmly, the patient feels a numbness-like sensation.  The swelling is not deeply embedded in the body.
Instead, it is under the skin, moving in every direction, but not forward or backward.  Any swelling in the joints should not be treated with a knife, for it may cause a festering sore.  And any swelling in the head or forehead...
Cut the skin with a scalpel.  If the swelling is small, hold it with forceps and cut it from the root. If it is large, tie it with a noose, then scrape it off, remove it, and stitch the wound, treating it until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-one.
On the removal of warts appearing on the abdomen.
Warts, called "fungal" because of their resemblance to fungi, often appear on the abdomen and throughout the body.  Their base is thin, and their head thick, sometimes with thickened edges.  They vary in size, from small to very large.
I have observed a man with two warts on his abdomen, resembling fungi in appearance.  Their base was thin and white, their edges thickened, and moisture constantly oozed from them.  I removed them, and the weight of one was approximately eighteen ounces and the other approximately six ounces.
If the wart is moist, white, and thin-based, cut it with a broad scalpel while having a cautery iron heated nearby, as considerable bleeding often occurs during removal.
If bleeding is excessive, cauterize the area.  If the patient is afraid of cutting with iron, take a tightly wound lead thread, tie it around the wart with these characteristics, and leave it for two days, gradually tightening the lead thread.
As the lead thread tightens the wart, it will eventually detach and fall off without further effort. If the wart has a thick base, cutting it carries a risk, especially on the abdomen, though half or part of it might be removed.
Then cauterize it to prevent recurrence, and treat the wound until it heals.  Beware of cutting a wart that is dark-colored, insensitive, and dull-looking, as it may be a cancerous tumor.  We will discuss cancer later.
The Fifty-second Chapter.
In the treatment of umbilical tumors.
Umbilical tumors arise from various causes, either from a rupture of the peritoneum in the abdomen, allowing the bowels to protrude as in other hernias, or from blood emanating from a vein or artery, as previously described, or from trapped air.
If it is from a rupture of the peritoneum and protrusion of the bowels, the tumor will be similar in color to the body, soft without pain, and of varied shape. If it is from the protrusion of the bowels, its shape will be even more markedly different as described.  If you press it with your fingers, it will disappear then reappear, and perhaps there will be rumbling. It will often become larger with bathing or intense exertion.  If it is from moisture, it will be soft.
If it does not disappear when pressed with your hand, and does not increase or decrease in size, then if it is from blood, the tumor will exhibit these signs, but also turn dark. If it is from a newly grown flesh, the tumor will be hard and firm, and maintain a consistent size.
If it is from air, it will be soft to the touch.  In this case, if the umbilical protrusion is due to blood from an artery or vein, or air, you should abstain from treating it, as there is danger and deception involved, as I informed you in the chapter on tumors arising from arteries and veins.
If the umbilical protrusion is due to bowels or intestines, you should instruct the patient to hold himself still and stand upright, then draw a line with ink around the entire umbilicus. Then, instruct him to lie on his back before you.  Then, make a wide incision around the umbilicus, at the marked spot.  Then, lift the center of the tumor upwards with a large hook.  Then, bind the incision with a strong thread or strong silk thread, firmly.
The knot of the bandage should be a slip-knot.  Then, open the center of the constricted tumor above the bandage, and insert your index finger and search for the intestines.  If you find that the bandage has caught the intestines, loosen the slip-knot and push the intestines back into the abdomen. If it is a fistula, extend it with a hook.
And cut off the excess.  If an artery or vein obstructs you, tie it off firmly, then return to your work.  Take two needles, thread them with strong threads, and insert the two needles into the incision you made around the tumor, penetrating them firmly.  Then, tighten the tumor at four locations using the needles.
And if you wish, remove the needles and leave the area until the tightened flesh rots and falls off of its own accord, or cut it if it has thinned and rotted.  Then, treat it with the appropriate ointments until it heals. If the umbilical protrusion is from flesh that has grown there.
Or if it is from moisture, you should cauterize the tumor as we said, and remove the blood or moisture that you find there, then treat it with what will heal the area.
Chapter Fifty-three.
On the treatment of cancer.
We have previously mentioned the types of cancer in the classification, and how to treat it with medications, and warned against treating it with iron, lest it ulcerate.  We have also described cancer originating in the uterus and warned against treating it.
The ancients stated that if the cancer is in a location where it can be completely excised, like cancer in the breast or thigh, and similar members that allow its complete removal, especially if it is small and in its early stages, then it should be removed completely. However, if it is advanced and large, it should not be approached.
I have not been able to cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone before me achieve this.  The procedure, if the cancer is accessible as we have described, is to proceed with cauterization with the black cautery, several times, and then to excise it if there is apparent fullness in the veins.
Then apply cauterization in a way that allows for effective treatment, then insert the appropriate forceps into the cancer, and remove it from every side along with the skin, completely, until no part of its roots remain.  Allow the blood to flow without quickly stopping it.
Instead, squeeze the affected areas and remove all the thick blood with your hand or whatever tools are available.  If during the process, a major hemorrhage occurs from a severed artery or vein, constrict the vein until the bleeding stops, then treat it with the usual methods until it heals.
Chapter Forty-five.
On the treatment of carbuncles.
We have previously discussed the various types of dropsy, how water collects, the symptoms of each type, and its treatment with medications.  Only the scrofulous type of dropsy is treated with iron.  The phlegmatic and fleshy types should never be treated with iron.
If, having treated this scrofulous carbuncle with medication, your treatment proves ineffective, consider whether the patient has succumbed to excessive weakness or has another ailment besides the carbuncle.
If the patient suffers from coughing, diarrhea, or similar ailments, avoid treating it with iron, as this is risky. If you see a patient of robust constitution, free from illnesses besides the carbuncle alone, neither a child nor an old person, proceed by having the patient stand before you. A servant behind him should press his abdomen with their hands, pushing the water downwards towards the pubic area.  Then, take a pointed lancet in this manner:
Having two limited ends, long, and pointed like a lancet, but with a slight curve, so as not to penetrate the intestines during use, and cause injury.  Then, consider if the carbuncle originates from the intestines.
If the carbuncle originates from a problem with the liver, the incision should be three finger-widths to the left of the navel, below it and above the pubic area. If it arises from a problem with the spleen, the incision should be three finger-widths to the right of the navel.
Also, the incision should not be on the side the patient wants to lie on, lest the fluids flow to that weakened spot.  Then, pierce the entire skin with the instrument and insert the instrument into the incision, raising your hand.
With the lancet, separate the skin from the peritoneum, as if you were peeling it back.  The amount peeled back should be about the size of a fingernail, or similar.  Then pierce the peritoneum until the lancet reaches an empty space, the location of the fluid.  Then extract the fluid and insert the instrument of this form into the hole.
It is like a thin tube made of silver, copper, or smooth polished leather.  It has a small hole at the bottom and three holes on the sides, two on one side and one on the other, as you see.
Its tip may have a pointed end, like the nib of a pen, of this form.
At its upper end there is a ring; when the instrument reaches the water, it will drain from it onto the instrument immediately.  Empty the water from it in a moderate amount, because if you empty more than necessary at once, the patient may die from the dissolution of their vital spirit.
Or the patient may experience a near-death fainting spell.  However, empty the water according to the patient's strength and what the patient's pulse and complexion indicate, and then remove the instrument and contain the water, as it will hold immediately.
Because of the skin that grips the opening in the diaphragm, which you were told to soothe in that manner, then use the instrument again another day if you see the patient is able to tolerate it.
Also remove a small amount of water.  Do this for several days while being careful to avoid mistakes, so that only a small amount of water remains.  If you fear for the patient's health and leave a lot of water behind, then treat the patient with burial in hot sand.
A lot of sweating in the bath and sun, and enduring thirst, and treating with dried medications until he is healed, and cauterize the stomach, liver, and spleen after removing the water as described before.

On treating children born with unperforated or narrow, or misplaced, urinary openings.

Chapter Fifty-Six.
Concerning the ulcer that occurs in the prepuce, the glans, the blackness, the decay, and the adhesion of the prepuce to the glans.
This ulcer often occurs in the urethra, as a fleshy, dark bump, and can be malignant or benign.  The benign one should be lifted with a delicate hook.
Cut it completely clean, then apply a cotton swab dipped in Egyptian ointment, then treat it with palm ointment until it heals.  If the ulcer is malignant, dark in color, cauterization should be used after cutting and removing it.
If the ulcer is in the prepuce of an uncircumcised person, and some of the ulcer is inside the prepuce and some outside, the ulcer inside should be removed first.  Then, once it has healed, treat the ulcer from outside.
Because if you treat both simultaneously, you risk the prepuce becoming perforated.  This can also occur in females, with blackness and decay in the prepuce; all blackened and decaying or already decayed material should be removed.
Then apply honey with ground and sifted pomegranate rind and crimson to it. Then treat it with further remedies until it heals.  If bleeding occurs, use a crescent-shaped cautery iron in this manner:
Cauterization is beneficial in both cases, meaning bleeding and the ulcer if it becomes infected.  If the foreskin is entirely destroyed at some point, a lead tube should be inserted into the urethra for the patient to urinate through.  As for adhesion of the foreskin to the glans...
This adhesion occurs in those whose foreskin was healthy and who did not undergo circumcision, or it may result from a wound or a tumor.  The foreskin should be separated with a sharp scalpel until the ligaments are loosened and the glans is freed on all sides.
If it is difficult to distinguish it through examination, a portion of the glans and foreskin should be separated. The foreskin is thin, and so it might perforate quickly due to its thinness.  Then separate the foreskin and glans with a thin linen cloth soaked in cold water, to prevent further adhesion.
Then treat with a styptic drink until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-seven.
In the purification of boys and the treatment of their errors.
Circumcision is nothing other than the separation of connection, like other surgical procedures. However, since it is of our own doing and specifically used in boys, we must establish the best method of procedure.
And the easiest way to safety, I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books, because it was not used in their laws; rather, it is something we have adopted.
By experience, for I found the majority of artisans and barbers using purification with a knife and scissors, and using a scalpel, tying with thread, and cutting with a nail. I have tested all these methods.
Therefore, I found no better than purification with scissors and tying with thread, because purification with a knife often causes the skin to adhere, as the foreskin has two layers, and perhaps the outer layer is cut while the inner layer remains.
One would be forced to make another cut and suffer further pain.  Purification with a scalpel does not guarantee avoiding cutting the urethral opening, as it might be entered.  As for purification with a nail, the skin might tear, spoiling your work, or the boy's skin might be naturally short.
Often, they are born needing no purification. I have seen this.  As for purification with scissors and tying with thread, experience revealed its superiority to me, because the scissors have even cuts, as the blade above is like the blade below.
When you squeeze your hand with the blades in harmony, they cut at the same level and in the same time.  This makes the thread's grip like a wall to the urethral skin on all sides; there is no error at all.  The method first involves reassuring the boy.
Especially if he is somewhat intelligent, explain that you are only tying the thread to his urethra and leaving it for another day. Then, please him and delight him in any way you can, and do what he accepts mentally. Then, have him stand before you, upright.
Do not have him sit down.  Hide the scissors in your sleeve or under your foot.  Do not let the boy's eyes see them or any other instruments. Then, insert your hand into his urethra and inflate the skin and pull it upward until the head of the urethra emerges.
Then, clean it of any accumulated dirt. Then tie the marked area with a double thread. Then, a second tie slightly below it.  Hold the lower tie firmly with your thumb and index finger and cut between the two ties. Then, quickly lift the skin upward and remove the head of the urethra. Let the blood flow for a little while, as this is better and lessens urethral swelling. Then, dry it with a damp cloth.  Afterwards, sprinkle dried, burned gourd ashes on it, as this is the best I have tried, or fine sawdust, which is also excellent.
Then, quickly lift the skin upward and remove the head of the urethra. Let the blood flow for a little while, as this is better and lessens urethral swelling. Then, dry it with a damp cloth.  Afterwards, sprinkle dried, burned gourd ashes on it, as this is the best I have tried, or fine sawdust, which is also excellent. Then, apply a poultice of a hard-boiled egg in rose water, pounded with soft, good rose oil, to the wound.  Leave it on for another day, and then continue the rest of the treatment until he is healed.
This is a suitable scissor design for the procedure.
The scissors should be straight, sharp, and have a nail-like tip.  The blades should be the same length as the handles.  A common mistake in the procedure is that the skin inside may be turned over completely or partially when cutting.  Therefore, you should pull the skin out with your fingernail before the area swells.
Cut it [the skin] evenly. If you can't hold it with your fingernail, pull it with a hook and cut it.  If three days pass and the area under the head of the urethra remains swollen and inflamed, leave it until the acute swelling subsides and gently scrape it off.
Cut it as you see fit, being careful around the head of the urethra.  If some of the head of the urethra is cut, it will not harm; treat it with the poultices that we described in the article on poultices.
If more skin is cut than necessary and it pulls back, this is not very harmful. Treat it as described until it heals.
Chapter Fifty-eight.
On treating urine retention in the bladder.
Urine retention in the bladder occurs due to blockage by a stone, clotted blood, pus, or overgrown flesh, and similar obstructions.  If you treat this with the various treatments described in the classifications,
If the urine does not flow and you suspect the retention is due to a stone damaging the neck of the bladder, the patient should be placed on their knees, then have someone sit on their back, pressing the abdomen towards the back. The patient should hold themselves as best they can.
The neck of the bladder will then be forced to push the stone backward, and the urine will flow. If the urine still does not flow and the patient's condition worsens, then the extraction instrument called catheters, whose image is this, should be used.
It is made of silver, thin and smooth, hollowed out like a bird's feather tube, about a foot and a half long.  It has a gentle funnel at its head.  To draw out the urine, a doubled thread with attached wool or cotton is securely tied to one end.
The thread's end is inserted into the lower end of the catheter.  Any excess wool is trimmed with pliers so it can enter the tube and seal it like a stopper. Then the catheter is lubricated with oil, butter, or egg white, and the patient sits on a chair.
The bladder and urethra are moistened with warm oil or water, then the catheter is gently inserted into the urethra until it reaches the base of the urethra. The urethra is then pulled upward towards the navel, and the catheter is pushed into it.
Once the urethra is pulled back, the catheter is pushed inward until it is close to the anus. The penis is then tilted downward, with the catheter inside, and pushed until it reaches the bladder. The patient will feel that it has reached an empty space.
This is done in this manner because the urinary passage has curves.  By pulling the thread with the wool a little forcefully, the urine follows the wool.  Then it is removed, and the urine is drained.  The catheter is then reinserted.
Continue doing this until the bladder is emptied and the patient feels relief.
Chapter Fifty-nine.
How to inject the bladder with a syringe.
Images of suitable instruments for this purpose are made of silver or hollowed ivory.  They have a long, thin tube, and are intended to instill water and medications.  This is done with an instrument called a syringe.  This is its image:.
Made of silver or hollowed ivory, it has a long, thin tube, hollow throughout except for the end, which is solid.  There are three holes in it: two on one side and one on the other side, as you see.
The hollow part, where the projectile is, is sized to fit it snugly, without any excess.  Thus, when you pull something out of the liquid, it is pulled out.  And when you push something in, it is pushed out; just as a pump used for throwing oil in naval warfare does.
If you want to remove the moisture in the bladder, insert the end of the syringe into the moisture and pull upward on the projectile. The moisture will be drawn into the syringe. Then, insert the tip into the urethra, as described in the catheters.  Then, push the moisture with the projectile.
This moisture will reach the bladder in such a way that the patient feels it.  This is also a picture of a gentle syringe for injecting the bladder.
It is made of silver or lead. Its upper head resembles a small funnel, and below it is a groove to hold the cord. Then, you take a camel's bladder and put into it the moisture you want to inject into the bladder. Then, tie it tightly between the two grooves with a doubled thread.
And you warm that moisture a little over the fire. Then you insert the tip of the syringe into the urethra. Then firmly press down on the bladder with the moisture until the patient feels that the moisture has reached the bladder.
If you do not have a bladder, take a piece of thin material, make a circle out of it, and pierce it all around.  Then, insert a strong thread through those holes as they circle, then gather the circle together, like gathering a table after putting in it the moisture, oils, and water you want.
Then tie it to the apparatus and work with it as you worked with the bladder, pressing with your hand until the moisture reaches the bladder.
Chapter Sixty.
On removing stones.
We have previously discussed the types of stones and their treatment with medicine.  We have described the difference between stones originating in the kidneys and those in the bladder, and the signs of each. We have also discussed stones requiring manual removal and incision.
This is specifically the stone originating in the bladder, and that which develops in the urethra, and I will describe this with clear and concise explanation.
I say that stones originating in the bladder most often affect boys.  Among its signs is that the urine exits the bladder resembling thin water in its consistency.
Sand appears in it, he scratches and fiddles with his penis.  Often it hangs down, then spreads, and the anus protrudes in many of them.  It is easier to cure in boys up to fourteen years old.  It is harder in the elderly.  As for young men, it is intermediate between the two cases.
The larger the stone, the easier the treatment; the smaller the stone, the opposite is true.  If we turn to treatment, first the patient should be injected with an enema to remove all the waste in his intestines, for it may prevent the stone from being detected during examination.
Then the patient is taken by his feet and shaken downwards to cause the stone to descend into the neck of the bladder, or he jumps from a high place several times.  Then, you seat him upright before you with his hands beneath his thighs so that the entire bladder tilts downwards, and then you examine him.
And feel him from the outside. If you feel the stone in the space, immediately make an incision to reach it. If it doesn't come under your touch at all, you should smear your index finger with grease from your left hand if the patient is a boy, or your middle finger.
If he is a fully developed boy, insert it into his anus and search for the stone.  Once you locate it under your finger, slowly move it towards the neck of the bladder. Then press on it with your finger and push it out towards the area you want to incise.
You instruct a servant to squeeze the bladder with his hand, and another servant to stretch the labia upward with his right hand, and with his other hand to stretch the skin below the labia towards the site of the incision. Then take the lancet, the image of which is as follows:
Make the incision between the anus and the labia, not in the center but along the left side of the scrotum. The incision should be precisely over the stone, with your finger inside the anus, pushing the stone outward in an oblique manner, so that the incision is wide on the outside and narrow on the inside, as much as possible to facilitate the stone's removal.
Do not exert excessive force, for perhaps the finger in the anus will push the stone during the incision and it will come out without difficulty.  Be aware that some stones have corners and edges, making removal more difficult.
Some are smooth, resembling acorns and round, making removal easy; those with corners and edges require a slightly wider incision.  If removal is still not possible, one must devise a method to extract it, perhaps using a tightly gripped instrument with a rasp-like tip.
So that it grips the stone and does not slip from it, or introduce a gentle, curved instrument beneath it. If you cannot manage this, then enlarge the incision slightly. If you encounter significant bleeding, stop it with a cauterization tool.
If there are more than one stone, first push the largest one into the bladder opening, then make an incision over it, and afterward remove the smaller ones.  Do the same if there are more than two. If the stone is very large, it is unwise to make a large incision, for this could cause one of two things to happen to the patient.
Either death or permanent dribbling of urine, because the area will not heal.  However, try pushing it out, or devise a method to break it with forceps so that you may remove it in pieces.
Once you have finished your work, dress the wound with kender, sopor, shiyan, and strips, and place a wet cloth soaked in oil and a potion, or rose ointment and cold water over it to calm the hot swelling. Then, the patient should lie on their back, and do not loosen the bandage for three days.
Once it has softened, wash the area with plenty of water and oil, then treat it with the نخلي ointment and the basilikon ointment until it heals.  If excessive heat, swelling, or inflammation appears in the wound, or if blood clots in the bladder and urination is blocked, this is recognized by blood coming out with the urine.
Insert your finger into the wound and remove the blood, for if it remains, it will lead to the corruption and infection of the bladder. Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and treat each type of condition with a corresponding treatment until it heals.
Throughout the treatment, it is necessary to bind the thighs together to keep the medications applied to the area in place.  If the stone is small and is in the urethra, causing swelling and blocking urination, treat it as described previously before it causes a tear.
Often, this treatment obviated the need for incision.  I have tried this method, which involves taking a curved iron instrument, with a form like this:
A triangular-pointed, sharp iron instrument is inserted into a stick.  Then, a thread is used to tie the penis below the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.  Then, the curved iron instrument is gently inserted into the urethra until it reaches the stone itself.
Rotate the instrument on the stone, slowly, until it pierces the stone from the other side.  Urine will then flow immediately.  Then, grasp the remaining part of the stone outside the penis.  It will break apart and be expelled with the urine, and the patient will be cured.
If this treatment is not possible due to an impediment, tie a thread below the stone and another above the stone.  Then, make an incision on the stone within the penis between the two threads and remove it.
Then, loosen the threads and clean the coagulated blood that has formed in the wound.  Tying the thread below the stone was necessary to prevent it from returning to the bladder, and the other thread above to ensure that, after the stone's removal, the skin returns to its place and covers the wound when the thread is loosened.
Therefore, when tying the upper thread, you should lift the skin upwards so that it returns to its place and covers the wound, as we said.
Chapter Sixty-one.
On removing stones in women.
Stones rarely develop in women. If one of them has a stone, its treatment is difficult and impossible for many reasons. One of them is that the woman might be a virgin, and the second that you will not find a woman who allows herself to be examined by a physician.
If she is chaste or from among women with close relatives, and thirdly, that you will not find a woman who excels in this craft, especially manual work. Fourth, the location of the incision on a stone in women is far from the location of the stone itself, requiring a deep incision.
In that case, there is danger. If necessity requires it, a skilled female physician should be sought, but these are rare. If none is available, seek a virtuous and compassionate male physician or bring in a skilled midwife or a woman knowledgeable in this practice to provide some guidance.
Bring her in and instruct her to perform all the examinations you order, first, to determine if the woman is a virgin.  If so, the finger should be inserted into her vagina to search for the stone.  If found, the stone should be pressed with the finger.
In that case, instruct her to perform the incision on it.  If she is not a virgin, but a married woman, instruct the midwife to insert her finger into the woman's vagina, search for the stone, and then place her left hand on the bladder, pressing it firmly.
If she finds it, the stone should be guided from the mouth of the bladder downward to the extent of her ability, until it reaches the root of the thigh.  Then she should make an incision over the halfway point of the vagina at the root of the thigh, whichever side feels the stone in that area.
Her finger must remain on the stone, pressing it.  Make the incision small at first. Then insert a probe into that small incision.  If she feels the stone, enlarge the incision as much as needed to remove the stone.  Know that there are many types of stones.
Among them are small and large, smooth and rough, long and round, and those with branches.  Know their types so that you may recognize what you want.  If blood overwhelms you, sprinkle powdered alum on the area and hold it for an hour until the bleeding stops. Then return to your work.
Until the stone is removed. Prepare the instruments mentioned for removing stones from men, to assist you in your work. If bleeding overwhelms you, and you know from the pulse that it is from a severed artery, place a styptic on the area.
Hold it tightly with the bandages, firmly. Leave it and do not return to it. Leave the stone; do not remove it.  For perhaps the patient will perish. Then treat the wound. When the severity of the blood subsides after a few days and the area becomes infected, return to your work until the stone is removed.
Chapter Sixty-Two.
In the fissure of the hydrocele.
A hydrocele is an accumulation of moisture in the white peritoneum, which lies under the scrotum surrounding the testicle.  It may also be in a special membrane naturally formed in one area of the testicle.
Until one might think it is another testicle. It is between the scrotum and the white peritoneum which we have mentioned. This rarely occurs. This hydrocele originates from weakness affecting the female.  This substance is deposited on the female.  It might also result from a blow to the female.
This moisture comes in various colours; it may be yellow, or bloody red, or dark-greyish black, or white like water. The white water type is the most common.  The signs by which it is recognized are...
Where the water accumulates, if it is in the white peritoneum mentioned, the swelling is somewhat elongated and round, like an egg. The testicle is not visible because the moisture surrounds it from all sides.
If the moisture is in a specific membrane, the swelling will be round on one side of the testicle.
This is why a person might imagine it is another testicle. If the moisture is between the scrotum and the white peritoneum, it is palpable.  To determine the colour of the moisture, probe the swelling with the square probe whose image was previously described.  Judge its contents by the substance that comes out on contact.
If we turn to treatment with leeches, the patient should be bled if possible and if their body appears full.  Then, they should lie on their back on a slightly raised surface with many cloths beneath them.  You should sit on their left side.
You should instruct a servant to sit on their right, extending the patient's penis to one side of the scrotum and towards the flank.  Then, take a broad scalpel and cut the scrotum lengthwise from the middle, towards the pubic area.
The incision should be straight, parallel to the line dividing the scrotum in half, until it reaches the white peritoneum.  Carefully dissect it, avoiding further cutting.  Focus your dissection on the side closest to the testicle and continue as far as possible.
Then, widely dissect the water-filled peritoneum and remove all the water.  Then, separate the edges of the incision with forceps, lift the peritoneum upward, avoiding contact with the scrotum, and cut the peritoneum as much as possible, either in one piece or in sections.
Especially its thin side; if you don't thoroughly cut it, you won't be sure that the water won't return.  If the testicle protrudes from its covering during your work, then, after emptying the peritoneum, put it back in place and stitch the edges of the scrotum together.
Then treat it like any other surgical wound until healed.  If the testicle has been damaged by another ailment, you should tie off the vessels in the suspensory ligament to prevent bleeding, then detach the testicle from the ligament and remove it.  Then treat it as we have described.
If the collected fluid is in both sides, know that it is a hydrocele in both.  Incise the other side in the same manner as the first.  If you prefer to do both at once, do so.  Then, clean away the blood.
Insert wool soaked in oil or rose oil into the incisions.  Put another layer of wool soaked in a mixture of liquid and oil over the testicles and the abdominal wall.  Place folded cloths, or bandages, on top. Tie it together from above using the six-sided bandage in this manner:
Then, take two cloths, stitch them together, and fill them with fluffy wool, like this example.  Make the ends to be tied from cotton or damp wool, and secure them tightly with a belt around the waist. One end will be placed over the buttocks and toward the back.
The other end goes towards the pubic area and the other two ends go down the thighs.  The remaining two ends are placed on the hips.  Tie all of the ends together with a belt around the waist.  This hydrocele may also be treated by cauterization instead of incision with a knife.
This involves taking a gentle, heated, knife-like cautery iron to cut through the scrotal skin, exposing the white membrane containing the water. Take another cautery iron in this shape:
It resembles a Greek eye, then cauterize that membrane while it is hot until all the moisture is released.  Then, pull the membrane with forceps and scrape off the containing layer using the cautery iron, cutting it as much as possible until all of it is removed, and do not burn the testicle.
Know that this method is safer and less likely to cause bleeding, and better than incision.  If, during the incision or cauterization, you encounter a hot tumor, bleeding, or anything else, you should treat each difficulty with the appropriate remedy as described above.
If the patient is cowardly and cannot endure this procedure because of its unpleasantness, use cauterization as described; if it proves effective in accordance with the signs presented previously.
If the discharge is watery, the patient should sit on a high chair, and then cauterize the tumor below with a wide probe.  Make the cauterization lengthwise along the body, and make the opening wide enough for all the water to drain out.  Then apply cotton to the area and bandage it.
Allow the remaining water to drain, then treat it until the wound heals. If the water does not drain during cauterization, it is because the white membrane obstructs the opening of the wound, preventing the water from exiting.
In that case, you should insert a goose feather, or the instrument used to extract the water of the affected area, whose image was previously shown, or slightly enlarge the wound opening.
Know that water may return and accumulate after six months or so. If it does, cauterize it in the same way, and the patient should rest for those days.
Chapter Sixty-three.
On the tearing of fleshy tissue and its treatment.
Know that tearing of these fleshy tissues is often a perilous condition leading to death. Therefore, we recommend leaving them alone and avoiding them. I will now discuss the treatments and types of tumors in these cases.
I say that many tumors may occur in the bodies formed by the union of two parts. This occurs for many reasons, either due to excessive heat flowing to the two parts, or due to blows. The tumor's color is like the color of the body, and it does not contain water.
The tumor is firm, and it might be stony, the color of mud, and without sensation. It might be from the entanglement and swelling of the arteries, as previously mentioned, or from the swelling of the veins. If it is from the swelling of the arteries, this is recognized by the dispersion of the tumor.
If you press it with your fingers, it should not be treated at all. However, if it is from the swelling of the veins, nothing will disperse when pressed with the fingers. This type can tear, and tear through the rest of the flesh.
One should cut the scrotum's skin, then pull the testicle upward, remove it from the white peritoneum, and free the appendices from the vessels.  Tie the vessels and cut the appendix after removing it from all sides of the testicle. If the testicle has adhered to the growing tissues.
Then, the testicle should be removed and cut. If the adhesion is between parts of the peritoneum or between vessels, then all of this adhesion should be freed and cut in a circular fashion.
If the flesh growth is in the area of adhesion, which is behind, then all of it should be cut and the testicle removed, as we said. Once your work is done, dress the wound with moist wool, rose-oil and drink, then treat it with other remedies until it heals.
The Sixty-fourth Chapter.
On the treatment of dropsy with dropsy.
Dropsy is a swelling twisted and knotted, resembling a bunch of grapes, with relaxation of the limbs.  It hinders the afflicted person's movement, exercise, and walking. In treating this condition, the remedy is similar to what was previously mentioned.
However, one must follow the methods of the ancients.  I say that the afflicted person should sit on a high stool, then pull the limbs downwards, then grasp the scrotum with your fingers, near the vessels close to the penis.
A servant of yours should hold it and stretch it forcefully.  Then, with a broad, sharp scalpel, make an oblique incision next to the vessels until the vessels are exposed.  Then, dissect from all sides as I described to you in dissecting the arteries in the temples.  Then, insert a needle with a double thread into them.
Tie it at the first points where the dropsy appeared, and also at the last.  Then, slit it in the middle with a vertical incision along the body, and remove the accumulated foul and corrupt moisture.
Then treat the wound like other wounds that require time to heal so the vessels that nourish the limb fall away.  This is acceptable. If the dropsy has affected all the vessels, then the limb, including the vessels, must be removed so that the testicle does not lose its nourishment. Otherwise, the vessels will be cut, it will wilt, and no use can be derived from it.
Chapter Sixty-five.
On the treatment of intestinal dropsy.
This dropsy occurs when a rupture appears in the peritoneum extending over the abdomen, near the groin, causing the intestines to protrude into one of the testicles.
This hernia is either from a rupture in the peritoneum or from its extension. These two types occur due to many causes, such as a blow, a leap, a scream, or lifting a heavy object, and the like.  If it is from an extension of the peritoneum, it will develop gradually over a long period.
It does not occur suddenly.  The swelling is flat, extending inward because the peritoneum compresses the intestines.  If it is from a rupture in the peritoneum, it is initially accompanied by intense pain, and the swelling is clearly different beneath the skin nearby.
This is due to the intestines protruding out of the peritoneum.  Pus may also exit with the intestines, making this dropsy intestinal and purulent, and it may also be accompanied by wind.  Mucus may also run into the intestines and become trapped there, causing the patient's demise.
Because it causes intense pain and gurgling, especially when pressed.  Treating these types of ailments with iron is dangerous; therefore, one must avoid it.  The procedure is to tell the patient to push the intestines back into their abdomen with their hand, if possible to return.
Then, he lies on his back with you at his side, and his legs are raised.  Then, the skin around the groin is pulled upward and the entire skin of the scrotum is slit lengthwise.  Then, hooks are inserted into the edges of the slit, as much as needed to open the slit.
The slit should be large enough to allow the testicle to come out.  Then, the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin is stripped away until the white, tough peritoneum is exposed on all sides.  Then, insert your index finger into the area next to the testicle.
Between the white peritoneum beneath the testicular skin and the second peritoneum, release the adhesions behind the testicle.  Then, with the right hand, pull inward on the scrotal skin, while with the left hand, pull the white peritoneum upward and lift the testicle with the peritoneum toward the slit.
You instruct the attendant to pull the testicle upward, and you completely release the adhesions behind it.  Examine with your finger to make sure there is no twisted bowel within the tough white peritoneum.  If you find any, push it back into the abdomen below.
Then, take a needle with a thick thread, twisted from ten strands, and insert it at the edge of the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin near the slit.  Then, cut the ends of the thread until there are four strands. Then, interweave some strands together in a firm manner and tie the peritoneum, which we said was beneath the scrotal skin, tightly on both sides.  Then, also wrap the ends of the threads and tie them tightly, so that nothing of the vessels nourishing it can reach it. So that a hot tumor does not arise from this, and also make a second ligature outside the first ligature, a distance of less than two fingers. After these two ligatures, leave a portion of the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin the size of a finger.
So that a hot tumor does not arise from this, and also make a second ligature outside the first ligature, a distance of less than two fingers. After these two ligatures, leave a portion of the peritoneum beneath the scrotal skin the size of a finger. Cut the rest of it entirely around, and remove the testicle with it.  Then, make an incision below the scrotal skin, one that bleeds and discharges as described previously. Then, use wool soaked in oil and place it in the wound, and use the ligature that we described.
The white peritoneum, which we mentioned after cutting it, may be cauterized cautiously to prevent bleeding, as this often occurs. Leave the ligature until it falls off on its own; if it is slow to fall off, soak it in hot water and apply something to cause perspiration, so that it falls off.
Then treat the wound with all other surgical treatments until it heals.
Chapter Sixty-six.
In this type of gaseous colic, I have seen no one dare treat it with iron.  The ancients described making it as we described for colic with a pouch, by tying the vessels gently below the incision.
Then it is incised in the center, and the swelling is treated to open it, so that the vessels fall out.  The wound is treated as we mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-six.
In the hernia that occurs in the groin.
The hernia in the groin may appear as we said, bulging the area but not dragging any intestines down into the scrotum.  If it does descend, it is slight and always returns.
However, if time passes, the tear in the peritoneum increases, so that the intestines and omentum descend into the scrotum. This is a result of the peritoneum's extension in the groin, as we said. This is because the peritoneum extends, then relaxes and bulges the area, and its treatment is by cauterization, as previously described.
And it may be treated with a cautery as follows: the patient lies on their back before you, then you cut the swollen bulging hernia with a transverse cut about the width of three fingers, then you cauterize the peritoneum under the skin.
Until the white peritoneum beneath is exposed. Then you take a thread, place it on the protruding area of the peritoneum, and press it deep into the abdomen.  Then you stitch the two protruding points on the thread of peritoneum, and sew one to the other.
Then pull out the thread, and do not cut the peritoneum at all, nor touch the testicle or anything else, as I instructed you in treating intestinal hernia. Then treat it with the treatment for surgery.
If the threads are severed, cleanse and close the wound until it heals, for the peritoneum contracts and the hernia bulge does not increase.
And this method is the best in this situation, because it is closer to safety.
Chapter Sixty-Eight.
On the relaxation of the scrotum.
The scrotum often relaxes in some people without the underlying tissues relaxing, making its appearance unsightly.  Therefore, those wishing to treat it should lay the patient on their back.
Cut all the relaxed skin away from the tough skin, then bring the edges together.  If you prefer, first stitch the excess relaxed skin with three or four stitches, secure the stitches, then cut between the stitches.
Then treat it with the usual treatment for other surgical wounds until it heals and the stitches fall out.
Chapter sixty-nine.
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our religion.
For this reason, I should not mention it in this book, but I have mentioned it for two reasons: one, so that it will be part of the physician's knowledge if questioned about it, and to understand the treatment of those who have suffered from it; and the other, because we often need to castrate certain animals for our own benefit.
Like sheep, oxen, and cats, and similar animals, I say that castration can be done in two ways: either by crushing or by cutting and dividing. The method by crushing involves placing the animal in hot water until its testicles relax, soften and hang down.
Then you crush them with your hand until they dissolve and are no longer distinct to the touch.  As for castration by cutting and dividing, one must hold the animal and use the left hand to squeeze the skin of its testicles, then tie the suspensory ligaments and cut each testicle once.
Until the testicles are exposed, then cut them, after removing the coverings, leaving no peritoneum except the thin membrane covering the vessels.  This method of castration is better than that by crushing.
For crushing might leave some of the testicles, causing the animal to desire mating.  Then treat the wound until it heals.
Seventy-first Chapter.
On the treatment of hermaphrodites.
Hermaphrodites in men come in two types. One appears as a space or a patch of skin between the testicles resembling a woman's vulva, with hair, and urine may flow from that patch of skin.
In women, there is only one type, located above the vulva on the pubis, like small, protruding man's testicles. One of them is like a man's penis, and both together resemble the female parts. Treatment for all three types.
In the two types of males and the one type of females, the excess flesh must be cut away until the mark disappears, then treated with other surgical procedures until healed.  However, in the second type of male from whom urine flows from the skin of the testicles, there is no work to be done and no cure at all.
Chapter Seventy-one.
On cutting the penis and protruding flesh from the vulva of women.
The clitoris may increase in size beyond the natural state, becoming discoloured and unattractive.  In some women, it may enlarge to the point of resembling a penis, and making intercourse possible.
One should hold the excess clitoris with one's hand or forceps and cut it, without excessive cutting, especially not deep into the root, lest it cause bleeding.
Then treat it with surgical remedies until it heals.  As for the growing flesh, it is flesh that grows in the vaginal opening until it fills it.
And it may protrude outwards, resembling a tail. Therefore, some of the ancients called it the "tail disease".  It should be cut, like the clitoris, and treated until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-two.
Concerning the treatment of prolapse.
Prolapse is when a woman's vulva is not perforated or the perforation is small, either naturally occurring at birth or accidentally.  The accidental type arises from a prior condition and may be from excess overgrown flesh.
Or from thin or thick peritoneum, located either deep within the uterus, or along its sides, or at the top or bottom. It prevents intercourse, childbirth, and possibly menstruation.  A midwife can diagnose this by observation.
If the obstruction is visible and close, but if not visible, she should investigate with her fingers or a probe. If the obstruction is from thin peritoneum and close to the labia, then proceed to pierce it by forming folds on the labia, and then place your thumbs of both hands on them.
And the woman should be on her back, with her legs spread. Then, forcefully spread the labia until the thin peritoneum is pierced and the obstruction opens.  Then, take wool, soak it in oil, and apply it to the area. Have intercourse with the woman daily to prevent the area from closing up again.
If the peritoneum is thick and dense, then it should be cut with a wide scalpel resembling a thin, flat blade. If the obstruction is from overgrown flesh, then use forceps to lift it and cut it.  Have with you medications to stop bleeding without cauterization, such as qaqia, shiyan, and frankincense mixed with egg white.
Then use a wide lead tube so that the wound does not heal quickly. Hold it for days, and use a thread of dry linen. Then treat it with the general treatment for abscesses until it heals.  Another growth of flesh may appear in the uterus, and it should be cut in the same way.
Unless it is a cancerous tumor, the cancerous tumor that is in the uterus should not be treated with iron at all.
Chapter Seventy-three.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red pustules that appear in the vulva of women.
Hemorrhoids are the swelling of the veins' openings, causing a constant flow of copious blood.  When hemorrhoids progress, they become warts.  Their types and signs have been discussed in the classification, and we will describe them here.
Regarding which hemorrhoids and warts respond to treatment and which do not, I say that if hemorrhoids and warts are deep within the uterus and do not present themselves to touch, then there is no iron-based treatment for them.  Those that are on the mouth of the uterus and are perceptible to touch are the ones that are treated.
The woman should enter a cool room, then the warts should be scraped with a lancet or a rough cloth and cut from their roots.  After bleeding from one of the blood-stopping devices,  apply [something] without causing a prick.
Like acacia, dates, frankincense, and similar things. Then raise her legs to the wall for an hour, then lower them into cold water. If the bleeding continues, seat her in a decoction of sumac, pomegranate peels, and gallnuts, etc. Then take barley flour, knead it with honey and vinegar.
Apply it as a poultice to her back. If the bleeding stops, then cease. Otherwise, apply leeches to the back, breasts, and chest, without restriction. Then take wool, dip it in the juice of turpentine, or the juice of comfrey, or the juice of the branches of elder, with gallnut drink, and apply it to the area.
If the swelling subsides, treat the area with ointment until it heals.  As for the red sore, it resembles the heads of warts, rough in appearance; it should be cut as much as possible as described for warts and treated until it heals.
Chapter seventy-four.
Concerning the treatment of abscesses occurring in the uterus.
Many types of tumors, such as cancer, hard tumors, ulcers, wasting diseases, consuming diseases, polyps, hemorrhoids, fissures, warts, and hot tumors, may occur in the uterus.  All of these diseases and their types and symptoms have been discussed in the classification.
Therefore, in this treatise, we must describe the hot tumor in the uterus, if it is a tumor that has accumulated for a period.  How should it be treated with iron?  One should observe; if the tumor's pain is severe at the beginning, accompanied by throbbing, inflammation, and fever...
And if you see it red, and if touching it causes pain, do not hasten to treat it with iron.  Treat it with methods that promote maturation.  Once these symptoms subside, then the woman should sit on a stool with two seats, leaning back.
And she should draw her legs towards the lower abdomen, with her thighs spread apart, and her arms placed below her knees, tied together with an appropriate bandage.
Then, the midwife should sit on the right side and use the instrument for opening the cervix, the image of which will be shown later.  When using this instrument, one must estimate the depth of the woman's uterus, so that what enters the instrument does not exceed the depth of the uterus.
Otherwise, the patient will be harmed. If the instrument is deeper than the uterus, a piece should be placed on the edge of the cervix to prevent the entire instrument from entering the uterine cavity. The spiral that runs within the instrument should be positioned from above.
While you hold the instrument, the attendant rotates the spiral until the cervix opens.  If the abscess is visible and soft when touched, it should be incised at its head with a wide scalpel.  Once all the matter has been discharged.
A soft wick soaked in rose oil or green oil with some astringent should be placed in the wound. The wick should be placed externally from the incision in the cervix, and pure wool soaked in boiled baker's water should be placed externally on the uterus and pubis.
Then, it should be treated with the appropriate ointment for two days until healed.
The uterus and wound may be washed by injecting water and honey, then water boiled with the root of wormwood or long-leaved wormwood.  Then return to the ointment.  This is if the abscess is hidden inside the uterus.
Iron should not be used for treatment; instead, the treatment should be as described in the division.
Chapter seventy-five.
On instructing midwives on how to treat living fetuses that emerge in an unnatural form.
The midwife must first know the normal form of childbirth.  Signs include the woman moving downwards, desiring to smell the air, and finding the afterbirth less burdensome.
And if the fetus emerges quickly, know that this is normal childbirth, with the head leading and the placenta either present or hanging from the umbilical cord. If you see these signs, then you should press her abdomen so that the fetus is expelled quickly.
If the head descends, the placenta will descend with it, and it is cleaned well of such impurities. Any birth other than this is abnormal and reprehensible, as the fetus may emerge on its feet, with its hands or feet emerging before the head.
And one hand or one foot, or its head and hand, or it emerges folded, or perhaps turned upside down, and similar abnormal forms.  The midwife should be skilled and gentle with all these things and avoid mistakes.
And I am describing each of these forms and how to handle each.  If the fetus emerges head-first, the normal way, and the delivery is difficult and painful for the woman, and you see her strength weakening, then have her sit on a chair and order women to hold her.
And bind her uterus with boiled rue and moist ointments.  Then the midwife should insert a small scalpel between her fingers to cut the placenta, or cut it with her fingernail, until the moisture within flows out. Press the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, the woman should be injected with rue juice and sherj ointment.  Then, after the injection, she should be made to cough and be sneezed at with a kunds.  Hold her mouth and nose, and the fetus will emerge shortly. If the fetus's hands emerge first, gently return them slowly.
If the hands do not enter, place the woman on a raised platform and raise her legs. Shake the platform on the ground, and the woman holds on so as not to fall during the shaking. If the hands do not enter and the fetus dies, cut them off and pull out the rest of the fetus.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and pull them gently, and it will emerge.  If the fetus emerges on its feet, push its feet upwards, then turn the fetus slowly until it is aligned, then take one of its feet and extend it slowly.
If they emerge to the thighs, instruct her to cough and sneeze her with a kunds, and it will emerge. If it does not emerge as described, gently return the fetus bit by bit until it is in its natural form. It will then emerge smoothly. If it resists all the methods described.
Take juice of hyssop, rue juice, sherj ointment, and dissolved myrrh.  Grind all well in a mortar.  Then apply this to the woman's vagina and lower abdomen.  Then seat her in lukewarm water until it reaches the sharafs.
If you see that it has softened below, make her a pessary of myrrh and have her tolerate it. If she holds the pessary for an hour, seat her on a chair, then sneeze her, and close her mouth and nose, and gently squeeze her lower abdomen.  The fetus will emerge at once.  If the fetus emerges on its knees and hands...
If it emerges in this way, gently insert your hands bit by bit.  Then, have the woman lie on her back, her pelvis hanging downwards as she lies on a bed, and have her hold her hands together.  Then, gently press on her upper abdomen. Then remove the pillows from beneath her legs so that her lower body is supported. If the fetus does not emerge, grasp both her legs and move them vigorously.  Then press on the sharafs gradually until the fetus ascends. Then, the midwife inserts her hand and shapes the fetus gradually.
And instruct the woman to strain until the fetus emerges.  The fetus emerges transversely, with one arm hanging down.  Try to straighten the arm. If unable to straighten it, make the woman stand and have her walk. If she cannot walk, have her lie down.
Shake her legs vigorously. Then use the poultice made of saliva that I described. If a discharge occurs, seat her on a chair. Then work to straighten the arm and shape the fetus into its natural form. Make the woman sneeze and instruct her to strain until it emerges.
If the fetus emerges on its back, arms outstretched, and face towards its mother's back, the midwife should grasp the fetus's arm and gently turn and straighten it. Once straightened, apply the grease and saliva as described previously to its forehead.
Then instruct her to move slowly.  When she does, seat her on a chair and instruct her to lean to the right side. Then, instruct her to strain, and make her sneeze with the kands.  The fetus will emerge, positioned upright on its side.
If the fetus descends into the cervix with the placenta in this manner, slit the placenta with your fingernail to release the moisture. If it is attached to the fetus's face or neck, cut it from the umbilical cord so that it doesn't suffocate and die.  Then push the fetus inside until it is in the proper position.
Tell the woman to strain repeatedly; this will cause easy delivery.  Concerning the birth of twins or multiple fetuses, know that twins often are born, and three or four may be born and survive, but this is rare.  Five is unnatural and will not survive at all.
And if they are numerous, they are enclosed by one placenta.  A partition separates them, attached to the umbilical cord of each.  They may emerge like a single fetus, easily and naturally, or they may emerge in a manner described as abnormal.
If some of them present difficulties during delivery, and become in any of the abnormal forms mentioned, treat them as described.  Let the midwife be gentle, act with care and patience, and use any means at her disposal to achieve safety.
Fetuses that form within the womb and then miscarry may number one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, or more than ten.  I have heard reports of a woman who miscarried seven and another who miscarried fifteen, all of whom had formed within the womb.
By the estimation of the all-knowing and all-powerful, all this should be within the knowledge of the midwife, for such a case might occur to her one day, and she should be prepared to address it properly.
Chapter Sixty-Seven.
In the removal of the dead fetus.
If you have treated the dead fetus with the methods of treatment described in the division and it does not come out, and your actions necessitate manual intervention, then you must consider the following. If the woman is strong and has no symptoms that raise concern for her well-being or risk of harm, the woman should lie on her back on a bed.
Her head should be tilted downward, and her legs raised. A servant should hold her from both sides or tie her to the bed to prevent her body from moving when the fetus is being pulled out.
Then, moisten the mouth of her uterus with moist ointments, along with the saliva of a *khatmi* plant, and fenugreek and flax seeds.  The midwife should then anoint her hand with these ointments and saliva.  Then gently insert it into the uterus.  She should then locate a place to insert the hooks in the fetus and the appropriate locations for this.
If the fetus is positioned to descend head-first, insert the hooks into its eyes, nape of the neck, mouth, palate, under the chin, collarbone, near the ribs, or under the collarbones.
If it is descending feet-first, then in the pubic area and the middle ribs.  The hook should be held in the right hand, its curve placed between the fingers of the left hand.  The hand should be inserted gently and the hook inserted into one of those locations mentioned.
Until it reaches an empty space.  Then, insert another or a third hook if needed to ensure the pull is even and not biased towards one side.  Then, the pull should be steady and even, and not merely in a straight line, but moved in all directions.
So that it is easy to remove, as is done with a tooth when extracting it.  Between these actions, the pull should be relaxed. If it is stuck in one direction, the midwife should anoint some fingers with ointment and insert them to turn what is stuck.  If some part of the fetus comes out, the hooks should be moved to other locations.
She should do this a little higher, until the entire fetus is removed. If a hand comes out before the rest and cannot be pushed back due to compression, then a cloth should be wrapped around it, and it should be pulled until the whole hand is out, then it should be cut.
Either at the wrist joint or the elbow.  The same should be done for the other hand and the feet. If the fetal head is large and faces pressure during delivery or has accumulated water in its head, then a pointed lancet should be inserted between the fingers.
Using it, the head is opened to release the water, or it is squeezed with the instrument called a *mashdakh*, which is shown with other instruments in the chapter following this one.
Likewise, if the fetus has a large head naturally, the skull should be opened or squeezed with the *mashdakh*, as we said, then the bones should be extracted with forceps. If the head comes out and is compressed at the collarbone, the chest should be opened to release the moisture within.
Then the chest will contract. If the chest does not contract and the delivery is not possible, cut as much as you can. If the lower abdomen is swollen or contains tumors, it should be incised to allow the moisture to drain.
If the fetus is on its feet, pulling it and positioning it towards the opening of the uterus is easy. If it is compressed against the abdomen or chest, it should be pulled with a cloth wrapped around your hand, and the abdomen or chest should be opened to drain what is inside.
If all other organs are removed and the head is retracted and stuck, the midwife should insert her left hand. If the opening of the uterus is open, insert the hand into the cervix, locate the head, and pull it with the fingers towards the opening of the uterus. Then insert a hook or two hooks and pull it out with them.
If the opening of the uterus has contracted due to a hot swelling, it should not be forced. Instead, moist, oily substances should be applied, compresses used, and the patient should sit in relaxing, moist water.
If the fetus is on its side, if possible, it should be positioned as described for a living fetus. If this is not possible, it should be cut and removed.  Nothing of the placenta should be left inside at all.  Once you are finished with your treatment, use the remedies mentioned for hot tumors that occur in the uterus.
If bleeding occurs, she should descend into constricting water and use other treatments. I have observed a woman who conceived, and the fetus died within her, and then she conceived again after that.
Then the other fetus also died.  After a long time, a tumor appeared on her vulva and swelled until it opened, discharging pus. I was called to treat her and treated her for a long time.  The wound did not close, so I applied some potent, drawing ointments to it.
Then a bone came out of the area, and after several days another bone came out.  I was astonished by this, as the abdomen is not a place where bones are found.  I reasoned that these were bones from the dead fetus.  I probed the wound and removed many bones from the skull of the fetus.
I continued this process until I removed many bones, and the woman was in the best of health.  She lived like that for a long time, with a small amount of pus discharging from the area.  I have presented this rare case here.
Because it contains knowledge and assistance for what the skilled physician attempts to achieve through his hands in treatment.
Chapter Seventy-Seven.
It contains images of the instruments needed for removing the fetus.
Image of a spiral for opening the cervix.
This binding, by which books are sewn together, has two spirals at the ends of the wooden boards.  However, this spiral should be finer than the binding, and made either from ebony or boxwood. The width of each board should be two fingerbreadths.
Their width is approximately one finger, and their length one and a half spans. In the middle of the boards, there are two pegs made of the same wood, secured to them.  Their length is half a span, or slightly more, and their width approximately two fingers, or slightly more.  These two pegs.
They are the ones that enter the cervix to open it when you rotate the spirals.  Image of another similar instrument, finer and lighter.
Made of ebony or boxwood, shaped like tongs, but with two pegs at each end, as you see. The length of each peg is about a span, and the width two fingers.  If you wish to open the cervix with it...
Place the woman on a bed, with her legs spread apart. Then insert these two pegs, pointed, into the cervix.  Hold the end of the instrument between her legs, below, then open your hand with the instrument, as you would with tongs, to whatever extent you want to open the cervix. So that the midwife may achieve her goal. Image of another spiral mentioned by the ancients.
Image of the instrument used to expel the fetus.
Image of the instrument used to crush the fetal head.
It resembles scissors, with teeth at the end, as you see.  It can also be made rectangular, like tongs, in this image, as you see. It has teeth like saw teeth, with which to cut and sever.
