Image of two wide scalpels for cutting the fetus.
All these tools, the more types there are and the more prepared they are by the craftsman, the quicker his work and the higher his reputation among people.  Do not scorn any tool for being in your possession, as you may need it.
Chapter seventy-eight.
In the removal of the placenta.
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, you should instruct the woman to hold herself, then make her sneeze with a cupping instrument, and press your hand on her mouth and nostrils.  If it comes out this way, do so; otherwise, use a certain amount [of another method].
Pierce a hole in the cover, place herbs that open the cervix in it, such as fumitory, dill, fennel, chamomile, rue, and agrimony, all or some of these.  Soak them in water, place on the fire, and then put a silver tube on the hole in the cover's lid, and the other end in the cervix.
Hold it until the steam reaches the bottom of the uterus, then make her sneeze as we said; the placenta will then come out quickly.  If it remains after this treatment and does not come out, instruct the midwife to immerse her left hand in the fat of a shrew or the saliva of a viper.
Then, she should insert it into the vagina and search for the placenta. If she finds it, she should grasp it and pull it out slowly, little by little. If it is stuck deep inside the uterus, insert the hand as described until, when she finds the placenta, she gently pulls it out little by little.
So that the uterus does not fall with excessive pulling, but rather it should be gently moved to the sides, right and left, and then the pulling should be increased in amount.  Then it will yield and free itself from adhesion.  If the cervix is closed, we have described the treatment with sneezing, the pot treatment, and herbs.
If it does not come out with all the methods we described, then be careful not to use force when pulling it again.  Instead, tie what has come out of it to the woman's thigh, then inject her with the four-part ointment, as it will cause it to decay after a few days.
It will dissolve and come out, but if it rots, a foul odor will rise from it to the stomach and head, and this will harm the patient.  Therefore, the appropriate incense should be used.
Therefore, some of the ancients tried the incense of the wood and dried figs, an image of the device used to fumigate the woman when menses and placenta are retained, and similar cases.
It is made of lead, resembling a funnel, or made of copper.  The narrow end is placed in the vagina and the wide end over the fire.  The incense is placed on the embers; it is held until the incense is gone and then another is replaced.
Chapter Seventy-nine.
In the treatment of the unperforated anus.
Many boys are born with unperforated anuses, often blocked by a thin membrane.  The midwife should pierce this membrane with her finger, or, failing that, incise it with a sharp scalpel, being careful not to touch the muscle.
Then, place wool soaked in wine and oil on it, and treat it with ointments until it heals.  If you fear it will close again, place a lead tube in the opening for many days, and remove it when the child wishes to defecate.
A blockage of the anus may also arise from a wound abscess or tumor.  One must incise this abscess and then treat it with the remedies mentioned, and with cauterization.
Eighty-third chapter.
On the treatment of warts that appear on the lower part of the body.
Warts that appear on the lower part are a thickening and hardening that occurs near the anus, either externally or in a space on one side.  There may be one or more warts.
If this thickening persists, it may open and discharge a whitish watery fluid or thin pus.  Some of these warts may penetrate into the rectum and intestines; others may not.  Penetrated warts may be identified by what is expelled with stool and flatulence when using the enema.
And possibly worms may be expelled from them.  Some warts, if in a space, may penetrate into the bladder and urethra.  Some may also penetrate into the hip joint and the tailbone.  The penetrated wart that is in the rectum is also distinguishable from one that isn't.
Insert your index finger into the rectum and introduce a thin probe of copper or iron into the wart. If there is no deviation in the wart, do so. If there is a deviation, insert a thin lead probe or a horsehair.
Until you feel the probe or hair in your finger. If you feel nothing at all, and no stool, flatulence, or worms emerge from the opening, as we have said, know that it is not penetrated.  Proceed with treatment.
If the wart penetrates the bladder or urethra, its sign is the discharge of urine from it and the inability of the affected area to heal with medication.  If it penetrates the hip joint or tailbone, the sign is the probe's reaching it.
If there is no deviation toward the bone, and pain is present in the area, and pus constantly drains from it, and no treatment is effective and it does not heal with ointment, then these penetrated warts have no cure.
Treatment is futile for those ignorant physicians who attempt it.  As for those that are not penetrated and are not chronic, healing is anticipated by cauterization, as I describe and have tested.  The patient should lie on their back before you.
Raise the patient's legs, and tilt their thighs towards their abdomen. Then, if the wart has a deviation, insert the copper or lead probe until you know where the probe ends.  If the patient feels it near the buttock, you should then insert your index finger into the buttock.
If you feel the probe has penetrated your finger itself, clearly exposed without feeling any membrane or flesh between your finger and the probe, then know for certain that it is penetrated.  Do not exhaust yourself on it, as there is no cure for it, as we said.
They say that it heals in some individuals rarely.  A useful treatment is to heat a thin cautery iron according to the size of the wart, as mentioned before, and insert it into the wart until it reaches the vicinity of the buttock. Then repeat this two or three times.
So that you know that all the excess, hardened flesh, resembling a bird's feather tube, has been burned. Then treat it with a thread soaked in clarified butter until the burned flesh comes out, then treat it with healing ointments.  If he heals.
Otherwise, it will never heal with any other treatment.  If you insert the probe and it does not reach your finger in the buttock, and there is a thick layer of flesh or membrane between it and the probe, and you see the wart just below the skin's surface, then cut the skin from the beginning of the wart. As you are passing through the incision with the probe inside the wart, continue the incision until you reach where the tip of the probe ends. The probe will be released and fall out. Then clean away the hardened flesh, resembling a bird's feather tube, leaving nothing of it behind.
Then treat it with healing ointments until it heals. If bleeding hinders your work in cutting away that flesh, the best and quickest remedy is to cauterize it with fire or a sharp remedy, because cauterization with fire combines two beneficial effects: burning away the excess flesh.
And cut away the blood and dry the moisture. Then treat it with a thread soaked in clarified butter or pulverized sulfur mixed with oil until the area becomes suppurative and the burned flesh comes out in the pus. Then treat the wound with healing ointments that promote the growth of firm flesh.
These are the remedies that have a binding and drying effect. If it is healed, it is cured, and there is no need to return.  If the wart extends deep into the buttock and is far from the surface of the body, then insert your finger into the buttock and search with it.
If you feel the probe, and between you and it there is a barrier of peritoneum or flesh, and it is close to penetration, then the only action is through hope and anticipation.  This means you should use one of three approaches: either cauterize it, as we said.
Or else, you should slit it until you reach its bottom to be able to insert the thread and treatment from close proximity.  Do not proceed with the slitting excessively, so as not to cut the muscle surrounding the buttock, causing involuntary bowel movements in the afflicted person. Then treat it as previously described. Perhaps it will heal as we said.
Or else, you penetrate that barrier and pierce it after you have slit it close to the buttock, as we said, either with a probe or another sharp instrument. Then thoroughly clean all the congested flesh in the slit to the best of your ability.
Then close the entire wound, including the slit and the upper opening, until it is sealed, and the wart remains open inside the buttock. This will be less painful for the patient.  The wart may heal in this way.
If, when inserting the probe into the wart, it is located on the side of the buttock near the surface of the body, along with the skin and the rim of the buttock, then use a probe with a pierced tip, like a cobbler's awl, in this manner:
And thread a twisted thread of five threads, or thereabouts, through it.  Then insert the probe with the thread into the wart until you reach its bottom. If it is perforated in the edge of the buttock from the inside, close by.
Then remove the thread from that hole by inserting your finger into the buttock and pulling out the end of the thread. Bring both ends together and tie them tightly. Leave it for a day or two.  Whenever the thread cuts into the flesh, tighten it well until the flesh between the ends of the threads breaks and falls off. Then treat the wound until it heals and is cured.
If the opening of the wart is not perforated, perforate it as best you can, but not if it is deep within.  Do not do this because of the muscle, lest you cut it. Then do with it as we mentioned until it heals.
The image of the pointed lancet, with which you cut the warts, should have a very sharp bend on one side and a blunt side on the other, so as not to cut what is unnecessary, as you see.
Chapter Eighty-one.
Concerning hemorrhoids that bleed, their excision, and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids come in two types: either they are within the anus, resembling red inflated sacs or like grapes, and are sometimes small and sometimes large, and blood constantly flows from them.
They may be single or multiple, and can also be found outside the anus, on its edges. However, those outside the anus are often less moist, flowing with yellow water or a little blood intermittently.  They are the color of the body.
The treatment for those inside the anus is to instruct the patient to defecate and strain until the anus is stretched and the small growths appear.  Quickly, grasp them with forceps or your fingernail, then cut them off at their base; if the forceps do not hold them well because of their moisture and looseness,
Then grip it with a rough cloth or a piece of sharpened wood, or pull it out with your fingers, then cut it, and after cutting, sprinkle sharp powders over it to act as cauterization, or cauterize it as described in the chapter on cauterization.
Then treat it with other remedies until it heals. If the anus does not come out, inject the patient with an enema containing a little irritant to wash what is in the anus and encourage it to come out quickly when the patient strains.  As for the growths outside the anus, their treatment is easy and simple.
To remove it, grasp it with your fingernail or forceps, and cut it off.  Then treat it as previously mentioned until it heals.  If one dislikes cutting with iron, one should use a needle.  Take a twisted thread and insert it into a needle.
Then pull the wart upward and pierce it with the needle at its base on the other side. Wrap the ends of the thread under the needle, which is positioned across it, and pull the wart firmly. Tie the thread and remove the needle.  Do this for all the warts, leaving one that is not pierced.
To cause blood to flow from it, then place a cloth soaked in rose oil or cumin oil on the anus, and instruct the patient to remain still.  Then leave it until the warts fall off. If the warts fall off, treat them with ointments and other remedies until they heal.
Concerning the treatment of fissures, they frequently arise from dryness, and constipation.  If they persist and are not helped by medication, it is necessary to scrape them with a scalpel or your fingernail until they become moist, and the upper crust that prevents their healing is removed.
Then treat it until it heals properly.  If it does not heal, repeat the scraping more forcefully than before until it bleeds and peels. Then treat it, and it will heal.
Chapter Eighty-Two.
On the treatment of ingrown nails, both ingrown and not ingrown, dry warts, and the pimple.
The nail is a round nodule the color of the body, resembling the head of a nail, found throughout the body, especially at the soles of the feet and toes.  Pain occurs when walking.
One should split the surrounding tissue, grasp the nail with a chisel or hook, and pull it out from its root. Then treat it.  Or, if desired, cauterize it, as was explained in the relevant chapter.  As for dry warts, each is a small, rough, hardened, round protuberance on the surface of the body.
They are frequently found on children's hands.  Treatment involves stretching the wart, cutting or piercing it with silk thread or a hair until it falls off. Or, if desired, cauterize it, as previously described, with fire or a strong medicine.
Inverted and non-inverted warts can be removed with the instrument I mentioned in the treatment of the pimple. The pimple is also a small, hardened, thick protuberance on the surface of the body, going quite deep. If it is affected by cold, it feels like the sting of an ant.
It occurs throughout the body, most often on the hands.  Treatment involves taking a tube made of strong goose or hawk feathers, placing it around the pimple so that the tube surrounds it on all sides, and then rotating your hand with the tube until the surrounding tissue is split.
Push it into the depth of the flesh and the pimple will be pulled out from its root.  Or, if you prefer, do this with a brass or iron tube in this manner:
The top of the tube should be solid and twisted, so that the fingers can easily control and twist it.  Alternatively, you can cauterize the pimple, as previously described in the section on cauterizing warts.
Eighty-third Chapter.
Images of instruments used for injections in cases of rectal ailments, diarrhea, and colic.
The injector may be made of silver, or pewter, or of hollow or hammered brass.  Small and large versions of this instrument may be made, depending on the intended users. Those used to treat young children or those with narrow or painful rectums should be very delicate.  Image of a large injector:.
The upper funnel, where the syringe is attached, should be wide, with a partition where the syringe is fastened, as you see.  The lower end, which enters the rectum, should be very smooth and perfectly rounded, with two holes on one side and one on the other.
The size of the hole should be the thickness of a reed or slightly thicker.  The syringe, into which the medicines are placed, should be made of animal bladder or sheep's skin, and should be the size of a small plate, one span in diameter.
Many holes are pierced along it, as it is rotated, with the distance between each hole being the thickness of a finger.  Then a strong, doubled thread of ten threads or so is inserted into those holes, and the syringe is gathered together like a plate. When the medicine is placed inside, tighten the top of the injector between the stones firmly, then inject. This is an image of the syringe and the hole.
The method of injection is to fill the bladder or skin with the medicine, warmed to the patient's tolerance.  Be careful that it is not too hot or too cold.  Then the patient lies on their back with their legs raised.
A mat or blanket is placed underneath.  Then the tip of the injector is smeared with ointment, egg white, or licorice juice, or similar.  Gently insert it into the rectum.  Then the syringe's attendant takes the syringe in both hands and squeezes it firmly until all the contents of the injector are emptied into the intestines.
Then the injector is removed, and the patient lowers their legs to the wall.  If the medicine is urging its way out, let them hold it as much as possible. If they remain in it all night, the effect on the ailment is better.  If it is injected for diarrhea, and the expulsion is hastened, repeat the injection until healed.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful tumors in the rectum, the injector should be very gentle, smooth like the syringes used for children.  This is an image of a gentle injector with its skin tightened.
Chapter Eighty-four.
In the treatment of surgeries.
I have already mentioned, in the division of treatment, the topic of cauterization, and here I am now discussing its treatment by hand and medications comprehensively. I say that surgeries differ according to the thing causing the surgery, and according to the location where the wound occurs.
The things that cause surgeries are numerous, such as a blow from a stone, a cut from a sword or knife, a thrust from a spear or a stick or arrow, and similar things.
And as for surgeries according to locations on the body, such as wounds that occur on the head, neck, chest, abdomen, liver, and similar organs.
And I will describe the treatment of some surgeries, to make them a standard and rule for other surgeries, beginning with simple head injuries, especially as those that are complex will be mentioned in the first chapter of the third book of this text.
So I say that if a simple wound occurs on the head, and it is not a bone fracture, I look to see if it was caused by a blow from a stone or something similar.  If only the skin was bruised and the wound was large, and I feared the occurrence of a hot swelling, then quickly lance the scalp at the spot. Do not delay this, and draw from him blood according to his strength, unless there is something preventing the bloodletting.  Especially if the blood coming from the wound is little; and let the bloodletting be from the opposite side of the injured area.
Warn the patient against fullness, drinking, and coarse foods. If anything prevents the bloodletting, let him lessen his food intake, avoid meat and drinks, and apply a bandage soaked in rose oil alone, or with a constricting drink, to the wound if a hot swelling occurs.
And if you fear a hot swelling, apply a bandage to the wound if it is fresh with its blood, and the air has not altered it—the air-treatment I will describe to you shortly. However, if the air has altered it, apply some ointments to it.
Until pus forms, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large, and caused by a sword cut or similar, and its edges do not come together by themselves, bring them together by stitching, as I describe in stitching abdominal wounds.
If the skin has exposed the bone and is hanging, and its attachment is only in a small flap, cut it off and then treat the wound with medicines that will cause strong flesh to grow in place of the skin. Instruct the patient to eat foods that provide strength, such as heads, and the like.
If a festering occurs in the skin, but it has not adhered to the bone, cut off all the festering material and throw it away, and then treat it. If in these wounds there is an artery or vein that bleeds, and the bleeding does not stop with medicines, seek out the artery.
If you find it, and it does not stop bleeding, cut it with a lancet and tie it off.  If the methods mentioned are ineffective, cauterize it until the bleeding stops.  If these wounds are small and simple, their treatment is easy and straightforward; simply apply this powder while they are still bleeding.
Before the air changes it, the recipe for the powder is: one part frankincense, two parts aloes, and three parts quicklime or unquicklime.  Grind all of these together, sift them, and fill the wound with them.  Apply a tight bandage so that it adheres well.
It will clot with the blood, then bind it from above with bandages.  Leave it without removing it as long as it does not swell.  The medicine will not be removed until it heals.  The lime alone may accomplish this if the frankincense and aloes are unavailable.
This action can also be done with the powder in many large wounds if it is correctly applied and bound, and if the wounds are fresh and bleeding, as long as the air has not altered them. However, if the air has somewhat altered them, especially during summer.
Therefore, there is no effective use of this powder.  Treat it with the methods described previously: apply some ripening ointments or a paste of barley flour, water, and honey, to draw out the pus.  Then treat it with other methods until healed.
However, if a wound involves a simple fracture of the bone, draw it out with a cloth.  Treatment of head fractures has been described elsewhere, in the section on neck surgery.  If the wound is in the neck, there is no difference in treatment compared to a simple head wound.
If a nerve or artery in the neck has been severed, if it is a nerve, there is no remedy except to avoid treatments that cause intense constriction, such as verdigris, alum, and similar substances, because they injure and spasm the nerve.
Do not apply anything cold to the wound at all, because the essence of the nerve is cold, and its connection to the brain, which is the noblest of organs.  Use gentle remedies such as moistened rosewater, washed with fresh water, repeatedly moistened with oil, or rose and tutty ointment.
Also wash with fresh water and other mineral stones in this manner, and use moist ointments.  If the wound is large, use sutures or bring the edges together with bandages, then treat until healed.
If the wound has a recess and pus has collected in its depths, lance it at the lowest point.  If an artery has been severed in the wound and you see blood flowing, tie it off, or cauterize it if necessary.
If the wound has severed some or all of the cartilages of the larynx, but the veins are intact, bring the edges of the skin together with sutures along the cartilage of the larynx. Do not touch the larynx itself; rather, smooth it out and return it to its natural shape, then tie it tightly and leave it for a number of days.
Then treat it with ointments that cause contraction and drying until healed. If the wound has affected the bone, probe it. If there are fragments of bone, remove them all or as many as possible. If you can't, leave it until the wound becomes festered and healed, and the removal is easier.
If the wound is fresh, sprinkle it with the powder we described. If bone in the wound has become festered and suppurated, treat it as described in the section on chest and shoulder surgery.
If the wound is from a spear or knife and you see a hollow in it, check if air comes out when the patient breathes. If it does, know that it is a fatal wound.  If it has no hollow, and is fresh with blood, do not initially apply the powder, nor tighten it, so as not to trap blood in its hollow.
It will recede to the heart and kill the patient. However, apply a drawing ointment twice a day. If you do not have ointment, place a used cotton swab in the wound to absorb the moisture, and have the patient sleep with the wound facing up.
To drain what collects there.  If three days or more have passed since the wound was inflicted, and the patient has shown no spasms, poor pulse, or shortness of breath, and you find the rest of their condition sound, know that the wound is healthy.  Treat it with bandages and other healing measures until healed.
If the wound fails to heal and the discharge persists, know that it has become suppurated. Treat it according to the relevant section.  The patient should lie on the wound to drain the accumulated material. If the wound is on the chest or back, treat it with the previous methods of suturing.
If it is large, use the powder. If it is small. If it has affected the bone, breaking off fragments, explore the wound and promptly remove those fragments if they are loose. If they are not loose, leave them until...
It will cause the wound to fester, making removal easier. As for other wounds occurring in other parts of the body, their treatment follows the principles we have described.
Chapter Eighty-five.
On abdominal wounds, intestinal prolapse, and their suturing.
A wound exposing the abdomen may be large, small, or medium-sized.  A large wound may protrude intestines, or several intestines, making their replacement and suturing more difficult and arduous.
A small wound, too, may present difficulties in another way, making its repair more challenging.  If the intestines are not returned to their proper position promptly, they swell and thicken, making their repositioning more difficult. Therefore, the intermediate wound is the best, since the intestines can be returned without the difficulty encountered with these other types of wounds.
Know that there are four types of abdominal sutures. Two are general-purpose, suitable for abdominal and other bodily wounds; the other two are specific, though they are more suited to abdominal wounds.
I will describe them to you one by one with explanation. If a wound is small and something of the intestines protrudes, its return may be difficult for one of two reasons: either due to the small size of the wound, as we have said, or because the intestine has swollen due to a cold from the air.
If this is the case, it is necessary to warm it by soaking a sponge or damp cloth in lukewarm water, alone or possibly with cooked raisins, dates, and myrrh, and apply it to the intestines until the swelling subsides.
A drink with astringents may be used, which is better than water alone for dissolving the swelling.  If the swelling has subsided, moisten the intestines with water in which hyssop or coriander has been cooked; this will facilitate their return with less effort.
If the return is still impossible after this treatment, make a small incision in the wound with the instrument used for lancing abscesses. The initial treatment is preferable to incision, which is only resorted to as a last resort when all else fails.  This is a depiction of the instrument.
Its one curved side is limited, and the other side is unlimited.  The thin end is not as thin as a scalpel, but rather slightly blunt. It is a tool resembling a small awl, as you see. If the wound is wide and the intestines have entered, it is necessary for the intestines to be returned to their natural shape.
His specific location is best if possible. However, if the wound is large and in the lower abdomen, the patient should lie on their back with their legs raised higher than their head. If it is in the upper abdomen, the head and chest should be raised higher than the lower body.
Likewise, if the wound is on one side of the abdomen, always aim to keep the side with the incision higher than the other side. This is how it should be applied in major and moderate surgical procedures.
For minor surgeries, place it as best you can.  Then have an attentive assistant hold the entire wound with their hands, bringing the edges together, and gradually revealing portions for the surgeon to stitch.
This is the general method for single-stitch sutures: take a needle or several needles, sized according to the wound's width.  Then leave a space equal to the thickness of the little finger on either side of the wound edges, and insert a single needle without thread into the skin's edges. With the edges of the peritoneum beneath the skin, on the inside, so that it pierces through there.  Having brought together the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, creating four flaps, then tighten the doubled thread around the needle repeatedly from both sides until the wound edges are firmly joined together.
Then leave a space equal to the thickness of a finger again and insert another needle, and tie it with the thread as you did with the first needle.  Continue this with as many needles as necessary to completely close the wound. Let the needles be of medium thickness.
Because very thin needles quickly cut the flesh, and thick needles are difficult to insert into the skin, therefore, the needles should be of medium thickness.  A needle should not be inserted close to the edge of the skin.
So that the flesh is not quickly cut and the wound opens before it is healed.  Nor should the stitching be too far apart, so that the wound does not prevent healing.  Then cut the needle ends so as not to harm the patient during sleep.  Prepare strips of linen from each side of the wound to hold the ends of the needles.
Leave it until you know that the wound has healed.  This type of stitching with needles is most suitable for small surgeries, as it may suffice to use only one or two needles, or similar.
As for the method of stitching for the common people, it is to gather the four edges, that is, the edges of the skin and the edges of the peritoneum, in one stitch with a needle with a thread of moderate thickness and thinness. Then, after the needle has passed through these four edges, return the needle from the same side from which it began.
So that the thread is interwoven at the top of the wound, to make the stitching like that of bags in which goods are fastened.  Make the space between each stitch the width of a little finger.  This method of stitching is known to everyone.
With this stitching, a surgical procedure was performed on a man who had a knife wound in his abdomen. The surgical opening was more than a span, and approximately two spans of the small intestine had come out. The wound was in the middle of the abdomen.
After the intestines remained outside the wound for twenty-four hours, the wound healed in about fifteen days.  He was treated until he recovered and lived many years afterward, managing all his affairs.  Doctors had judged that he would never recover.
Surprisingly, I did not treat it with any ointment, as I was in a place where there were no medicines. I used to put used cotton on the wound twice a day, and I would wash it with honey water until it healed.
Concerning one of the specific types of stitching, I mention it according to Galen's text:  Begin the stitching at the skin, inserting the needle from outside to inside.  When the needle passes through the skin and the muscle, it should be directed straight along the length of the abdomen.
Leave the edge of the peritoneum on the side where you inserted the needle.  Pass the needle through the opposite edge of the peritoneum from inside to outside, then again through that same edge of the peritoneum from outside to inside.
Leave the edge of the peritoneum on this side, and insert the needle into the opposite edge from inside to outside.  At the same time as you pass through the peritoneum, also pass the needle through the edge of the mesentery on its side until you have passed through the whole thing.
Then, begin again from the same side, tracing the edge of the peritoneum on the opposite side.  Bring the needle out of the skin near it, then return it to that same skin, tracing the edge of the peritoneum on the opposite side, together with this edge of the mesentery.
Bring it out of the skin on its side.  Do this again and again, until the entire wound is sewn in this manner.  In summary, the characteristic of this stitching is that it sews like the stitching of furriers for furs.
That it sews the peritoneum once from one side with the edge of the skin and leaves it once from the other side until it is empty.  As for the second type of specific stitching, which Galen also mentioned, this is his statement verbatim.
It is that you sew it like some healers do, by bringing each part to its corresponding, similar part; naturally, they join the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge, and the edge of the mesentery to its other edge.  This is how I describe it to you.
The needle should be inserted into the edge of the mesentery nearest to you, from the outside, and pierce through it alone to the inside. Leave the edges of the peritoneum.  Then, return the needle and pierce from the outside to the inside through both edges of the peritoneum. Then return it again.
And pierce it from inside to outside in the edge of the other mesentery on the opposite side. This type of stitching is better than the ordinary, easy stitching, which is the one mentioned previously. This is Galen's statement verbatim. Some have said that sometimes abdominal stitching can be done with two needles. That is, inserting a single thread, and beginning by inserting the needle from your side and piercing it to the other side, and inserting the other needle from the opposite side, piercing it to your side, according to the stitching method of the leather workers.
Know that a rupture in the middle of the abdomen is more difficult to stitch than other areas of the abdomen.  As for treating the wound, its treatment is like the treatment of other wounds.  If you catch the wound fresh with its blood, before the air changes it, and you replace the intestines, stitch it, and make it strong, then apply the styptic dressings to it.
If the wound has been affected by air, apply some of the ointments that you have available until it becomes suppurated, the sutures fall out, and the peritoneum and mesentery adhere. Then treat it as you would any other wound until it heals.
If you do not have any medicines available, apply to it from the beginning of suppuration, cotton wool that has been worn.  Change it twice a day, as I have taught you, until it heals.  If the wounds are minor, you will usually not need any other treatment. If you fear that the wound may cause pain to the principal organs.
Therefore, you should dip soft wool in moderately warm oil or rose oil, and place it around the areas between the groin and armpits.  If he feels pain or putrefaction in his intestines, which often occurs, irrigate him with a lukewarm, dark astringent drink.
Especially if the putrefaction has reached the intestines and has become a penetrating wound into its cavity.  Know that the intestines that are thick are easier to heal, and those that are thin are more difficult to heal.
As for the intestine known as the jejunum, it will not heal from an injury that occurs in it at all, because of the abundance of its large blood vessels and the thinness and fragility of its tissue, and its proximity to the nervous system. If the extruded part of the wound is a portion of the intestine that has been excised and has been caught fresh, return it to its place, the same as you would return any similar section of intestine.
If it has been a while and it has turned green or black, you should tie it with a thread above the blackened area to prevent bleeding, as the extruded part contains blood vessels and arteries. Then cut what is below that ligature.
And you should leave the ends of the thread hanging from below the incision, outside of it, so it is easier to pull it and remove it when the extruded part falls out and the wound becomes suppurated.  Describing the wound that occurs in the intestine.  Now, if a perforation occurs in the intestine and it is small...
If it is possible to repair it in some people, because I have seen a man who had been stabbed in the abdomen with a spear. The wound was to the right of the stomach.  The wound festered, and feces and air were coming out of it.  I treated it, although I did not expect it to heal. I continued to treat him until he healed, and the wound closed.  When I saw that the wound had closed, I feared for the patient that a bad event might occur in his abdomen.  However, no such bad event occurred, and he remained in the best possible condition. Indeed, he ate, drank, had intercourse, went to the bath, and exercised while under my care.  Experienced people have mentioned that if a small wound occurs in the intestines, it should be treated in this manner.
Take large ants, then bring the edges of the wound together. Place one ant, its mouth open, on the edges of the wound. When it bites down and closes its mouth, cut off its head.  It will adhere and not separate. Then place another ant near the first one.
Continue doing this with several ants, according to the size of the wound. Then stitch the wound. These heads remain adhered to the intestines until the intestines heal and recover. No harm will come to the patient. The intestines can also be stitched with fine thread.
Take a strand of this animal's adhesive material from the papyrus.  Then, carefully separate the string.  Tie a fine linen thread to one end, and thread that combined string into the needle. The papyrus thread is used to stitch the intestines, then returned to the body cavity.  This method of stitching with ants and papyrus is only a matter of hope and expectation.
However, if the tear is large and extensive, especially if it is in one of the small intestines, there is no remedy and no cure at all.
Chapter Eighty-six.
On the treatment of colds and nasal polyps.
Know that any wound or tumor, if it becomes chronic, protracted, and ulcerated, without healing, and continually discharges pus, is generally called a polyp, in any part of the body, and we call it a cold, or a polyp.
It is, in fact, a hard, thickened, white mass, without pain, with a cavity like the cavity of a bird's feather.
Therefore, some called it a feather. It is sometimes moist, constantly discharging pus; and sometimes the moisture may cease.  This moisture may be abundant, or scant, thick, or thin.
I previously mentioned in my classification of surgical conditions that every ulcer that does not heal, and in which flesh does not grow, is due to one of nine causes; one of these being either a deficiency or poor quality of blood in the body, or else hardened flesh within the ulcer and on its edges preventing the growth of good flesh. Or that the ulcer is excessively harmful and dirty, or that the ulcer itself is foul, and the matter it discharges is of poor quality, or that the treatment is inappropriate, or that a plague-like disease has spread in the region, or a local affliction.
Or that the ulcer is excessively harmful and dirty, or that the ulcer itself is foul, and the matter it discharges is of poor quality, or that the treatment is inappropriate, or that a plague-like disease has spread in the region, or a local affliction. As it occurred in the city of Saragossa, where the maturation of illnesses is difficult and the healing of tumors is slow by nature, or because of a single bone or multiple bones; and I have explained all these causes, their signs, and their treatment with remedies in the classification.
Here, I must inform you about the treatment of nasal polyps and colds, which involves manual methods.  Know that polyps can occur in all parts of the body, some of which end in large veins, arteries, or nerves.
or to the peritoneum, or to the intestines, or to the bladder, or to a rib, or to a vertebra of the spine, or to a joint of the many complex joints such as the hand and foot, and some lead close to a major organ, and the like.
Therefore, those polyps that exhibit these characteristics are among the difficult-to-cure diseases that do not accept treatment, and working on them is troublesome and ignorant.  If there are polyps that do not reach the aforementioned locations and I hope and expect to treat them...
And if you wish to ascertain this, take a probe of copper or iron; if the polyp runs straight, explore it with it.  If the polyp has a curve, explore it with a thin lead probe, because lead is soft.
It will then glide upon entering and turn towards the curve. If the polyp has many openings, and you cannot identify them with the probe, inject one opening. Then the moisture you inject will travel towards the other openings and flow out.
Then, thoroughly investigate in any way possible to determine if there is bone or nerve, or if the bottom of the polyp is distant or near, or if it is a single polyp with multiple openings.  Understand all of this to the best of your ability through careful investigation and the pain felt when you touch the area.
And similar to this are the signs and causes of the tumor. If you have accurately ascertained all of this, then proceed to treatment with confidence.  It is then to be seen if...
If the polyp is superficially located, close by, or in a healthy area distant from a joint, nerve, artery, vein, or any of the locations I have described, then incise the polyp as described before, and remove the hardened matter, corrupted flesh, and excess tissue from within.
And treat any flesh or warts that grow in its mouth, until it heals. If the polyp is deep-seated and straight, you should cut it as deeply as possible, then remove all its corrupted flesh.
Then use a twisted applicator, soaked in strong medicines, and insert it into the bottom of the polyp which you can locate with an instrument. Repeat this several times until the strong medicine consumes any remaining corruption at the bottom of the polyp.
Then apply ointments that promote the growth of healthy flesh until it heals. If it does not heal this way, cauterize it, as previously described. If the cause of the polyp is bone, and you are certain of this, then probe and incise it as I have instructed, unless something prevents you from doing so.
If it is a vein, nerve, or main organ, as we said, if the bone is exposed to you and contains some corruption and darkness, then scrape it clean until the corruption disappears, and then treat it with what will knit it together until it heals. If it does not heal and the pus persists, as it previously did.
Know that you have not reached complete removal of the corruption.  Expose all that is affected again, and meticulously scrape and cleanse it to the best of your ability. Then treat it.  If it heals, well and good. If not, know that the corruption lies in a deep recess, further corrupted.  Expose all that is affected again.
Thoroughly scrape and cleanse it to the best of your ability. If the corrupted bone is small and you can remove it, pull it out with the delicate forceps suitable for that purpose.
If there are many bones, remove them all completely. Do not leave any behind. If any ligaments obstruct your efforts, and if cutting them does not pose a risk, cut those ligaments and any flesh present and free the bones.
If you cannot remove the bones at the time, apply something to the wound to cause putrefaction, and leave it for a number of days so that the flesh and ligaments surrounding those bones putrefy. Protect the wound, ensuring the opening you made during your work does not heal.
Instead, if you fear that, apply a cotton ball soaked in powdered sulfur with oil, or a cotton ball soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments.  When the ligaments putrefy and the bones loosen, removal and extraction will be easier.
If it is a single large bone, like the tibia or femur, and only its surface is corrupted, thoroughly scrape it until the blackness and corruption disappear, then close the wound.  If a large portion is corrupted, and the corruption has reached the bone marrow, then it must be spread open.
Cut it entirely where the corruption ends.  Then treat it until it heals.  I tell you about a case that happened to a young man in his leg, to make it an example and aid in your treatment. This man was young.
About thirty years ago, pain appeared in his leg, caused by matter moving from within the body until it reached the leg and caused a large swelling. There was no external cause. Then time passed with the errors of the physicians until the swelling opened.
A large amount of matter flowed from it, and his treatment was poor, until the leg became infected with many openings, all discharging pus and bodily fluids. A group of physicians treated it for about two years, but none of them were skilled in surgical practice.
Until they came to me. I saw his leg, with matter flowing from those openings in great abundance. The man's body was wasting away and his skin had turned yellow. I inserted a probe into one of those openings, which led to the bone. Then I examined all the openings.
I found that they connected to each other from all sides of the leg.  I then incised one of those openings, exposing some of the bone. I found it corrupted, decayed, black, putrid, and riddled with holes, even reaching the marrow.  I reported what I found.
I removed the corrupted bone, and I believe that there was nothing but this corruption that I cut and exposed, and that I had excised it. Then I applied healing remedies to the wound for a long period, but it did not heal.  Then I exposed the bone again, over and above the first exposure.
I found the corruption connected to the bone, and I exposed whatever corruption appeared to me from it also.  Then I attempted to mend it, but it did not heal or close.  Then I exposed it again. I continued cutting the bone piece by piece, and trying to mend it, but it would not heal until I had cut off about a span of bone.
I removed it, along with its marrow, and mended it with remedies, and it healed quickly and recovered.  This repetition of the work and the incision was necessary due to the patient's weakness, his poor tolerance, and my fear of his death, since he often suffered fainting spells from excessive vomiting. He recovered completely.
Strong flesh grew in place of the bone, and his condition improved in his body. His strength returned and he managed his affairs.  He experienced no impairment in walking that harmed him at all. If a bone that had grown in place had broken.
It is necessary to expose it in this way: take a bandage and tie it to the end of the grown bone, and have someone pull it upward. Make another bandage of thicker wool than the first.
Then tie it to the flesh beneath the bone and pull its end so that the flesh is drawn downward.  As you expose the flesh in the area you want to expose, so that the saw does not injure the flesh, place a board or plank beneath the bone securely from below.
Because if you do this, the saw will not be prevented from cutting the diseased bone.  The exposure should be slightly above the affected area, so that there is no decay in the bone cavity which would not appear externally, requiring another exposure.
If the bone is diseased but not grown, rather connected piece to piece, and the disease is in its center or in some part of it, expose the flesh from all its sides. Then place the board underneath and expose it from the side where the decay is.
Once you have finished exposing it, expose it from the other side.  The exposure should be a little distance from the decay, as we have said. If the decay is in a joint, cut the decayed joint itself and expose the bone where it connects from the other side.
If the decay is in the connection of two joints, there is no remedy but removal. If the decay is in the hand or foot, the treatment is extremely difficult, but you must scrape away the decay as much as possible, removing and cleaning it however you can.
By whatever method is appropriate for you, provided no vein or nerve is encountered.  Know that the instruments for cutting these bones (such as saws and chisels) vary according to the position, configuration, thickness, thinness, size, smallness, hardness, and fragility of the bones. Therefore, a suitable tool should be prepared for each type of work.

Chapter eighty-seven.
In the cutting of limbs and the spreading of bones.
The extremities may decay either from an external cause or from an internal one. If you treat this decay with medicinal remedies and the treatment is ineffective, and you see the decay progressing in the limb and nothing stops it.
Then you should cut the limb to where the decay has reached, so that the afflicted person is saved from death or a great affliction greater than losing the limb.  A sign that this has happened is that the limb will turn black, until you think that fire has burned it.
Or it may rot after turning black, so that this rot spreads to the limb's adjacent parts and affects the entire body.  So, cut it immediately.  Likewise, if the cause of the decay is a sting from some venomous creatures, such as a sea scorpion, snake, or centipede, and similar creatures.
If the decay or sting is in the tip of the finger, cut off the finger and do not delay, lest the decay spread to the hand.  Likewise, if it has reached the hand, cut it off at the base of the forearm, and do not delay, lest the decay spread until it reaches the forearm.
Likewise, if it reaches the arm, cut off the arm at the elbow, at the joint itself.  If the decay spreads and you see it reaching towards the shoulder, do not cut off the shoulder at all, for that would be the patient's death. Use other treatments to the best of your ability.
Likewise, do the same for the leg. If decay affects the toe, cut off the toe at one of the phalanges. If it affects the foot, cut off the entire leg. If it reaches the knee, cut off the lower leg at the knee joint.
If the decay has reached above the knee, there is no remedy but to leave it and allow the patient to die.  The procedure for cutting or spreading the affected limb is to tie a bandage at the lower part of the area you wish to cut and another bandage above the affected area.
One attendant pulls the lower bandage downward, another pulls the upper bandage upward. You, using a broad scalpel, carefully separate the flesh between the bandages until all the flesh is exposed.  Then, cut or spread it.  Cotton cloths should be placed around it from all sides.
So that the saw does not touch the correct spot, causing the patient excessive pain and inflammation. If bleeding occurs during your work, quickly cauterize the area or apply some hemostatic powders, then return to your procedure until you are finished.
Then bind the injured limb with a suitable bandage and treat it until it heals.  I am telling you about a case, an example, of a man who had this exact affliction in his leg; namely, there was blackness and burning in his leg like a fire.
This decay began in his toe and spread throughout his foot.  The man, seeing the decay spreading in the limb, and experiencing great pain and burning, had the limb amputated at the joint and recovered.
After a long time, this same decay appeared in his index finger. He came to me, and I applied the remedies I had prepared for the hand after cleaning his body. But the decay did not stop, and continued to spread to the other finger.
Until the decay spread to the whole hand, and he asked me to amputate it. I refused, hoping to stop the decay, and fearing that he might die from the amputation since his strength was failing.  When he lost hope in me, he went back to his town.
I was later told that he eventually cut off his entire hand and recovered.  I have related this story to be of assistance in similar cases of this disease and to serve as a guide for treatment.
Chapter Eighty-eight.
On the treatment of abscesses and how to inject medications into them.
If a tumor develops in some fleshy part of the body and its duration extends until it gathers pus, then bursts or opens, and all the pus within it is discharged, leaving the affected area empty, like a vessel.
The skin over it, like a ragged cloth, has become thin, and the damage was not severe enough to affect bone, nerve, or ligament.  Therefore, it deserves to be called an abscess, not a fistula, unless its duration is prolonged, causing the damage to affect any of these structures.
Then it is called a fistula or ulcer, and its treatment is to cut away all that skin, as it goes around, especially if it is thin and like a rag, and you are certain that it will not adhere to the affected area because of the damage.
If you hope the skin will adhere, and the damage has not reached that extent, and there is thick flesh in the skin, then treat it with injection.  Look at the abscess; if it is large and the pus it produces has a foul odor, inject it with Egyptian medicine.
Which is vinegar, oil, honey, and alum, in equal parts, mixed in a vessel and cooked over the fire until the medicine thickens and takes on the consistency of honey, then take what you need of it, thin it with water and honey, and inject it into the abscess.  Tighten the opening and leave the medicine there for about two hours.
Then you remove it by squeezing.  Do this for several days until the abscess is cleansed and the foul odor is gone.  If you do not have this medicine, you can inject with ash water. This means taking the ash of olive wood or oak wood, and pouring water over it.
Then strain it and inject with it until you are certain that the abscess has been washed out. If the patient is not likely to respond to Egyptian medicine or ash water, inject with water and honey mixed with a bit of ground alum.
Or inject with honey and a drink mixed together, because honey cleanses and washes, and the drink adheres to the abscess, especially if the drink has a quality of astringency and dryness.  If you do this repeatedly and are certain that the abscess has been cured, then inject with something that will promote the growth of flesh in it.
For example, take some palm ointment and dissolve it with rose oil and an astringent drink, or inject it with some other remedies and ointments, whose properties we have described in the chapter on ointments. If the opening of the abscess is narrow and will not accommodate the syringe.
If it is narrow, widen it slightly with an iron instrument, or place a thread soaked in Egyptian ointment or Ceriacon ointment inside it to make it wider. Also, if the opening is wide, stitch the edges together, leaving only enough room for the syringe to enter without excess.
Also, if the opening from which pus is draining is elevated, incise it at the lowest point to allow the pus to drain downwards, because if the pus is retained in the depths of the abscess, it prevents the flesh from growing there.
If you are unable to incise the abscess towards the bottom as desired, then try to position the affected part so that the pus drains easily, as best you can, and ensure that no pus remains trapped inside.
As for the cohesive remedies that are applied to the abscess from the outside, and how to apply them, take one of the cohesive ointments suitable for your needs, spread it onto a linen cloth, and ensure the cloth covers the entire abscess. Then, using scissors, cut a hole slightly larger than the abscess opening, apply a soft ointment to another cloth, sized to fit the hole, and place it over the hole, keeping it away from the abscess opening so that the pus can drain out easily.  Do not remove the larger cloth until later.
For several days, remove the one covering the abscess opening at all times to allow the collected pus to drain out, and to determine if the pus is abundant or scant, and if it is ripe or not.
Furthermore, examine the abscess itself: does the patient feel pain, or not? Is there swelling, or not?  If your treatment continues in this manner and you see the material draining from the abscess opening is scant,
Know then that the abscess has adhered, or is close to adhering. In that case, treat it again tomorrow, or on the third day. Once you have treated the wound, replace the cloths and renew the ointment. If thin pus drains from the abscess after a long time, do not despair of its adherence.
Then its healing is near, because some people have slow wound healing and others have rapid healing. If after many days, unripe pus drains from the abscess, know that the abscess has not adhered. If the abscess is slow to adhere,
Then increase the drying medications, and let the medications be moist in texture and dry in strength, like the palm ointment, if extra colocynth has been added. The most effective treatment is to use palm ointment.
It is treated by applying rose oil and sprinkling moderate, thick, old wine over it, then mixing it into a paste and using it.  A similar action may be taken if you do not have palm ointment: boil honey until it is thick and use it, or take myrrh, licorice, and kender, pulverize them all, and sprinkle them onto the honey while it is on the fire. Then apply it to a cloth and bandage it onto the abscess, or apply the boiled, thickened honey, sprinkle the remedies on it, place cloths over it, and bandage it.
In such cases, long-leaved zeraoun, the root of the asphodel, kresna flour, and rue, either individually or mixed, may be used. Grind them, sift them, and sprinkle them on the honey I described, then use them. If the abscess has lasted a long time and has not healed with this treatment, know that there is corruption within it.
If it has affected the bones or other hard nerves or ligaments, treat it with the treatment for fistula, as previously described.
Chapter Eighty-Nine.
In the treatment of the abscess of the finger, the injured nail, the excessive cutting of the finger, and the separation of fused fingers.
Daḥs is excessive flesh that grows under the nail of the thumb or toe, and perhaps on other fingers. If it persists and its treatment is neglected, it swells into a hot tumor.
Or it may become corrupted and fester, even eating away the base of the nail and perhaps corrupting it entirely.  The corruption may reach the bone, giving off a foul odor, and the end of the finger becomes broad and discolored like bruised flesh. If you treat it with what we have described in the classification and your treatment is unsuccessful,
Then you should cut with an instrument all excess flesh growing from the nail, then cauterize the wound afterward. Cauterization in this case is very beneficial. If the bone is intact, and the nail is also intact,
If the corner of the nail had pressed the flesh inward, causing it to be pricked and irritated, then a thin dressing should be placed under the corner of the nail that is pricking the flesh, lifting it upwards, and the flesh should be gently cut.
And apply to the remaining flesh the burning and corrosive medicines until it is all gone, then treat it with ointments until it heals.  If the corruption has affected the bone, then that bone should be cut out and removed, for it will never heal as long as there is corrupt bone within it.
If you see the decay and corruption spreading in the finger, amputate it at one of the phalanges, as mentioned before, and then treat it until it heals.  If the nail is struck or bruised, and intense pain occurs, you should first lance the affected area.
Then split the nail with a sharp scalpel, making an oblique split from top to bottom, taking care not to reach the flesh beneath the nail.  For this would cause the affected area intense pain, and be a cause for the growth of excess flesh at the site.  Then treat the area with something that relieves the pain.
As for the superfluous finger that develops in some people's hands, it may be entirely fleshy, or it may contain bone in some parts, or it may have a nail, and some of it may grow at the root of a joint of some of the fingers.
Or it may grow on some of the finger's phalanges, and those that grow on some of the finger's phalanges do not move, and those that grow at the finger joint might move.  Whatever is purely fleshy should be easily cut, cutting it at its base with a broad scalpel.
However, those that grow at the base of the joint are difficult to treat; avoid cutting them.  As for those that grow on the finger at one of the phalanges, it is necessary to first cut the flesh in a circular fashion, towards the bone, and then to separate the bone with one of those appropriate saws.
Then treat it until it heals.  As for the adhesion that sometimes occurs between fingers, it often happens at birth or as a result of a wound, burn, or similar injury.  It is necessary to split this adhesion.
So that the fingers return to their natural position, then place between them a bandage or a slit soaked in rose oil, so that it does not quickly adhere; and separate them, or put a thin lead plate between them until the place heals properly.
Likewise, if adhesion occurs between some fingers and the palm, split the adhesion according to what is suitable and beneficial for the shape of the limb.
Chapter Ninety.
On cutting the veins.
Veins are twisted, thick blood vessels filled with black impurities, occurring in most body parts, but most frequently in the legs, especially in the legs of travelers, laborers, and porters.  First, it is necessary to repeatedly vigorously cleanse the body of black impurities.
Then, lance the affected person with a lancet.  Treatment with iron can be done in two ways: one is to cut and release the black blood; the other is to cut the vein and remove all of it.  In the case of cutting, the leg should first be soaked in hot water.
Until the thick, murky blood is loosened. Then, the patient's leg is tied with a bandage from above the thigh to below the knee.  Then, the vein is cut in one, two, or three wide incisions.
Then, extract the black blood from the bottom of the leg upward, and from the top downward, until you have removed the amount of blood you deem sufficient, or as much as the patient's strength allows. Then, bandage it and instruct him to avoid foods that generate black bile, and repeat emesis and bloodletting.
When the veins become filled and this harms the patient, bloodletting should be performed as follows: shave the patient's leg if it has much hair, then take them to a bath and soak their leg in hot water until it turns red and sweats, or have them engage in vigorous exercise.
If a bath is not available to heat the limb, then cut the skin lengthwise next to the vein, either near the knee or near the heel.  Then, open the skin with forceps and scrape the vein from all sides until it is visible.
When it appears, you will see it as a red, glistening vein.  Once freed from the skin, it resembles a tendon.  Then, insert a curved needle underneath it until it rises and is exposed above the skin.  Hang it with a smooth, blunt hook, and then cut another incision near that one, about the width of three fingers.
Then, scrape the skin away from the vein until it is exposed.  Then, lift it with the needle as you did before and hang it with another hook as you did at first.  Then, make another incision or several incisions if necessary.
Then, dissect and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel, and pull it out.  Dissect and pull it until it emerges from the second incision.  Then pull it to the incision above it, and do this until you pull it from the third incision, above all the incisions.  Once it is all out, cut it.
If pulling and dissecting it does not work, insert a needle with a strong doubled thread, tie it, and pull.  Insert the needle underneath and twist your hand in every direction until it emerges.  Be careful not to break it, for if it breaks, it will be very difficult for you to dissect it.
This will cause harm to the patient.  Once you have dissected the entire thing, place wool soaked in a concoction and rose oil or oil on the surgical site and treat it until it heals.  If the vein is constricted, with twists and turns that have bends in different directions...
If it is not straight, as we said, you should make an incision on each bend and twisting point.  Then, hang it with the hooks until you dissect it completely.  Be careful when making the incisions not to cut or injure the vein, for this will make dissecting it difficult and you should conserve your effort.

Image of the blind hook:.
It should not have a bend like other hooks, nor should its tip be sharp, so as not to injure the vein.  It should be thick and smooth in its bend, because if it were thin, it would cut the vein with its thinness.  Instead, it should have a thick, as we said, a considerable thickness.
Chapter Eighty-nine.
In the vein of the arm.
This vein arises in the legs in hot countries like the Hijaz and Arab lands, and in hot, sparsely populated countries. It may also arise in other parts of the body besides the legs, arising from an abscess occurring under the skin.
As it occurs within the bodies of snakes, worms, the husk of gourds, and worms that develop between the skin and flesh. A sign of the beginning of this vein's formation is that a severe inflammation occurs in the leg, then the area becomes puffed up, and then the vein begins to emerge from the location of that puffiness.
As if it were the root of a plant or animal, and once its tip emerges, it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing from one to two dirhams, tying it, and leaving the lead hanging from the leg.
Whenever something emerges from it externally, wrap it in the lead and tie it. If it grows excessively, cut off some of it and wrap the remainder, but do not cut it off from its root before it all emerges, because if you cut it off, it will contract and enter the flesh, causing a tumor, abscess, and a bad ulcer at that location.
Therefore, it should be gently coaxed and pulled out little by little until its entirety emerges from the body, and it may be that from this vein, in some individuals, what emerges is five or ten cubits in length.
I have heard that it emerged from a man to a length of twenty cubits. If it breaks off during your treatment, insert a probe into its hole and carefully press it back against the body for a long time until all the material inside is drained, and try to heal the area with medicines for a few days.
Then treat it with the treatment for tumors. This vein may have many branches, especially if it appears in a joint of the leg or in the leg itself, creating many openings and branches emerging from each opening. Treat it as described in the classification, and as mentioned previously.
Chapter Ninety-two.
Concerning the abscess on the worms that develop under the heel, called "the affliction of cattle.
This disease, in some of our countries, is called the affliction of cattle, because it often affects cattle.  It is a small single worm that develops between the skin and flesh and crawls throughout the body, both upward and downward.
Its movement from limb to limb can be felt, until it pierces the skin at a point, emerges, and forms from the putrefaction of some of the humors, like the worms, snakes, and gourd-like tumors in the abdomen.
Its danger is that if it crawls through the body and ascends to the head and reaches the eye, it might break through it and exit, thus destroying the eye. This occurs frequently.  Therefore, treatment and extraction must be undertaken when it is moving and perceptible.
Therefore, one should tie tightly above and below it with a strong bandage, then cut open and remove it.  If it has burrowed into the flesh and cannot be found, apply a cautery to the area with fire until it is burned away.  The worst outcome is its destruction of the eye, as we mentioned.
If it is seen near the eye in the head, tie tightly under it on the forehead, then cut open and remove it.  The patient should also be treated with medicines to cleanse the body of the foul, bad humors, and should avoid foods that produce putrefaction.
Chapter Ninety-Three.
On the incision of the disease known as the "nafar".
This disease, called the "nafar" in our country, is a pain that appears in some organs, then spreads from one organ to another. I have seen it as I describe to you.
I was called to a sick woman in a remote area. She uncovered her arm, and I saw a slight swelling in the vein of the arm. After an hour, I saw the swelling travel up the forearm with the ulna, like a worm ascending to her shoulder as quickly as quicksilver.
If it moves from one place to another, the pain disappears from that location and settles in the shoulder. Then, after an hour, it ran throughout the rest of the body, even to the other arm.  She then recounted that it circulated her entire body, as I observed.
I was amazed by the speed of its movement from one organ to another. Before this, I had only heard of the disease spreading from one organ to another, but I had not seen it with my own eyes in this way.  I had heard of it happening in others, but I had never before seen such an example as with this woman.
I concluded that this was because the woman was from a rural area, with a dry physique and exposed veins.  This is why the moving pain became apparent.  It is likely not to manifest in this way among those who live in comfort and whose bodies are moist, with hidden veins.
If you wish to treat it, and the patient feels pain, if it manifests as we described, then bind tightly above and below the affected area with a bandage, and lance it until the accumulated wind is released.
If you do not see it, treat it by shaking the body and dispersing the wind, using remedies such as rotten bean seeds or skirret seeds, and similar medications.
Chapter Ninety-four.
On the extraction of arrows.
Arrows vary according to their types and the locations in the body where they lodge.  Their differences in type include large and small, hollow and solid glass, and those with three or four angles.
Some have tongues, others have shards.  Regarding their location in the body parts, they fall into two categories: either they are in hollow major organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, and bladder, and the like. If an arrow lodges in one of these organs, and the signs of death, which I will describe to you later, appear, you should avoid extracting the arrow, for death will most often befall the patient. However, if these bad signs do not appear, and the arrow is not lodged deep within the organ, extract it and treat the wound.  Signs of a head wound by an arrow that pierces the skull and injures the meninges include severe headache, dizziness, and fainting.
However, if these bad signs do not appear, and the arrow is not lodged deep within the organ, extract it and treat the wound.  Signs of a head wound by an arrow that pierces the skull and injures the meninges include severe headache, dizziness, and fainting. Redness in the eyes and tongue, convulsions, confusion of mind, vomiting, possibly bleeding from the nostrils or ears, and possibly loss of speech and voice, and from the wound site, a white, porridge-like moisture, similar to the watery substance of meat.
If these signs appear, refrain from treating the patient and removing the arrow if it has not already been removed.  Concerning signs of an arrow striking the heart, if it is near the left breast and feels as if it has lodged in something solid rather than hollow...
The arrow might also pulse like the heartbeat, and dark blood might flow from the wound, followed by cold extremities, cold sweat, and fainting.  Know that death is inevitable.  A sign of an arrow wounding the lung is the discharge of frothy blood from the wound.
The vessels around the neck swell, the patient's complexion changes, the breathing is rapid, and he craves cold air. If the arrow strikes the diaphragm in the chest, it will be close to the smaller ribs, and breathing will be labored with intense pain.
He sighs and all the shoulder muscles move. If the arrow strikes the liver, intense pain follows, and blood resembling the liver in its redness flows from the wound. If the arrow strikes the stomach, undigested food may emerge from the wound.
Its condition is evident. If the arrow strikes the abdomen, causing a rupture, and excrement, or a portion of the intestines, emerges from the wound, there is no hope of treatment or arrow removal. If the arrow strikes the bladder and urine emerges, ...
If something protrudes outward and the patient's pain intensifies, know that he is doomed.  As for the other organs, such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, spine, collarbone, thigh, leg, and similar organs, in most cases they are spared.
Unless the arrow strikes an artery or nerve, and unless the arrow is poisoned, I will tell you something I have witnessed regarding these arrows, so you may use it to guide your treatment. An arrow struck a man in the bridge of his nose, near the root of the nose.
I extracted it from the other side, beneath the earlobe, and he recovered.  No harm came to his eye. I also extracted another arrow for a Jew that had struck his earlobe beneath the lower eyelid.  The arrow had receded, and only a small tip, the part that attaches to the shaft, remained.
It was a large arrow, from a composite bow, with a square, smooth iron head, lacking ear-like projections.  The Jew recovered without further eye injury. I also removed another arrow from a Christian's throat; the arrow was Arabic.
It had two ear-like projections, and I split it open between the epiglottis. It had pierced his throat, and I gently worked on it until I extracted it. The Christian recovered and healed.  I also removed an arrow from a man's abdomen; we thought he would die from it.
After about thirty days, and no change in his condition, I split the arrow open and maneuvered it, extracting it. He recovered and suffered no further harm. I saw a man with an arrow lodged in his back; the wound had closed over it.
Seven years later, the arrow emerged from the base of his thigh. I saw a woman with an arrow in her abdomen; the wound had closed, and the arrow remained.  No change in her condition occurred, and she experienced no hindrance to her usual activities.
I saw another man with an arrow in his face; the wound had closed over it, and he experienced little pain.  Many such cases exist. I extracted an arrow from a Sultan's officer, who had been struck in the middle of his nose, slightly deviating to the right.
The arrowhead was completely buried.  I was called to treat him three days after the injury.  I found the arrow wound very small.  I probed it with a fine instrument, but felt nothing.  He was experiencing a prickling and pain beneath his ear, on the right side.
I hoped that the prickling was from the arrowhead, so I applied a dressing with drawing and ripening properties, hoping that the area would swell and reveal the arrow's location, allowing me to extract it.  However, no event occurred to indicate the arrow had reached that spot.
I continued the dressing for many days, but nothing happened.  The wound eventually closed, and the patient despaired of removing the arrow.  Eventually, he felt the arrow inside his nose and informed me.  I applied a strong, corrosive medicine to the wound for many days.
Until it opened, and I probed it, feeling the thin arrowhead that was adhering to the bone.  I then continued opening the wound with the strong medicine until the arrowhead became clearly visible.  It took me approximately four months.
Then, when the wound widened and I could insert the forceps, I pulled and moved it, but it would not come out.  I continued to manipulate it with various instruments until I finally grasped it with strong forceps, as will be depicted at the end of the chapter.
The physicians had judged that the cartilage of his nose could not be set, but I set it, and the wound healed, and the patient recovered completely, suffering nothing at all.  I am telling you how I removed some arrows, so that this may be a standard and a guide for what I have not mentioned.
Because the parts of this craft and its details cannot be grasped by description, nor encompassed by a book.  Rather, the skilled craftsman judges the many from the few, what is present from what is absent, and deduces new work and new instruments when faced with unusual cases.
If I descend from this subject, I say that arrows are removed from the limbs in which they lodged in two ways: either by pulling them from the point of entry, or from the opposite side, and from where they entered.
Either the arrow is protruding in fleshy tissue, and can be pulled out.  If it does not come out at the time it entered, then you should leave it for a number of days until the surrounding flesh rots, and it will be easier to pull and remove it.  The same applies if it lodged in bone.
If it does not come out, leave it for several days and try pulling and moving it each day.  It will come out.  If it does not come out after days, you should pierce around the arrow in the same bone from every side with a fine drill, until you have widened the space for the arrow, and then pull it out.
If the arrow lodged in the skull bone and has penetrated one of the brain ventricles, and some of the symptoms I mentioned appeared, then refrain from pulling the arrow and leave it until its condition improves after several days.
Then work on pulling and removing the arrow. If it is deeply embedded and cannot be pulled out, use the drills around the arrow as I described, then treat the wound until it heals.  However, if the arrow is hidden in a place in the body, If it is hidden from sight, probe it with a probe.  If you feel it, pull it out using some suitable instruments. If you are unable to, due to the narrowness of the wound and the arrow's depth, and there is no bone, nerve, or vein in the way,
then enlarge the wound until you can get hold of the arrow and remove it. If it has two barbs, grasp them and remove all the flesh entwined around them by any means you can.  If you cannot remove the flesh by breaking the barbs and twisting them, then
If you try to remove the arrow at any place, use your hand with pincers in all directions to remove it.  Be extremely gentle, so that the arrow does not break, making it harder to pull out.  If you cannot remove it in your time,
leave it for a few days, until the surrounding flesh has festered, then try again. It will then be easier. If bleeding occurs, use the treatments mentioned in the relevant section, and take care not to cut a vein, nerve, or tendon. Use your ingenuity.
By every means you can, remove the arrow gently, carefully, and firmly, as I have described.  When pulling the arrow out, you should position the injured person in the same way they were when the arrow struck, this is the best approach.
If you cannot do that, use whatever methods you can.  As for an arrow that emerges from the opposite side, either something of the arrow will be protruding outward, or you will feel the arrowhead close to the skin, protruding.
Cut the arrow, making the cut large enough for the pincers to fit. Then pull it out, as this will make it easier to remove. If it is stuck in bone, twist your hand in a circle until the arrow has an effect on the bone and makes room for itself, then pull it out.
Otherwise, leave it for a few days, and then try again until it comes out. If the arrowhead is still in place, push it out with it. If the shaft has fallen off, and you want to use a pushing tool, insert the hollow tool into the arrow's shaft, then use it to push it out.
If the arrow is hollow, push it out with a tool that fits into the hollow. This will make it easier to remove. If the arrow is poisoned, you should cauterize all the flesh affected by the poison if possible, then treat it with appropriate remedies.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, abdomen, bladder, or flank, and it is close enough to be felt with a probe, and you can make a cut, make the incision, being careful not to cut a vein or nerve, and remove it.
Then suture the wound if it needs stitching, and treat it until it heals.  Image of the pincers used for removing arrows:.
Its tips are like the beak of a bird, crafted to resemble a scraper. If it grasps an arrow or anything else, it will not let go.  They are made in large, small, and medium sizes, all according to the size of the arrow and the breadth of the wound.
