Image of the hollow cannon:.
Image of the solid cannon.
This one has a solid tip, like a ramrod, to easily enter the hollow arrow and push it out.
Chapter ninety-five.
On the opening of veins.
Thirty veins are customarily opened on the body.  Sixteen are in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears, known as the *ḥasīsīn*; the two arteries visible on the temples; and the two veins at the corners of the eyes, known as the *nāẓirīn*.
The vein that stands up in the middle of the forehead, the vein at the tip of the nose, the two veins in the neck, the two veins in the upper lip of the mouth, the two veins in the lower lip, these veins known as the *jayhārak*, and the two veins below the tongue.
As for the veins opened in the arm and hand, there are five: one of them is the *qayfāl*, which is on the outer side, and the common people call it the vein of the head.  The *akḥal*, the middle vein, is composed of a branch of the *bāsiliq* and a branch from the *qayfāl*; the common people call it the vein of the body.
The *bāsiliq*, which is situated on the inner side, is also called the axillary vein. The common people call it the vein of the belly.  The *ḥablu al-dirāʿ*, situated on the forearm, is the one to which the *bāsiliq* is attached, and is clearly visible above the thumb.  The *ʾāsīlim*.
It is the vein between the little finger and the ring finger; it has two branches. In the leg and foot there are three veins: one under the knee on the outer side, the second the saphenous vein, located near the heel on the inner side, and the vein of the women, located near the heel on the outer side.
And in the other leg, there are three similar veins.  As for the two veins behind the ears, their opening is beneficial for chronic colds, migraines, scabies, and chronic, malignant head sores.  Their method of opening will be described as I explain.
This is done by shaving the patient's head and rubbing its back at the location of the two veins with a rough cloth, rubbing well. Then the patient's neck is strangled with a turban until the veins appear, their location being behind the ears at the low points of the head.  You should locate them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, mark it with ink.
Then take a scalpel, which is known as a lancet, and insert it beneath the vein in the skin until the scalpel reaches the bone. Then raise your hand with the vein and skin upward and sever the vein with the skin, cutting it cleanly, and the length of the cut should be the equivalent of two clenched fingers.
Or similar, and release the amount of blood desired.  Then bind them with bandages and leave them until they heal.  Earlier in the book, their cutting and cauterization were discussed.  As for the two arteries in the temples, their opening is beneficial for chronic migraines, severe headaches, persistent eye inflammation, and acute, profuse eye discharge.
Their method of opening, as I describe to you, is to bind the patient's neck with a turban until the veins are clearly visible, and their pulse is felt under your finger.  Then, mark the location with ink.  Then lift the skin above the vein with your index finger.
Insert the lancet from below, lift the vein upward, and sever it as you did with the other veins. Release the blood as needed. Then loosen the patient's neckcloth and place your finger on the vein for a while.
Then place a compress and bandage on it, and bind it tightly from above. Leave it until it heals. Their mention, cutting, and lancing were previously discussed in the beginning of the book.  As for opening the forehead vein, its benefit comes after opening the temporal vein for chronic facial conditions such as ringworm, sores, and chronic redness.
How to open it, as I describe to you: Bind the patient's neck with a turban until the vein appears.  Then, take the instrument called the lancet, and here is its image:.
Place the pointed tip of the lancet on the vein, strike it from above with a mallet or similar object, and allow the blood to flow as desired.  Then loosen the patient's neckcloth and bind it until healed.
A wide lancet may also be used, but it should not have a sharp tip like other lancets.  It should be slightly wide at the tip.  Open it using a twisting motion, because the bone is close by; the lancet might break if it is too thin.
As for the two veins in the eye sockets, they are beneficial for eye ailments such as scabies, redness, and other facial diseases.  To open them: have the patient tighten their neck with a turban, then open them while standing over their head. The incision should be made slightly twisting in a lengthwise manner, using a small, slightly wide lancet.
If the area has little flesh, the lancet will break if it is too thin.  Take the desired amount of blood, apply a bandage, and secure for one night. Then, remove the bandage.  Opening the nasal vein is beneficial for acute fevers.
Severe headaches and facial diseases, such as red pustules on the nose, especially if chronic, are also treated this way.  To open the vein: have the patient tighten their neck, hold their nose with your left hand, and use a thin, long lancet.
Insert the lancet into the middle of the nostril itself, between the nasal passages in a straight line, because the vein is not apparent there. Blood will immediately appear.  You should press the lancet in slightly, take the desired amount of blood, and then bandage it for a night, as it will quickly clot.
As for the jugular veins, opening them is beneficial for shortness of breath, the onset of leprosy, and melancholic diseases appearing on the skin's surface, such as black vitiligo, scabies, foul ulcers, and cankers.  To open them: the patient should tighten their neck beneath them with a bandage, and the surgeon should stand over the patient's head.
The patient sits on a chair, and the vein is opened lengthwise, a somewhat wide opening. Then, drain a moderate amount of blood, or as much as is needed.  Do the same with the other vein, then remove the bandage.
Apply a moderate pressure to both veins to prevent suffocation, and leave them until the next day; the wound will heal.  As for the veins of the temples, opening them after opening the cephalic veins is beneficial for mouth sores, diseased gums, foul ulcers, cracked lips, and foul ulcers.
Those that are in and around the nose.  To open them, have the patient sit before you, and secure their neck with a turban.  Then, turn their lips and look for the veins, one on the right of the lip and the other on the left.  Identify them by their blackness.
Around them are also thin, dark veins, which you should cut off cleanly.  If it is unclear and you don't know which one it is, aim for the largest and most prominent ones.  Do the same for the veins in the lower lip.
As for the veins under the tongue, opening them after opening the cephalic veins is beneficial for the constrictions that occur in the throat due to the disease of sore throat and mouth diseases.  To open them, have the patient sit before you facing the sun. Raise their tongue, and look under the tongue on one side and the other side for a vein; their color is dark.
Open them, but be careful not to cut them deeply, as there are arteries underneath, and excessive bleeding from those arteries might occur.  The three veins opened at the elbow are those customarily opened by people in general, and opening them is done in two ways.
Either with a wide, gentle lancet, or an olive-shaped one, until it pierces.  Or by slicing with a lancet, which is cupping [and this is its image].  Image of the wide, gentle lancet:
It is wide, as you see, to be suitable for opening hollow, full, prominent, visible, thick veins that contain thick, dark blood.  This is the image of the olive-shaped lancet:
This lancet is narrower and has a thinner tip, suitable for opening thin veins containing thin, yellowish blood.  This is the image of the cupping lancet:
This cupping lancet, suitable for slicing, comes in various widths and thicknesses, depending on the width or narrowness of the veins.  It can be used as a guide for other instruments.  It is well-known among artisans.
Now, concerning the basilic vein, one of these three veins.  The benefit of opening it is that it draws blood from illnesses located under the throat and neck, towards the chest and abdomen.  The phlebotomist, when opening it, must be cautious and positioned above it; if an artery lies beneath, any mistake or excess lancing will sever that artery.
This will cause bleeding, so it is important that it not be opened with a pointed lancet, but rather be sliced open with the cupping lancet.  If the basilic vein is not clearly visible, it should be avoided, and one should move to another vein or seek out a branch of it.
Or open the arm's cord, for it is evident, and slice it with the cupping lancet, as we have said.  If you wish to open it directly, then before tightening the arm, you should feel the location to identify the pulse, and then mark it with ink.
Then the arm is tied, and the vein is sliced at an oblique angle with the cupping lancet, as we said.  Ensure that the incision is made away from the artery's position.  When you see swelling at the location you marked with ink after tightening the bandage, that swelling is the expansion of the artery.  Avoid it.
If you see the blood during the phlebotomy surging like a child's urine, and it is thin, red blood, know that it is arterial blood.  Immediately, place your finger over it for a long time, then remove your finger; if the bleeding stops, it often stops.  Tighten the arm.
Leave it be, and warn the patient against neglecting it. Let it be on a pillow and do not move it for days until it heals.  If the bleeding does not stop, and you are overwhelmed and do not have a remedy available, sever the artery if it is visible to you, for its ends will contract and the bleeding will stop.
Or take a pistachio shell, split it, and take one half and tightly bind it to the vein with bandages and ligatures until the next day. If the bleeding stops, then do so; otherwise, treat it with the previously mentioned methods of applying cauterizing agents to stop bleeding. In most cases, cutting off its blood flow is not difficult.
Because of the small size of the wound and the secure binding of the arm, know this.  As for the dark vein, the benefit of its phlebotomy is to draw blood from the top of the head and the lower part of the body, because it is composed of a branch of the basilic and a branch of the cephalic, as we said.
The phlebotomist should be careful in his phlebotomy, because there are nerves beneath it.  If he increases the thrust of the lancet and strikes a nerve, numbness will result, difficult to resolve, and perhaps it will not heal at all.  This nerve is often apparent to the touch.
If, in some people, it is hidden and delicate, not clearly visible, then you should make your incision in it with a pulling motion, avoiding the nerve to the best of your ability. If the vein is between two nerves, then cut the vein lengthwise. As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of its phlebotomy is that it draws blood from the head.
It is beneficial for eye diseases, and in this vein, especially, if you wish to perform phlebotomy,  you should make the incision with an olive-shaped or wide rue-shaped lancet.  This is the safest of all veins, because there is neither an artery nor a nerve beneath it.
However, when performing phlebotomy, you should avoid striking the head of the muscle with the lancet and instead seek the soft area.  If the vein is not hit on the first attempt, it is not harmful to repeat the phlebotomy several times, though it may cause swelling in some individuals if not lanced on the first try.
But that swelling will cause no harm.  Regarding the method of phlebotomy, its symptoms, and what must be done to remedy them: first, one must know that phlebotomy is used either to preserve health.
Or it is used for diseases.  The type used to preserve and maintain health, and to prevent illness, should be performed on one of the three veins in the elbow: the cephalic, the basilic, and the median cubital vein.  Phlebotomy should be performed in early spring if signs of fullness appear.
Phlebotomy should be performed on a Sunday or a Tuesday after three hours of daylight have passed.  Phlebotomy used for illnesses, however, does not have a set time, but is performed when necessary, whether night or day.
At any hour and at any time.  Boys should not be bled until they reach fourteen years of age, nor should elderly men over sixty years of age.  If someone intends to perform phlebotomy for any reason...
He should cleanse his bowels before phlebotomy with a gentle enema if there is much stagnant phlegm, so that foul impurities from the bowels are not drawn into the veins during phlebotomy and harm the major organs.  The feverish, the intoxicated, and the drunk should not be bled until these conditions have passed.
One should also avoid phlebotomy after dysentery, vomiting, diarrhea, excessive sexual activity, strenuous activity, exercise, late nights, fasting, and any physical or mental exertion that weakens the body.  Then one should consider thinning the humors before phlebotomy if the blood is thick.
With food, drinks, and medications, if possible.  Then he should take a bath if nothing prevents him, or engage in some exercise, to thin the blood.  He should perform the phlebotomy in the middle of the day, as we have stated.  And he should clear his mind of all bad thoughts that day.
He should avoid worries, anger, and fear, and all bodily afflictions such as extreme fatigue, exhaustion, and sexual activity, and the like.  He should have in his room things that people are accustomed to using, such as fragrances, herbs, and amusements, as much as each person can afford.
Then the phlebotomist should sit on a higher cushion than the patient, then extend the patient's arm and the phlebotomist should rub it with his hand two or three times, then tighten the bandage with the ligature and twist it two or three times, and the tightening should be moderate.
Because if the tightening is not moderate, either excessive tightening will prevent the flow of blood, or if it is too loose, it will also prevent the flow of blood.  Then, after tightening, the patient should rub his entire hands together.
Until the veins swell and become noticeable to the touch.  Then the phlebotomist should wipe the lancet with a little old oil, especially.  Then he should place his left index finger on the same vein just below the spot where he intends to lance it slightly, so that the vein does not move and avoid the wound. Because some veins are like tendons, and they move during the lancing. And some are filled with air, so when the lancet is placed on them, they sink under the lancet and deceive the phlebotomist, and the lancet does not open the vein. And if it does open it, the opening is narrow.
Therefore, the phlebotomist should be firm and careful in all these matters. Then he lowers the lancet.  If the vein opens on that first attempt, then he does it; otherwise, he repeats it once more just below or slightly above that spot quickly if the area hasn't swollen.
If the patient's arm swells or hurts, leave it alone for a day or two, and do not tighten the bandage, for it may cause a hot swelling.  He should not enter a bath.  Then he can repeat the lancing if he wishes. If the lancet is inserted and the opening is small and the blood flow is thin.
And fear that not the desired amount of blood will come out, so reinsert the lancet into the same opening, gently and straight, and increase the opening slightly. Do this quickly before the area swells, for in many people, the area may swell with a small opening.
If you see that it has swelled, do not reinsert the lancet at all; it will not help.  Place some clarified oil on it.  It facilitates the flow of blood and is better in this location than plain oil or other ointments.
Use clarified oil in all your venesections when the blood flow is difficult.  The miraculous remedy and the schokhnaia may also do this, if one of them is applied to the area. The blood will become thin and dissolved if it is thick.
If a large swelling occurs at the site of the venesection, which often happens, especially in those who have not had it done before or if the vein opening was small, then quickly apply a sponge dipped in warm salt water to the swelling and bandage it for an hour. It will dissolve.
You should do this after the blood has completely exited the vein itself or another vein.  If a dark or greenish discoloration remains at the site after a few days, this does not harm.  If you wish, apply some dissolved gum and aloes.
Or something from the juice of the Foudang and the like.  A swelling and bump often occur when venesecting the Basilic vein.  Place your hand on it, and if you feel it pulsating when you press on it, it is a bad bump. Beware of applying any of the aforementioned remedies.
For it might bleed arterial blood, but bandage it with something astringent to firm the area, then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.  One should remove from each person an amount of blood according to their strength and the prevailing color of the blood.
If the blood is black, let it flow until it turns red.  Likewise, if you see it thick, let it flow until it becomes thin.  And if it is sharp, let it flow until its sharpness subsides.  For those who are robust and full-bodied and require bloodletting in one session...
...the vein incision should be wide, and the lancet wide.  For the weak, the opposite; and it should be removed in stages and the opening narrow.  The best tool for venesection is a curved, oblique incision, not a piercing one.
This type of venesection is free of bleeding and of nerve damage. It is better and safer than transverse or longitudinal venesection.  If someone is prone to fainting during venesection, they should be given something to eat beforehand, such as bread soaked in pomegranate juice or sherbet.
If the patient is free from illness and blood is drawn in three or four sessions, if they are cool-natured, they should eat bread soaked in date syrup, or honey syrup perfumed with flowers, or good aromatic syrup before venesection.
If fainting occurs during venesection, and it is due to excessive blood loss, then the patient should be given meat broth and a delicate aromatic drink.  Perfume with rose water should be used, and the chest should be rinsed with it.  All other treatments mentioned in the section on fainting in the division should also be used.
This [treatment] is for vomiting.  However, for those who wish to further release blood flow in the arm, if the venesection was due to a great deal of vomiting and their strength is weak, then the blood should be released gradually, little by little, according to their strength over a series of days.
And if someone wishes to release more blood from the arm, and their body is strong, let them do so seven or nine hours after the initial venesection.  However, if one wishes to draw blood from the body to the opposite side from which it has flowed, then it should be done on the second or third day.
If a person has a lot of hot, inflamed blood in their body that has caused a fever, then blood should be released in one session, a large quantity being removed, and the opening widened until fainting occurs, after carefully observing all the conditions for venesection. And you should place your hand on their pulse while the blood is flowing, to prevent death from occurring instead of fainting.  This often happens if the surgeon is careless and inattentive.  This is not necessary if you intend to further release blood from the arm.
If the vein opening closes and blood flow is difficult, do not forcefully probe or twist it, as this is very bad.  Rather, either leave it until it can be opened again, or use the scalpel to remove any blood clots in the vein opening.
Or apply something dissolved in water, like salt, or a remedy like *al-fāriq* or *al-shaqazānī*, and gently probe it until blood flows. If the vein is swollen, leave it.
Do not touch it until the swelling subsides.  If further bloodletting is absolutely necessary, then either perform it above the affected area or in the other arm, or another vein.
As for bloodletting from the arm's vein, it is done in place of bloodletting from the *akhal* and *basliq* if they are unavailable or hidden, since the arm's vein is composed of them.  The procedure for bloodletting is that the patient should immerse their hand in hot water until the forearm becomes red and the vein is clearly visible. Then, a moderate ligature is placed slightly above the vein.
Then, incise the vein slightly off-center, neither horizontally nor vertically, and make the opening wide.  Incise it just above the wrist joint. If the blood does not flow easily, re-immerse the arm in hot water and let the blood flow into the water.
Until you have extracted the required amount.  If it is summer, you may not need to re-immerse the arm in hot water.  Using hot water is mostly necessary during the winter.  Bloodletting from this vein is safer than from all other veins. Because there is no important vein or nerve underneath it.  As for bloodletting from the ulnar vein in the right arm, it is beneficial for liver ailments. The procedure is to tie the wrist with a ligature, or with your hand, after immersing it in hot water until the vein swells.
Because there is no important vein or nerve underneath it.  As for bloodletting from the ulnar vein in the right arm, it is beneficial for liver ailments. The procedure is to tie the wrist with a ligature, or with your hand, after immersing it in hot water until the vein swells. The vein will be clearly visible.  Then, incise it slightly off-center.  Even if you sever it completely, it will not harm him.  Take care not to use the lancet vigorously, for beneath it lie the nerves of the fingers. The area is also bare of flesh.  Then return the arm to the hot water.
Leave it [the arm] to continue bleeding. If you do not return it to the hot water, the blood will clot in the opening of the vein and cease to flow. When you have drawn out the required amount of blood, apply grease and salt to the vein so that it does not clot too quickly.
Similarly, this procedure should be followed for any narrow vein.  As for the benefit of bloodletting from the left arm, it is beneficial for ailments of the spleen.  The procedure for bloodletting is the same as for the second [type of vein].  As for bloodletting from the saphenous vein, its benefit is for illnesses in the lower part of the body, such as ailments of the uterus.
And [for] retention of menses, kidney diseases, chronic ulcers of the thighs and legs, and similar ailments.  The method for bloodletting is to immerse the leg in hot water and apply rubbing until the veins dilate. Then, tie above the joint of the leg with a ligature. The vein is located near the heel, visible towards the big toe.
Many branches extend from it [the vein] along the leg. Bloodlet in the widest branch or at the point where it joins the heel; this is better and safer.  If you draw blood in the area along the leg, take care to avoid the nerves beneath the skin of the leg.
Make your incision by angling the lancet as if you were about to sever it, and let the lancet be sharp.  If the blood flow is difficult, then return the leg to the hot water and let the blood flow until it is drained. If the bloodletter misses the vein the first time.
If the bloodletter misses the vein the first time, he should return to the incision a little higher.  The area is safe, so there is no danger if you avoid the nerve, as we said.  The same method applies to the saphenous vein of the other leg.  As for the saphenous vein, its location is, as we said, at the heel on the outer side.
Its benefit for hip pain, if it is caused by hot blood, is as follows: Have the patient enter a bath and rapidly apply pressure, tightening their leg from the hip towards the heel with four fingers using a thin, long bandage. This is the only way it will be visible.
If it appears, then lance it in any manner; either by angling the lancet, which is preferable, or by severing it or making a slit, for its location is safe.  It is very hidden in most people, and if you do not find it...
If it does not appear at all to the touch, lance some of its branches, which appear on the back of the foot near the pinky and ring fingers, avoiding the nerves.  Release the amount of blood you desire.
Then loosen the bandage, place a compress on the lanced area, and bandage the site.  It will quickly heal.
Chapter Ninety-Six.
On Cupping and its Use.
Cupping vessels can be made of horn, wood, copper, or glass.  Cupping is done in two ways: one is cupping by incision and drawing blood, and the other is cupping without incision.
This cupping without incision is of two types.
And the cups for the cheekbones, which are the sides of the neck on both sides, and the chin cups, which are under the lower jaw from the mouth, and shoulder cups, and sacrum cups on the tailbone, and elbow cups, which are the middle of the arms, and leg cups, and heel cups.
Cupping draws blood from the fine veins spread throughout the flesh.  Therefore, it does not deplete strength like venesection, and cupping, whether with or without fire, should not be used in any disease arising from fullness until the entire body is emptied.
If cupping is needed for illness or custom, we use it at any time, at the beginning, end, and middle of the month.
For some people, when blood increases to the point where cupping is needed, they experience heaviness and headaches in their heads.  Some experience fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. Others experience itching in their face, temples, and eyes, and dark circles and soreness in their eyes.  Some itch the site of the cupping.
Some laugh excessively, some taste blood in their mouths, and gums bleed, and others spit blood. Some sleep excessively, and some see blood, redness, slain people, and injuries in their dreams. Whenever we see any of these signs...
Especially if it's in the middle third of the month, we order cupping after two or three hours have passed.  The benefit of a cupping puncture is for heaviness in the head and water dripping down the eyes.
However, it is necessary to do this after the body has been emptied.  This cupping can be a substitute for venesection of the temples.  Caution should be taken not to use it for those with a cold head or those with a cold, as it will cause great harm.
Therefore, it should not be used by the elderly, nor by those with cold ailments in their heads, and those who habitually use it may experience forgetfulness.
Therefore, the cupper should lower the cup slightly to avoid causing forgetfulness.  As for cupping the shoulder, it is a substitute for bleeding from the temples and the jugular vein.
Therefore, it is beneficial for asthma, shortness of breath, rupturing of the breathing apparatus, coughs, and fullness.  The cupping of the shoulder should be raised somewhat, as if it is lowered, it will cause weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for cupping the temples, it is beneficial for head pains, ophthalmia, migraine, sore throats, and toothache. It is a substitute for bleeding from the jugular vein.  The cupper should be instructed not to insert the instrument too deeply to avoid cutting a blood vessel and causing excessive bleeding.
As for cupping under the chin, it is beneficial for sores in the mouth, gum disease, and similar mouth ailments. It may take the place of bleeding from the veins in the lips.  As for cupping the shoulders, it is beneficial for palpitations resulting from fullness and heat.
As for cupping the hollows of the forearms, it is beneficial for what is beneficial from bleeding the three veins: the jugular vein, the temple vein, and the vein of the forehead.  Because it draws blood from all the thin veins within the flesh and draws those thin veins from other, thicker veins, until the drawing reaches the three thick veins.
The cupper should be instructed not to press too hard with the instrument, as the area is thin and has nerves and blood vessels underneath.  As for a single cupping on the coccyx, it is beneficial for hemorrhoids and sores of the lower parts.
The cupper should be instructed to use a large, brass cupping vessel, as the area requires strong suction, and glass cupping vessels might break.  Cupping the legs noticeably reduces fullness.
Because it draws blood from the whole body, it is beneficial for chronic ailments of the kidneys, uterus, and bladder.  It promotes menstruation, and is beneficial for boils and abscesses. It takes the place of bleeding the radial and ulnar veins, although it greatly weakens the body, and fainting occurs in most people.  Cupping the ankles is similar in benefit to cupping the legs.
The method of applying cupping is to first place the empty cupping vessel and apply moderate suction without prolonging the application.  Place and remove the cupping vessel quickly, to allow the humours to rise evenly to the area.  Repeat this until the area becomes red and open.
When the redness of blood appears, then gently apply pressure, and resume suction gradually.  Then assess the condition of the body.  If the patient's skin is delicate and porous, only one application of pressure should be made, to avoid causing a sore.
The cupper should widen the pressure slightly and deepen it a little, and regulate the suction gently and with light movement.  If the blood is thick, the pressure should be applied twice.  The first time, to open a path for the thin, watery blood; and the second, to remove the thick blood.
If the blood is very thick, the pressure should be applied a third time to achieve the desired result.  In general, if a little blood is to be drawn, one pressure is sufficient.  If a lot of blood is to be drawn, more pressure is applied; and if the blood is judged to be thick.
The pressure should be deep. The moderate limit in pressure is to only penetrate the skin.  What ointments should be used when applying cupping, and what waters, and what should the cupper beware of?  As for those whose skin is thick, hard, dry, and pores narrow...
The sites for cupping should be anointed with softening, relaxing, and dissolving ointments.  In summer, examples include  the ointment of Khirri, or violet ointment, or sweet almond ointment, or pumpkin seed ointment.
In winter, examples include narcissus ointment, lily ointment, chamomile ointment, or hyacinth ointment, and similar ones. If the excess is thick and cold, the ointment should be one of  marjoram ointment, hyssop ointment, benzoin ointment, or rue ointment, and similar ones.
If the cupper has wide pores and soft flesh, the use of ointment should be avoided.  These individuals should wash their cupping marks after the procedure with rose water, or cold water, or elderberry water, or gourd water, or borage water, and the like.  And as for those whose blood is excessively moist...
He should wash his cupping marks with vinegar, or rue and sumac water, and the like.  As for those whose excess is thick, he should wash their cupping marks with old wine, or marjoram water, or rue or chamomile decoction, and the like.  It is important to avoid cupping in the bath, and after the bath.
Rather, it is necessary to use [the remedies] an hour or two after emerging from the bath.  And it is not appropriate for anyone to sleep after cupping.  This is what the cupper and the leech should consider before and after cupping.
One must first consider whether the cupper or leech is bilious, and if the blood is predominantly sharp and inflammatory.  Then they should take cooling foods such as pomegranate, endive, vinegar, lettuce, sassafras, and the like. Their diet should consist of poultry, mutton stew, and similar meats.
And if one's temperament is cold, they should be given honeyed drinks, and drinks made from myrrh or sassafras sassafras, and consume moderate fragrant wine, which is between old and new.  They should be ordered to eat less food, and their diet should consist of poultry, quail, sparrows, and pigeon stew.  On the day of cupping and bloodletting, drinks should be more plentiful than food.
Sometimes, certain individuals may be given antidote, musk medication, or shiltha before cupping and before or after bloodletting to strengthen the principal organs and thin the blood.  However, this should not be given to those who are feverish.
As for cupping that does not require a specific condition, it is the cupping placed on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, kidney area, and hip joints, because these organs cannot tolerate the condition.  Instead, the intent is to draw blood from one organ to another.
For example, placing a cupping on the breasts in cases of epistaxis, or to remove a cold wind that may have affected an organ, such as placing a cupping on the abdomen and navel. This loosens the organ, warms it, and alleviates pain by resolving that wind.
It may also be placed on the kidneys if blockages or stones are present.  Through its strong suction, it may open the blockage or dislodge the stones.  Likewise, it is used on the liver and spleen when wind affects them. These cuppings may also be used by suction alone, without a cup.
It may be used with fire, or filled with lukewarm water for conditions of lameness.  This involves filling a large cupping with hot water alone or water in which herbs suitable for this purpose have been boiled.
Then, filled, it is placed on the affected area, held, and removed, repeated as needed. This is the method of using a cupping with fire.
Its mouth opening should be two fingers wide, as depicted, and its depth about half a span.  A small hole, large enough for a needle, should be made in one side, near the middle.  Made of thick-rimmed Chinese or brass.
It should be smooth, flat, and polished so as not to injure the organ when placed upon it.  A transverse bar of brass or iron is placed in the middle to hold the candle for the fire. This cupping can be made larger or smaller, according to the ailment.
The practitioner must consider the user's constitution; cupping for children and thin individuals is different from cupping for strong, robust people.  As for how to apply this cupping with fire to the affected area, a wick made of tightly wound linen or a small candle of pitch is placed on the central bar in the cupping device.
So that the heat rises from the bottom to the top of the cupping device, to avoid burning the patient. Then it is placed on the affected area, and a finger is placed on the hole we mentioned.  So when the cupping is held, the finger need not be removed.
Steam will then emerge from that hole, and the cupping device will adhere to the area. Then the wick is adjusted, and it is replaced if necessary.  However, the cupping used for dropsy is without a central bar and without a hole.
Instead, it is used by filling it with water and placing it directly on the affected area.  This is its image/form.
The larger the cupping device, the better, as it can hold more water.
Chapter Ninety-Seven.
On applying leeches.
Leeches are used mostly on parts of the body where cupping cannot be applied, either because the area is small, such as the lip, gums, and the like, or because the body part is exposed, such as the finger and nose, and the like.
To use them, select leeches from pure, unpolluted freshwater, then leave them in fresh water for a day and a night so that they become hungry and have nothing in their bodies. Then the body is first bled by phlebotomy or cupping, and then the affected area is cleaned.
Until it turns red, then a leech is placed on it.  If it fills up and falls off, that is good.  If it's possible to draw out the matter with cupping, this is more beneficial.  Otherwise, wash the area with vinegar, then with plenty of water, and massage and squeeze it.  If blood continues to flow after the leeches fall off, and it is a trickle, then soak a linen cloth in cold water.
Place it over the area until the trickle stops. If there is a lot of blood, sprinkle powdered alum or astringents of similar kinds over it to stop it, or put peeled halves of chickpea over the area.
Leave it until the chickpea adheres to the area, and the bleeding will stop.  If the leeches need to be reapplied, and other leeches are available, do not reuse the same leeches.  If the leeches refuse to attach, then the area should be wiped with fresh blood.
Or, insert a needle into the area until some blood comes out, then place the leech.  If it senses blood, it will attach to the spot. If you want it to fall off, sprinkle some acacia, salt, or ash on it, and it will fall off the spot.
Chapter Three.
On Fractures.
This chapter also, from the craft of medicine, concerns the setting of fractures and dislocations in bones.
Know, my children, that this chapter is claimed by ignorant physicians and laypeople, those who have never examined the writings of the ancients or read a single word from them.
For this reason, this art of medicine is lacking in our land, and I have never encountered a skilled practitioner in it. However, I gained what I gained through extensive reading of the writings of the ancients and my diligence in understanding them, so that I extracted knowledge from them.  Then I adhered to practice and expertise throughout my life.
I have presented to you in this chapter everything my knowledge encompasses. My experience has been applied to it after I brought it closer to you, purifying it of unnecessary length, and condensing it to the utmost brevity.  I have clarified it completely and have depicted for you many images of the instruments used in it.
This is, as in the preceding chapters, an enhancement of clarity. There is no power except with God, the Most High, the Great.
The first chapter.
This chapter contains summaries and essentials concerning the breaking of bones.  Before we begin to discuss the fractured and dislocated limbs individually, it is necessary to offer general statements and sections at the beginning of this chapter. This will first require you to understand them and grasp their true meaning, you and all others who are eager to learn this noble craft.
If someone suffers a fracture, dislocation, sprain, or fall, one should first attend to the reduction or easing, or both, of the injury. Unless prevented by factors such as weakness, or if the affected person is a child or an old man, or if the weather is extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then, his diet should be limited to cold legumes, poultry, and hare.  Drinks, fatty meats, overeating, and any food that causes a flood of blood to the veins should be avoided until swelling subsides and the risk of further fluid buildup in the affected area is gone.
Then, the patient should return to his usual regimen.  If the fractured bone is beginning to knit, the patient should consume foods that are nourishing, dense, and strong, with a viscous quality, such as barley, rice, heads of grain, and the bellies of cattle. Eggs, soft fish, and thick drinks, and similar things, will expedite and improve the healing of the fracture.  Know that broken bones in strong men and old people will rarely rejoin as perfectly as originally.
Due to the dryness and hardness of their bones, bones that are extremely flexible, like those of young children, may knit together. However, nature forms a substance resembling glue, thick and adhesive, around the fractured bone on all sides, binding the fragments together.
And binds them together until they attain their original strength and firmness, so that nothing interferes with their function.
Therefore, the patient's diet should consist of foods with strength, viscosity, and density, as mentioned. Know that fractures vary according to the affected body part, as a fracture of the leg differs from a fracture of the head, and a fracture of the chest differs from a fracture of the spine.
And so do all other body parts. We will describe each type of fracture in detail in its own section, and the nature of a bone fracture may also vary within itself.
For the fracture may be a simple crack without splinters, or it may be lengthwise with splinters and detached or non-detached fragments.  The fracture may also be accompanied by a wound or tearing of the skin, or it may be a slight fissure.
Each type requires a specific method of reduction, as will be detailed in its proper place.  A sign of a fractured bone is its distortion, unevenness, visibility, and grating sound when touched with the hand.
If there is no visible distortion, grating, or discomfort when palpating the bone, and the patient does not experience significant pain, then there is no fracture; it may be a sprain, a minor fracture, or a minor fissure.  Therefore, it should not be moved or prodded at all.
Instead, apply suitable medications to be mentioned later, then gently bind it.  Know that if a bone is fractured in two without splinters, each piece has separated from its neighbour.
Therefore, you must promptly realign and straighten it before a hot swelling occurs. If a hot swelling does develop, leave it for a few days until the inflammation subsides, then realign it gently and skillfully.
Know that setting and straightening a bone without splinters is easier.  Then it is bandaged as will be described later. If the bone has splinters, the injured limb (arm or leg) must be extended from both sides.
Either by your hands, if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or with both rope and hands.  Place the limb on a level surface in its natural position.  Once the fractured bone pieces are extended, then carefully return the protruding parts to their correct place using any method that you can devise gently.
Make every effort to avoid causing the patient pain or discomfort.  Make every effort to fit one bone piece against its mate in the best possible position.  At that time, you should touch and feel the pieces with your hands. If you find any irregularity, correct and realign them to the best of your ability.
Beware of excessive stretching and forceful prodding, as many ignorant people do.  Often, their actions cause inflammation or swelling in the limb, as I have repeatedly observed.
Then, after the alignment, precision, and bandaging, keep the limb at rest and quiet. Warn the patient not to move it during wakefulness or sleep, and during changes of posture, restlessness, bowel movements, or any other movement to the fullest extent possible.  Ensure that the limb's position is safe from pain.
If, in the course of maintaining the limb's position, the patient feels pain, then move the limb to a position in which no pain is felt.  Ensure that this new position is straight and aligned.
To prevent the limb from becoming crooked if it is set.  As for how to bandage a fractured limb, as I am describing to you, understand that broken limbs vary in size, and their conditions.  Smaller ones, like the arm, fingers, forearm, and similar parts...
Therefore, the bandages for small parts should be soft and gentle.  For larger parts, such as the thigh, back, and chest, the bandages should be firm and strong.  A wide bandage is necessary for large limbs, to apply even pressure on all sides, without any inconsistencies.
Then, after you have finished aligning it, apply the appropriate plaster. Apply a soft bandage to the fracture site.  Then start wrapping the bandage around the fracture itself three or four times, depending on the limb's size.  Slightly tighten the bandage, then continue wrapping it to the upper portion of the fracture.
Tighten the bandage less than you did for the fractured area, and then move the wrapping away from the fracture slightly, loosening the bandage gradually until the correct position is attained.  Then take another bandage and wrap it around the fractured area as well.
Wrap the bandage further, continuing towards the lower part of the fracture.  Maintain the tension and looseness as described in the first wrap on the upper part.  Then place soft bandages or cloths between the layers to correct any misalignment of the fracture.
If there is misalignment, correct it; otherwise, do not insert anything.  Then wrap another bandage around it.  Then, if there is no swelling or inflammation in the limb, apply firm splints to the bandages.
If there is swelling or inflammation, apply something to reduce the swelling and inflammation. Leave it for a few days, then apply splints at that time.  Let the splints be made of cut, prepared strips of reeds or from the wood of sieves.
Made of pine, palm fronds, rushes, or similar readily available materials.  Each splint should be of the same size and shape.
However, the splint placed directly on the fracture should be slightly thicker and wider than the others.  The length of the splint should be appropriate for the size of the fractured limb.  Image of the splint:.
Then, wrap the splints with another bandage, tightening it in the same way as the first bandage.  Then tie securely with strong threads, as we described earlier, making sure the tightening is greater over the fractured area. The tighter the wrap, the closer it is to the fracture.
The threads should be of medium thickness and not too thin.  Preferably, use damp linen threads, as thick threads, like those used by the ignorant, made from twisted linen strips, is a serious error.
Because tightening with them is disproportionate, and thin threads are also unsuitable, as you cannot achieve the desired tension.  Also, there should be at least one finger's width between splints.
If the patient is irritated by the edges of the splints after proper tightening, place soft pads or fluffy wool beneath them so nothing irritates him.  If the fracture is accompanied by a wound and the skin has been punctured, we will discuss this separately.
Know that not every fractured limb should be splinted from the first day.  If the limb is large, splints should not be applied until five, seven, or more days later, depending on the risk of a hot swelling.
This is a description of the bandages used by the ancients to treat fractures, dislocations, and sprains—a concise, common bandage for fractures, suitable for most temperaments, especially children and women, as it is neither excessively hot nor cold.
Take the flour dust, the fine residue that clings to the millstones during grinding. Knead it as it is, without sifting it with egg white, into a consistency that is neither thick nor thin, then dry it in the sun.
Another bandage for fractures, dislocations, and sprains: Take ten dirhams each of mastich, ladanum, acacia, rasīn, and mugath, five dirhams each of myrrh and saﬀlower, and twenty dirhams of ashl (a type of wood).  Also, twenty dirhams of Armenian or Roman clay. Grind all the ingredients together, mixing them with ashl water or egg white if the patient's temperament is hot.  Then use this bandage; it is well-suited, quickly sets fractured bones, and is suitable for most people because of its balanced nature.
Another bandage useful for fractures and sprains: Take ten dirhams each of mugath, mastich, and white hellebore, five dirhams each of myrrh and saﬀlower, six dirhams of acacia, and twenty dirhams of Armenian clay. Grind all together finely, sift, knead with water or egg white, and apply.
A bandage for joints and bones dislocated from their sockets, which relieves the pain and throbbing of bone and fractures: Take prepared wool, dip it in boiled vinegar and oil, and apply it to the affected area. This bandage is not a strong means of setting the bones.
However, it is excellent for relieving hot swelling and pain. Another bandage for setting broken bones: Take fig leaves and wild poppy leaves, grind them together, and apply them as a wet compress. Another concise bandage is used in the case of a fractured bone's displacement.
To further reduce the swelling, take one part each of hellebore root, chamomile, pansy, and carnation flour. Grind all together and knead into a poultice if the limb is not inflamed. If it is inflamed, knead it with coriander water or regular water and apply.
Another stronger resolving bandage is used when a hard swelling occurs with bone displacement: Take one part each of hellebore root, flaxseed, fenugreek, rosemary, pansy, and chamomile.
Grind all together and knead with vinegar water, fresh water, or a poultice, according to the heat of the limb and its cooling.  Hippocrates, however, did not mention in his book that this should be applied to a fractured limb during setting.
However, the preparation made of wax and oil alone, should be of a medium consistency between thick and thin. Galen, on the other hand, believed that dry things with some heat, such as myrrh, aloe, and frankincense, and similar substances, should be applied to the fractured limb during setting.
A dressing for weakness and pain: Take equal parts of mugwort, roasted chickpeas, scraped human hair or bird feathers, hellebore, and salt. Grind, sieve, knead, and apply as a dressing.  The amount of ligament that should remain...
Then, it should be loosened.  Observe; if the injury does not cause pain, irritation, or displacement of the fractured bone, do not loosen it for many days.  If, however, there is severe irritation, disturbing pain, or swelling at the site,
Then, immediately loosen it and do not delay; remove the dressing.  Then take a soft cloth or a wet sea sponge, dip it in lukewarm water, and wash the area until the irritation and pain subside.  Then allow the limb to rest for an hour.
Then, apply softened wool soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil, and bandage it overnight, so that the hot swelling subsides, the limb's swelling is reduced, and the pain disappears.  Then, reapply gentle tension and a light bandage, but do not tighten it as much as before.
And treat it gently until it heals.  If you see that the swelling, redness, pain, and inflammation, and all symptoms have completely gone, and a bandage and tension are needed, then redo it as you did originally.  If nothing of what we have mentioned happens to the limb,
Then, do not loosen it until three, four, five, or seven days, or even twenty days, all depending on the condition of the limb, as we said, until the fracture is set and the flesh around it begins to heal.
Then, increase the tension more than your original tension, and also increase the patient's nourishment, as mentioned before.  If you see the fracture site has dried and wasted more than necessary, know that nourishment is not reaching it.
Loosen it with lukewarm water each time you loosen it, every three days, and slightly reduce the tension.  In this way, nourishment reaches the limb and it heals quickly.  As for what the ignorant among the setters of fractures do, of breaking the limb again...
For if it is not set correctly the first time, but is set crookedly, that is an error on their part and a great deception.  Even if it were correct, the ancients would have mentioned it in their books and practiced it.  I have seen no trace of this from any of them. And the correct course of action is not to do it.
The second chapter.
In fractures of the head.
Fractures of the head are numerous, their forms varied, and their causes diverse.  Some fractures are caused by a sword blow, and these can extend all the way to the peritoneum beneath the bone. As the ax does to wood.
And so this type of fracture is called "ax-like".  Alternatively, the sword may have cut only a portion of the bone, exposing only one side, without penetrating to the other side. This type of fracture is called a "simple extraction," and the wound from these two types of fractures can be either large or small.
Some fractures are caused by contusion or impact, such as a blow from a stone, or a fall onto a stone, or similar. This fracture may either penetrate the membrane beneath the bone, or it may be on the surface of the bone.
And the wound from these fractures can also be either large or small.  Some fractures are hidden within the bone, hairline cracks, very slight separations.
Therefore, this type of fracture is called a hairline fracture.  One such fracture occurs from a fall, or a blow from a stone or similar, where the surface of the bone is driven inward, causing a depression in the area, similar to how a piece of copper is indented when struck, a part of it moving inwards.
This is most common in the soft skull, such as in young children.  All of these types of fractures may have detached or non-detached fragments. We will discuss the treatment for each in its place, and we will learn to recognize all these types of fractures through examination.
Examination with probes and removal of putrid flesh from them.  The hairline fracture is recognized by examining the bone, smoothing it, and staining it with ink; the fracture will then appear black.  As for the treatment of the fracture, first look at the patient's symptoms.
If you see symptoms that clearly indicate fear, such as vomiting bile, stretching, loss of intellect, loss of voice, fainting, high fever, bulging and redness of the eyes, and similar symptoms, do not approach the patient or treat him, for death will almost certainly befall him with these symptoms.
If you see symptoms that do not alarm you and hope for his recovery, then take action in treating him.  This is if the injured person comes to you in the initial stages of the injury, and this is in the winter.
In any case, you should strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day.  If it is in the summer, you should hasten the removal of the bone before the seventh day, before the membrane beneath the bone deteriorates, leading to the symptoms we described.
If the bone fracture has reached the membrane covering the brain, and there is also shattering and contusion, then you should cut away the shattered and contused portion, as I describe to you: shave the head of the injured patient.
Expose the bone in any way possible, according to the nature of the wound and what is easiest for the patient. If, during the exposure, you encounter bleeding or a hot swelling, then deal with it appropriately.
This means to fill the area with rags soaked in a drink of rosewater and oil, and clean the wound until the swelling subsides and the bleeding stops. Then begin to adjust and remove the bone, in one of two ways.
One way is to cut the bone with a thin, narrow blade.  This is its depiction:.
Then use another cut, a little wider than the first. This is its depiction:.
Then use yet another cut, wider still than the second.  You should have a series of different cuts, some wider, some narrower, all with extremely sharp edges, made of Indian steel or good-quality wootz.
Use gentleness when striking the cut, so as not to dislodge the head and injure it. If the bone is strong and solid, it is necessary to pierce around it before using the cuts, with the so-called non-piercing awls.
Because they do not penetrate beyond the skull bone, as the awl's head, sharper than the rest of the tool, is rounded like a ring or small circle. This prevents it from sinking and passing through the thickness of the bone.
You should have many such awls, each suitable for the thickness of the bone.  Thus, for each skull, you should have an awl with a sharp point of the appropriate length and width, corresponding to the skull's thickness. Here is an illustration of three types. Of large, medium, and small awls.
To pierce around the fractured bone, place the awl on the bone and rotate it with your fingers until you know the bone has been penetrated. Then move the awl to another spot, leaving a space between each puncture the thickness of a lead rod or similar.
Then, with the nippers, cut between each pair of holes.  Do this with as much gentleness as possible, as we have said, to remove the bone, either by hand or with another appropriate tool.
Such as the rasps and fine forceps.  One must be extremely careful not to touch the peritoneum with the awl or nippers. If you have scraped the bone free of the peritoneum, if it was attached, and have removed it, it should then be prepared.
The roughness remaining on the bone is smoothed with another tool similar to the nippers, but it should be finer and gentler than other nippers.  If any small bone fragments or splinters remain, carefully remove them with the appropriate tools.
Then, after that, treat the wound with the compresses and ointments described later.  As for the other approach, it is a straightforward method, free from risk. Galen mentioned it and praised it greatly. This is what he said. First, you must begin by exposing the part of the bone where the fracture is most severe and prominent. Once you have exposed that part, place the tip of this lens-shaped nipper under it. This is its image/form:.
The lens-shaped part of it is smooth, cutting nothing.  The sharp part extends along its sides, as you see, so that the lens-shaped part rests against the peritoneum, and the sharp edge of the nipper is against the bone. Then strike the nipper from one side.
With a small hammer, so that you carefully sever all the bone as it turns, and you are safe from harming the membrane; there will be no frightening accident, even if the surgeon is the most ignorant and timid person, yes, even if he were asleep.
If any part of the membrane remains attached to the bone in some places, scrape it off with the tip of the lens-shaped nipper itself, removing it gently.  It will come off without harm or fear.
If the fracture of the bone did not reach the membrane, and the broken surface of the bone remained rough and with splinters, then you must remove that roughness and those splinters with small, various-sized tools.  Choose the one best suited for each spot, according to what the work requires.
The broken bone, with its shape, should be scraped first with the widest of the tools, then with a narrower one after that. Continue in this way, using successively narrower and finer tools until you get to the finest and most delicate one.
For all smaller, hairline fractures and delicate breaks, a treatment must be used for each one according to what promotes its repair.  This is something not hidden from anyone with even a slight understanding of the craft, and who has considered what we have written and shown about major fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed after removing the bone, a linen cloth, sized to the wound, should be soaked in rose oil and placed over the wound's opening.  Then, another cloth, folded or triangular, should be taken. Soak it in rose water and oil, place it over the first cloth, and do this with the least possible weight to avoid burdening the peritoneum.  Then, apply a broad bandage over it, tightening it only enough to hold the wound in place.
Leave it with this treatment for one or two days until the hot swelling subsides. Then, remove the bandage and use the following drying remedies:  iris roots, caraway flour, kandar pollen, and zarand and similar substances.  Make these into powders.
Dust the wound with these dry powders.  In general, use a remedy that cleanses but does not sting.  Strive, during your treatment, to keep the wound clean, free of fat, filth, or pus.  Do not allow pus to accumulate.
For if pus accumulates on the brain membrane, it corrupts and rots it, causing the afflicted great harm.  This may occur in the peritoneum of the head when the bone is exposed, especially if its treatment is neglected.
If a dark discoloration appears on its surface, and the affected person presents the symptoms we mentioned, know that he is certainly doomed.  If the discoloration resulted from a medicine applied, and the medicine's potency is such that it caused this discoloration,
You should take one part honey and three parts rose oil, thoroughly mix them, and then apply the mixture to a cloth.  Place the cloth on the peritoneum, and treat it with the appropriate remedies until healed.
Chapter Three.
Fractured nose repair.
Know that only the upper portions of the nose, or one of them, can be broken, because they are bony.  The lower part is cartilaginous; it does not break but may be injured, bent, or punctured.
If one side of the nose is broken, you should insert your little finger into the nostril, and straighten the fracture from the inside with your index finger and thumb on the outside, returning the nose to its normal shape.  Do this gently, avoiding causing the patient pain.
If the fracture is high up on the nose and cannot be reached by the finger, it should be straightened with a blunt, slightly thickened instrument. If the fracture is in both sides, do the same.  Promptly treat the fracture on the first day following the break.
If possible, or else after the seventh or tenth day, when the hot swelling subsides, insert a thread of linen into the nostril, if the fracture is on one side, or two threads if the fracture is on both sides.
Let the threads be thick enough to fill the nostril. Some early bone-setters said to soak the threads in clarified butter and change them every day.  I do not agree with this, but the threads should be soaked in egg white mixed with mill dust.
Then leave the threads until the bone is set and the cartilage hardens. You may insert goose quill tubes, wrapped in soft linen, into the nasal cavity where the threads are placed, to hold the fractured nose more firmly and prevent the patient from difficulty breathing.
This is not essential; if you wish, do it, or if you wish, make the threads. If, during your work, a hot swelling appears on the nose, bandage the nose with linen or with cotton soaked in vinegar and rose ointment or some diachylon ointment.
If no hot swelling appears, you should bandage it from the outside with semolina flour and crushed kandar mixed with egg white, then place soft linen over it. Do not bind the nose at all, if the nasal bones are fractured in small pieces.
Or if it shattered, you must cut through it and remove it with the appropriate instrument. Then sew up the gap and treat it with ointments suitable for healing and causing suppuration. If a wound occurs inside the nose, you must treat it with threads and use lead tubes. Until it heals.
Chapter Four.
On the setting of the lower jaw if it is broken.
If the lower jaw is broken, but the fracture is not accompanied by a wound, and if the fracture is only from the outside and it is not broken in two and does not curve inward, then its identification is easy.
If the fracture is on the right side, the index finger of the left hand should be inserted into the patient's mouth.  Likewise, if the fracture is on the left side, the index finger of the right hand should be inserted, and gently lift the bump of the fracture from the inside to the outside.
Your other hand, from the outside of the bone, controls its alignment.  If the jaw fracture has broken in two, then traction should be applied from both sides in a straight line until its alignment is achieved. If it has affected the teeth, they should be treated.
Or, if parts have separated, tie together what you hope to remain joined, with a golden or silver thread or silk thread. Then place a splint of wood on the fractured jaw.  Then place a folded cloth over it, and over the cloth a large, tight bandage or a piece of leather equal in length to the jaw. Then tie it from above according to how you can tie it to match its joining, so that it does not dislocate.  Instruct the patient to rest and abstain from strenuous activity. Provide the patient with soft, easily digestible food.  If you suspect a change in form has occurred, then begin treatment again on the third day.
Then tie it from above according to how you can tie it to match its joining, so that it does not dislocate.  Instruct the patient to rest and abstain from strenuous activity. Provide the patient with soft, easily digestible food.  If you suspect a change in form has occurred, then begin treatment again on the third day. Then repair the bandage with flour from a millstone and egg white, or with semolina flour, after you have removed the splint.  Place a soft compress over the bandage.  As long as the bandage adheres and the bone's condition hasn't changed, leave it in place.
Do not remove it until the fracture heals and strengthens.  Often this fracture takes three weeks to strengthen.  If, during that time, a hot swelling appears, use the methods we mentioned previously to reduce it, until it subsides.
And if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, then if a piece or pieces of bone have become detached, gently remove them with an appropriate instrument.  If the wound opening is narrow, widen it with a forceps, as needed.
Then, if you have removed all the bone fragments, if the wound is wide, suture it.  Otherwise, apply one of the suitable ointments. And treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On the setting of the clavicle if it is fractured.
The clavicle is most often fractured from the front, at the acromial end.  Its fracture occurs in one of three ways: either it fractures and splinters into two pieces without causing any fragments, and this is easiest to set.
Or the fracture may include fragments, which is more difficult to set.  Or the fracture may be accompanied by a wound.  If the fracture is without a wound, have two assistants; one to hold the arm adjacent to the fractured clavicle, and the other to extend the neck towards the opposite side.
Then straighten the fracture with your fingers until its shape is correct, and there are no protuberances or irregularities. If further extension is required, place a ball of linen or wool under the patient's armpit.
Adjust the bone to the extent needed, extend and raise the clavicle, and press the ball with your hand until the fracture is properly aligned. If you cannot draw the clavicle outward because it has retracted into the body, the patient should lie on their back.
Place a moderately sized cushion under the patient's shoulder, and have the assistant press down on the shoulder to raise the retracted clavicle. Then, correct and align the fracture with your fingers.
If you feel that a fragment of the clavicle has broken off and is moving, you should carefully separate and remove it. If the fragment is lodged in the bone, use one of the prepared instruments to cut it out. After placing the instrument that supports the diaphragm, which is made of wood or iron, underneath the clavicle... (and here is its image).
It resembles a spoon without any ornamentation, its width adjusted to the size of the bone, and its length to the extent of the work needed.  Let it have two ends; one wider and the other narrower, as you see.
If the wound you made while removing the bone fragment is wide and you fear inflammation, stitch the edges of the wound together. If the wound is small, or you fear serious inflammation, close the wound with sutures and bandages, in proportion to the size of the wound opening.
If there is a spreading inflammatory swelling, soak the bandage in rose-water and vinegar or a medicinal drink and apply it.  If the bone is fractured without a wound or incision, apply a bandage made of mill-dust and egg white to the bone. Place the soft splint on it, and then place a pad under his armpit if needed. Then take a bandage very long, with a width of one span or thereabouts.  Then apply a folded bandage over the splint and the dressing.  Then take a splint of thin wood. Its width should be three fingers' breadth and likewise in length.  Then enclose it in a cloth, then enclose that cloth with the splint in the part of the bandage that falls on the fracture site.  Then bind the bandage around the fracture as you wrap. And bandage it around his neck and under his healthy armpit and under his injured armpit, and repeat on every side, and how you see that the bandage will securely set the fracture is something clear to you.
The entire aim is that the splint not move from the fractured bone. Therefore, you should examine the patient every day.  Whenever the ligament loosens and you see the splint has shifted, correct it and tighten the bandage.  Then have the patient sleep on their back, and place a small pillow under their armpit when they sleep at night.
So that their arm is raised from their side, and the fractured collarbone is raised with the shoulder or the arm is tied to the neck.  Do not loosen the bandage unless a wound or swelling occurs in the area within twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see reason for it. Maintain the compression and leave it until the fractured collarbone is healed and firm, and it strengthens and becomes more stable within twenty-eight days. And in some people, it may be in less than that.
Chapter six.
Concerning the setting of a fractured shoulder.  The shoulder rarely fractures at its broad part; rather, its bones fracture.  When a fracture occurs in one of its parts, or if it breaks in the middle, the type of fracture is identified by palpation.  Then, according to the appearance of the fracture, apply a compress.
Adjust it and return it to its natural shape by every means possible.  Then, apply a mixture of mill dust, egg white, and soft flaxseed. Place a folded cloth over it, then put a wide splint of wood on top.
Make the splint as wide as the entire shoulder, or slightly wider. If there is a swelling under the splint in any part of the shoulder, smooth that swelling with soft flaxseed until the splint lies flat. Then, tie a long bandage over it.
Tie it firmly and secure the splint so it does not shift from its position.  Examine the bandage every day; whenever the bandage loosens, tighten it and adjust the splint if it moves.  Let the patient lie on their sound side.  The shoulder will be healed within twenty days.
One or twenty-five days.  When this period is complete, loosen the bandage and you will be safe, for it is among the bones that do not fear shattering or dislocation. If a piece of bone protrudes and is irritating the skin, then cut into it and remove it. Do what was mentioned regarding the treatment of a fractured collarbone, in terms of relieving any heat or swelling that occurs.
Chapter Seven.
On setting a fractured rib.
The chest can break in the middle, but this is rare.  However, the ends are more likely to fracture and splinter.  Symptoms of a fractured middle section include tilting downward, severe pain, and difficulty breathing, as well as coughing.
Sometimes blood may be coughed up, and the fracture in the bone will be evident and perceptible to touch.  Treatment involves the patient lying on their back with a cushion between their shoulders.  Then, their sides are pressed together and the ribs are brought together manually, gently smoothing out the affected areas from all possible angles.
Keep applying pressure until the bone returns to its proper shape. Then, apply a bandage and splint, placing a thin board made of poplar, willow, or a similar lightweight wood over the bandage. Then gently secure the board to the fractured bone to prevent its displacement. Wrap the bandage around the back in a circular motion, tightening it firmly.  Inspect the bandage frequently, and whenever it loosens, tighten it again.
If the bandage needs to be removed due to swelling, pain, or inflammation at the site, then promptly remove the bandage and correct the affected area appropriately.  Replace the bandage if deemed necessary. Apply a remedy and maintain the pressure until healing occurs.
Chapter Eight.
In the treatment of fractured ribs, know that fractures typically occur in the thick parts of the ribs near the spine, and their anterior ends.  This is because they are cartilaginous; this fact is readily apparent when examining them with the fingers.
To set a fractured rib, align the fracture with the fingers until the shape is corrected. Then, bandage and splint the fractured bone if necessary. If the fractured rib is angled inward, it will cause the patient intense pain.
And the pain is like the pain that comes from a ruptured bladder, because the bone pierces the diaphragm, and also causes shortness of breath, coughing, and significant blood spitting.  This is difficult to treat, and many complicated remedies have been attempted in the past.
Some have said that the patient should eat foods that produce gas and wind, so that the abdomen inflates, expands, and the fracture is pushed outward.  We dislike this, lest it exacerbate inflammation if it has not already occurred; if it has already occurred, it will worsen and confirm it.
Some said that a cupping device should be applied to the area and then vigorously suctioned.  This is more analogous to a logical approach, but there is concern that the cupping device, due to the area's weakness, will pull excessively at the site. Others suggested that the area should be covered with wool soaked in hot oil.
The space between the ribs is filled until the ligaments are flattened when wrapped around. Then, the patient is treated for a ruptured bladder, with dietary and medicinal remedies. If the patient is severely afflicted, they cannot bear it. The bone was piercing the diaphragm painfully, and we feared for the patient.  Therefore, we must incise the area, expose the fractured rib, then place the instrument that protects the peritoneum (previously described) beneath it, and carefully cut and remove the bone.
Then, if the wound is large, we bring the edges together with stitches and treat it with ointments until it heals.  If a hot abscess occurs in the meantime, promptly apply remedies soaked in rose oil to the area and treat the patient internally to relieve the swelling.
He should lie on the side on which he sleeps more easily until he heals.
Chapter Nine.
On the setting of the vertebrae of the back and neck.
Concerning the bones of the neck, if they are fractured, this rarely occurs.  More often, they are bruised.  Likewise, the vertebrae of the spine.  If this happens to someone, and you wish to know whether they will heal or not, If you see that his hands have become relaxed, numb, and dead, and he cannot move them, extend them, or close them; and if you pinch or prick them with a needle, he feels no pain, then know that in most cases he will not recover, and is doomed.
But if he moves them and feels the pinching and pricking, then know that the spinal marrow has been spared, and the patient will recover with treatment.  If a similar injury affects the vertebrae of the spine, and you wish to know whether they will also recover or not,
Examine his legs. If you see that they are relaxed and the same condition has occurred in them as in his hands, and then, if he lies on his back, urine and feces are expelled involuntarily, and if he lies on his stomach, urine is expelled involuntarily. And if he lies on his back and tries to urinate, he cannot, then know that he is doomed.  Do not bother with his treatment.  If none of these things occur, the matter is less serious.  Treatment for what has occurred should involve attempts to alleviate the hot swelling.
By applying rose oil alone, or with roasted egg yolks, to the injured vertebrae three times a day, until the hot swelling subsides. Then, apply a strengthening, dry dressing to the area, securing it with a bandage.  Order him rest and stillness.
He should sleep only on the side that does not cause him pain until he recovers. If a splinter or something else has broken off the bone from the injury, then it is necessary to cut open the skin and remove that piece of bone. Then, if the wound is large, bring the edges together with stitches, and treat with healing ointments until it heals. If the last bone of the coccyx (the tailbone) is fractured, then insert the thumb of your left hand into the anus.
Then, align the broken bone with the other hand, as much as possible and achievable.  Then apply a bandage and splint, if needed, and secure it.
If you feel a fractured splinter in it, cut open the area and remove it. Treat the wound with the previous treatments until healed.
Chapter Ten.
On the setting of a fractured hip.
Hip bones rarely fracture; if they do, the fracture typically involves splintering at the ends, longitudinal cracking, and inward tilting.  The patient experiences pain, throbbing, and numbness in the affected leg.
To set it, run your hand over it to determine the nature of the fracture. If the fracture is only at the ends, adjust the ends so they match the natural shape.
If the fracture is longitudinal or has tilted inward, lay the patient on their stomach to facilitate setting the fracture.  Once set, apply a bandage, then a splint of wood or leather, and bind it firmly.
Do not worry about the fracture shifting or the splint coming loose.  Fill in any hollows in the hips to ensure the binding is even.  Instruct the patient to sleep on their back or healthy side, if a hot swelling appears.
Refrain from manipulating or setting it until the hot swelling subsides.  Apply what was previously mentioned to soothe it. Then, return to setting and binding it properly. If fragments or splinters are visible in the bone, do not remove them.
And do not touch it internally, but rather adjust it externally as described.  Leave the binding until it heals.
Chapter Eleven.
On setting a fracture of the humerus.
The humerus is the bone between the elbow and the shoulder. If it is fractured, set it using one of two methods.  One is to take a curved, smooth stick of medium thickness, like this:
Tie straps to each end of the stick. Then hang it from a high point.  The patient sits on a chair, and then lays their fractured arm over the stick until their armpit is positioned in the middle of the curve.  Then hang a heavy object from above it.
Or a servant supports the arm downwards, and the physician then straightens the fracture with their hands until it is restored to its proper position. The other method is for the patient to lie on their back and for their arm to be hung from their neck by a strap.
Then instruct two servants, one to hold the upper part of the fracture with their hands, and the other to hold the lower part, and each to pull in their direction.  If you want a stronger pull, secure a strap below the fracture and one above it, and have each servant pull in their direction.
If the fracture is near the shoulder joint, the strap should be positioned under the armpit and the other below the fracture, near the elbow. Similarly, if the fracture is near the elbow, the strap should be placed at that location and on the elbow itself.
Then gently straighten the fracture without force until it is properly aligned and fits well.  Then bind it, unless a hot swelling appears. If a hot swelling does appear, refrain from binding for seven days. Apply moistened wool soaked in vinegar and rose ointment.
Until the swelling subsides, then bind it.  The method of binding is to place the bandage on the fracture, then a layer of new cloth on the bandage. Then bring the arm to the upper arm, and place the hand open on the shoulder.
Hold the cloths and binding on the upper arm and forearm so that the arm acts as a splint, if nothing prevents you from doing so and the fractured bone shows no change. If you fear that the bone may shift, use splints.
This means placing a splint on the fracture itself, wider and stronger than the others.  Keep a finger's width between each splint.  The length of the splints should be according to the fracture, with an additional three fingers on each side.
