Then apply the binding described in the first section to the splints.
The binding should be tighter at the site of the fracture.
The further from the fracture, the less tight the binding should be.  If you see the positioning of the splints and binding, as we mentioned in the context of setting the limb, then do so.
If you fear inflammation, leave the binding and splints until the seventh day, as we stated. Then check the joint every three days to prevent irritation, swelling, or impaired nourishment of the limb due to excessive binding. Correct all of this as described.
If you are certain that none of this will occur, do not loosen the bandage until several days have passed.  The patient should lie on their back, with their hand on their stomach, and a padded, moderate-sized arm sling should be placed under the elbow.
And check at all times, day or night, to prevent the deformed limb from becoming more displaced or the bandage from loosening. Correct all of this diligently. Make the patient's diet as we previously described, first with a soft diet, until the bone strengthens.
Then the diet should be thickened.  The arm and leg typically strengthen in forty days. At that time, the bandage should be loosened, and the patient should use a bath and be treated with suitable ointments, if the fracture is severe and comminuted.
Do not remove the bandage until fifty days, or even two months.
Chapter Twelve.
On the setting of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, called the radii. One is small, located next to the thumb, and the other is large, located beneath the small one, further down.  Sometimes only the larger radius is fractured, sometimes only the smaller one, and sometimes both are fractured.
If the smaller upper radius is fractured, its setting is easier and its healing faster.  If the lower radius is fractured, the fracture is worse and healing more difficult.  The worst case is when both bones are fractured.  If the fractured bone is the smaller upper radius...
When setting it, the physician should apply a gentle, slight traction to align it.  If the larger radius is fractured, the traction should be stronger.  If both radii are fractured, the traction should be considerably stronger.
The hand should be positioned, during the setting and traction, extended on a cushion, with the thumb positioned higher than all the other fingers, and the little finger lower than the other fingers. The patient should be seated upright.
Let the cushion be at the appropriate height so that the patient does not have to strain.
Then, one attendant supports the arm from below, either with their hand or a bandage, and another from above, similarly.  Then, the physician aligns the bone, returning it to the best possible position.  If the fracture has splinters, they must be addressed.
Return each fragment to its proper place, using all your effort.  If a fragment protrudes, pierces the skin, and you have no hope of setting it, incise it and remove it as described previously.  If the fracture involves a wound, this requires a separate treatment.
Then treat that accordingly. If a hot swelling appears at the initial setting, apply a cloth soaked in rose-oil ointment and white wax, neither too thick nor too thin. Bind it firmly to the area until the swelling subsides.
Remove the cloth, apply a bandage prepared from mill dust mixed with egg white, then apply splints.  Let the splint placed directly on the fracture be wider and stronger. Know that in most cases, six splints are needed for the arm.
The fracture may be in one ulna or both ulnae.  Then, make your binding stronger and tighter over the fracture site.  As you work upward or downward with the binding, make it progressively looser, as previously mentioned in the beginning of this chapter.
Let the bandages wrapped around the fracture be soft and moist, not too firm.  Let the thread used for binding be linen, of a medium thickness, as previously described.  Check the limb and ligaments after several days.
If something needs correcting, such as an irritation appearing on the limb, the limb should be bathed in warm water until the irritation subsides.  Leave the limb unbound for the night to rest, then resume the binding.
If the binding has become loose and the bone has shifted or something similar, rectify it to the best of your ability.  Also, if the limb is deprived of nourishment due to excessive binding, loosen it slightly and leave it for several days to allow nourishment to reach it, then re-tighten.
If the patient experiences none of the issues mentioned, the bandage should not be removed until twenty days or so.  Then, suspend the patient's arm from their neck, keeping the arm straight.  The patient should refrain from strenuous movements and sleep on their back.
Know that this arm fracture can be set in thirty, or thirty-two days, and perhaps in twenty-eight days, all according to the patient's temperament and strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
On setting fractures of the hand and fingers.
The metacarpals of the hand and the phalanges of the fingers rarely fracture, but are often bruised.  So, when a fracture or bruise occurs in the hand...
The patient should sit cross-legged with a chair in front of him at the same level.
Then, he places his hand on it outstretched, then the attendant stretches the fractured bone and the physician straightens it until, when it is well aligned, then the bandage and splint should be applied if no hot swelling occurs, then a splint should be applied on top, according to the affected area.
And you should wrap it in a soft cloth.  If the fracture is toward the palm of the hand, create a ball shape from a cloth and instruct the patient to grasp it with their injured hand, then secure it with a long cloth.
The splint should be made of soft leather so that the skin can heal with the flexing of the entire hand, and it should be secured properly. If the fracture is outward, then a splint should be placed above and another below the hand so that the hand remains open and upright.
Then, the splint should be applied as the hand rotates and fastened between the fingers with a bandage. If the fracture affects one of the fingers, if it is the thumb, it should be straightened properly and then secured with the hand; if desired, a small, upright splint can be applied to support the fracture.
And do not move it.  If the fracture is of the other fingers, like the middle, index, ring, or pinky finger, they should be aligned and bandaged with the healthy adjacent finger, or all of them should be bandaged together, which is better, or a small upright splint can be placed on them.
As we mentioned regarding the thumb, and examine during and after your treatment of the inflammation.  If any of that occurs, treat it as described previously.
Chapter Fourteen.
In the treatment of a fractured femur.
The femur frequently fractures and is apparent to touch, since it moves forward and backward.  Its treatment is by tying a bandage above the fracture and another below the fracture, with the patient lying on their face.
Then each attendant stretches the bandage towards its side evenly, if the fracture is in the middle of the bone.  If it is close to the origin of the femur, a soft bandage should be tied, either of wool or similar material.
At the origin of the femur, towards the pubic area, the stretching should be upwards, and the other bandage below the fracture.  Similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, the bandage should be near the knee, to be stretched downwards.  Then the physician straightens the bone with both hands until it is restored to its natural shape.
And the bone will unite well.  Then, the bandage and stretching should be maintained, unless a hot swelling develops on the limb. If a swelling occurs, leave it for a few days until the hot swelling subsides, then return to its treatment.
As for the stretching, a wide, firm bandage should be wrapped around the fracture two or three times, leaving some excess.  Then the leg should be wrapped until the heel reaches the origin of the shin, and a long thread should be inserted between the thigh and the leg near the knee from below, with the ends of the thread being on top on both sides.
Then wrap the excess bandage around the leg and thigh.  Then place splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture itself, and make one splint on the shin bone. Then fill the gap between the thigh and leg with soft cloth to make the stretch even.
Then begin with firm stretching from the middle, over the fracture site, three or four turns.  And as you move the bandage away from the fracture site, let your stretching become less firm, softer, and looser.
Then take the ends of the thread that you inserted between the thigh and leg, and tie to it the adjacent splints from above. Then pass the ends of the thread downwards until you reach the ankle of the leg, and with them tighten the ends of the splints on the other side.
So that the bandage will not shift from its place.  Then leave the stretching on it as long as the limb shows no sores, swelling, or puffiness, or anything similar.  If anything of that sort happens, immediately loosen it, and rectify whatever has occurred as we have repeatedly mentioned.
If there is a splinter in the bone that is piercing, then you should smooth it if possible. Otherwise, cut around it and remove it. Treat the wound as described previously until it heals. This fracture of the thigh may be treated without adding the leg with splints.
As we mentioned regarding the humerus and forearm, except that setting it this way will not cause lameness for the patient. However, if you set it alone without adding the leg, the patient will always limp.
Know that the thigh takes fifty days or a little more or a little less, all depending on the varying temperaments and other conditions.
Chapter Fifteen.
In the fracture of the kneecap.
The kneecap rarely fractures, but is often bruised. If a fracture does occur, it is either a split or a fragmentation of its parts, and this can happen with or without a wound.
And all of this is done by feeling; the fracture is set by bringing the separated parts together with the fingers, in a manner that is achievable with gentleness and care, then a bandage and a circular splint are applied, if needed.
And it is held together with an appropriate bandage on top of that, then all the conditions described in other fractures, such as a hot swelling and the like, are managed by addressing each symptom appropriately until recovery.
Chapter Sixteen.
On setting fractures of the leg.
The leg has two bones, one thick, called the tibia, and the other thin, called the fibula.  They are subject to the same types of fracture as the bones of the arm. Therefore, its setting is like that of the arm, and the procedure is the same. If both bones are fractured, the leg is displaced in all directions. If the thinner bone is fractured, the leg is displaced forward. If the thicker bone is fractured and this occurs from below, this is obvious to you.
Therefore, use traction and alignment, and bind the splints accordingly, regardless.  However, if the fracture of the leg is severe and has many fragments, the traction should be less and gentler, and exert your effort carefully in setting it.
In the case of the leg, there is a supplementary procedure compared to the arm.  After you have aligned the splints and completed all your work, take two pieces of pine wood, which are used in levelling ceilings and are placed between the gaps of the planks or are made of palm leaves or similar materials.
Select from them one that is slightly thick, not thin, and whose length is the length of the leg from the knee downwards. Then wrap each one with a cloth twice along its length, placing one on the leg and the other on the opposite side.
Let them be from the knee to the bottom of the foot. Then tie the two pieces of wood in three places, at the ends and the middle.  With this restraint, the leg will not tilt to either side and will be properly supported.  A wooden gutter may also be used along the length of the leg.
It is placed in it to prevent movement, and this is especially important in fractures accompanied by a wound.  Then, examine the leg daily and meticulously for swelling or inflammation, or any other such problem. If anything of that sort occurs, treat it as appropriate until it heals. The leg bone will be set within thirty days or so.
Chapter Seventeen.
On fractures of the leg and fingers.
The heel bone is not susceptible to fracture.  The leg bones may be fractured, and the fingers are rarely fractured, but more often suffer contusions.
If a fracture of the leg bones occurs and you see the bones are displaced, have the patient stand upright on the ground like a walker.  Then place your foot on the displaced bone, and press down to realign it.
Until it returns to its proper position, then apply the bandage and splint. Place a small, flat board with two ends underneath the sole of the foot. Secure the foot firmly with bandages and other necessary materials.
After three or four days, remove the splint.  You will find the bones aligned, whether they were fractured or dislocated.  The procedure is as described.  If some of the fingers are fractured, realign and set them as described in the section on setting finger fractures.
Then make a splint for the fractured finger that is the length of the finger, slightly wider.  Place the board, as described, under the foot, and secure it firmly. If two, three, or more bones are fractured.
Place a splint on each finger, sized to fit, wrapped in soft cloth. Secure the board under the foot, ensuring each end of the board extends outside the foot's sole for a good fit.
You should not forget to consider all the symptoms of fractures, as described, and treat each symptom appropriately.
Chapter Eighteen.
In fractures of the female pubic bone and the male penis.
When the woman's vulva is fractured, have her sit on her heels, then gently lean her back slightly, holding her from behind.  Then have the midwife pack her vulva with cotton until it is full, like a ball.
Then, the woman is shaken, and her pelvis is raised slightly, bit by bit, and the cotton will come out until it is like a ball at the opening of the vulva and the broken bone is returned to its place.  Then, a pad is placed on her back. When she wants to urinate, the cotton is removed gently so that she can urinate.
And it is returned to the position it was originally in. Then she returns to her first pad. Do this for seven days or so, and it will be healed.  If you wish, you can take a sheep's bladder, tie a reed tube over its opening, and insert the entire bladder into her vulva.
Then, blow into the tube forcefully until the bladder inflates inside the vulva, and the fracture will return.  Then pack it with cotton, and keep her as described for a few days until it heals.  As for when the pubic bone of a man or woman is fractured, this is used in its treatment.
Its treatment is the same as described for the hip bone. The correct approach to these rare fractures, especially for those with some skill and who understand this book, is apparent; because the fracture itself often reveals the way forward.
For the correct way to set and secure it, understand this. Now, if a man's penis is fractured, take a goose's windpipe, insert the penis into it, then place a bandage of cloth over it and tie it.  Leave it for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen.
On setting broken bones if they are accompanied by a wound.
If someone presents with a fracture accompanied by a wound, especially if the bone is large, such as the femur or humerus, or similar, one should promptly perform phlebotomy if the conditions for it are met as previously mentioned.
If the wound is bleeding, one should immediately cauterize it with powdered alum if nothing else is available. Then, proceed to set the fracture on the same day and do not delay.
If no inflammation occurs, if inflammation does occur, then postpone the setting of the fracture until the ninth day, so that the inflammation subsides. Do not approach it at all on the third and fourth days, as it exposes it to poor symptoms.
If the fractured bone is protruding and exposed on the skin, you should gently attempt to reposition and align it with your hands and a slight pull. If you cannot reposition and align it with your hands, reposition it with this instrument, which is made of iron, seven or eight fingers in length. And its width should match the size of the wound.
Therefore, the physician should use three or four of these, according to the treatment required for each type of fracture.  They should be round, with a slight thickness so that they do not bend when used.
It should be sharp-pointed, with a bend at the end.  The top should be thick, and from the middle to the bottom, much thinner.  This is its illustration:.
It is called a "perim" in Greek, meaning a small lever.  The sharp, bent end should be placed on the protruding bone end, and used to push it back once.  If the bone has been repositioned and is somewhat aligned, then complete the alignment by fitting the broken bone ends together.
If the fractured end is thin and the instrument does not grip it well, then the end of that bone must be cut so that the instrument can hold it.  If you cannot reposition the bone as described, then cut it using similar cuts as mentioned previously.
Or spread it open with one of the saws, as you can. Then remove any remaining roughness and thin scales from the bone.  If, after repositioning the bone, the patient experiences severe, painful discomfort, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you can reposition it to its natural place, do so, as you will greatly benefit the patient.  Once the bone is fully set, dip a cloth in a dark, astringent drink, especially if it is summer. Do not apply any bandages or anything greasy to the wound.
So that no rot or decay occurs.  Then, use splints when you are finished setting the bone, and leave the wound uncovered by cutting a hole in the bandages just large enough for the wound with scissors.
Beware, very much, of tightening the wound with the fracture, as ignorant physicians often do this, causing their patients either death or ulceration or cold sores.  Make your tightening gentle and loose, different from the tightening of other fractures.
If the wound is bad or large, and you fear some of the bad symptoms we described, and if he feels disturbing pain in the area, then you should not apply splints. Make bandages of strong cloth where the splints would go, and tighten them with these.
If after a day or two, you see the wound beginning to produce pus, remove the cloth you applied with the drink. Then use the dressings and ointments that we customarily use to treat wounds, such as the four-fold ointment, and similar ones. You should also loosen the bandages.
Examine the wound every evening and morning until it heals and is cured.  The limb should be positioned so that the pus drains downwards easily. If the wound takes many days to heal, and the pus does not stop, know that there are small pieces of bone fragments.
Then you should probe the wound.  Remove and extract any of those fragments that have loosened, and if any of them remain unloosened, causing the limb to be pierced and creating pain, then cut and extract them in any way possible.
If the wound develops a cold, or eating away, or any other type of corruption or putrefaction, then you should treat each symptom with the similar treatment described in its section.
If a large, prominent bone, such as the femur or humerus, or similar large bones, is fractured, do not attempt to pull or remove it. Often death results from this; rather, leave it until it becomes putrid.  Perhaps it will fall out on its own after twenty or thirty days.
Then treat the wound if you see an area for treatment; otherwise, leave it.
Chapter Twenty.
On the treatment of the complications that arise after some fractures.
This complication often arises after the fracture heals, especially when it is near the joints.  It makes the limb's appearance unsightly and may prevent the limb from its natural function.
If the complication is recent, use astringent medications such as dates, frankincense, myrrh, asphodel, acacia and the like, taking some or all of them and mixing them with an astringent drink, or egg white, or vinegar.
Apply it to the complication with difficulty and tie it firmly.  Leave the bandage in place for many days, then remove it and repeat with another bandage until the complication disappears, God willing, or attach a tight lead plate to it.
Lead has the property of removing any abnormal tissue growth from the limb. If the complication has hardened and become severe, and necessity dictates its removal, cut into it from above and remove the excess tissue, or scrape it away with appropriate instruments until it's gone. Treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In treating a fracture, if the limb, after being set, remains thin and weak, unlike its original condition.
If a broken bone is set and the limb remains thin and weak afterward, this is due to many reasons. One reason is either excessive loosening and tying of the ligaments improperly, or excessive tightening of the ligaments, preventing nourishment from reaching the limb; or excessive and improper poulticing; or excessive movement at inappropriate times; or insufficient and weak blood in the patient's body.
The treatment for this is to nourish the patient and strengthen his body so that blood increases, using baths, bringing cheer and joy, and the like. Then apply pitch to the limb to attract nourishment, or poultice it with lukewarm water. Until nourishment flows to it and it returns to its natural form.
Chapter Twenty-two.
On treating fractured bones that have been rejoined crookedly and are prevented from functioning properly.
If, after a limb has been set, a fracture exhibits crookedness, protrusion, or irregularity, and the appearance of the limb is thus marred. However, if the limb is not prevented from its natural function, one should not accept the claim of those who assert that the limb should be broken from the head, since many ignorant physicians and bone setters in our country do so.
This action is highly reprehensible and leads to great harm, even resulting in injury.  However, if the crookedness and irregularity are recent, it is appropriate to apply water in which relaxing herbs such as marshmallow leaves, roots, and crown flower, and similar items have been boiled.
And the limb is bandaged with relaxing dressings, such as well-made diachylon, or the juice of the marshmallow root is taken, mixed with chicken fat and ghee, and bandaged with it; or ripe figs are taken, pounded with pigeon dung, and similar medications called resolvents.
And the irregularity may be resolved by gentle continuous rubbing with the hands, and the limb is moved in all directions at all times. If the crookedness is old, severe, and hardened, and if treatment with iron is necessary.
Then it is necessary to cut it [the bone] above and release the connection of the bone and cut away any excess irregularity or bone with fine instruments, employing gentleness and care. Then treat the wound with what was previously mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
The jaw is the displacement of a joint from its position, hindering movement, disfiguring the limb, and causing severe pain and suffering to the patient.  Therefore, when a jaw is dislocated, one should immediately attempt to realign it.
Delay this absolutely not, for if delayed, the area will swell, forming a mass that makes realignment difficult.  Therefore, one must not delay or move or stretch it while it is swollen, because it often causes painful spasms and suffering to the patient.
However, if this occurs, one should first bleed the patient, then wait until the swelling subsides somewhat, and then apply warm water and ointment to the limb.  Then, gently realign it and treat each part as described in its respective section.
The sections on the jaw are also arranged, as previously presented in the fractures, from the upper to the lower body.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On the treatment of the lower jaw.
The jaws dislocate rarely.  Their dislocation occurs in one of two ways: either they depart slightly from their positions, becoming somewhat relaxed, or they completely and entirely dislocate, becoming relaxed. They may be displaced toward the chest, so that the patient's saliva runs and they cannot retain it, nor can they close their jaws or articulate words, their tongue trembling.  If the dislocation is slight, it will usually return on its own with the least effort.
However, if the dislocation is complete, one must quickly restore it and delay nothing.  The patient's attendant should hold the patient's head while the physician inserts the thumb of one hand into the base of the jaw inside the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side.
Or if the dislocation is on both sides, both thumbs should be inserted; and the rest of his fingers should adjust from the outside.  He should instruct the patient to relax and open their jaw in every direction. The physician should adjust and push the jaw back to its proper place.
If restoring them is difficult, especially if both jaws are affected, apply warm water and ointment compresses to ease their return.  Do not delay their repositioning, as we have said. When they have been repositioned correctly and the patient's mouth is closed and not relaxed, then apply strips of cloth with a salve made from wax and rose ointment.
Then gently tie with a loose bandage. The patient should sleep on their back, with their head supported between two pillows, so that they do not move it to the right or left, or chew anything.  Their diet should consist of soft, easy-to-swallow foods.
Until the pain subsides and the jaw sets, let him eat whatever he desires.  Use gentleness in this, and do not force his mouth open for eating, drinking, or yawning until the jaw is firm and healed.
If the jaws are difficult to reposition after they have dislocated and not returned to their proper places, fevers and persistent headaches often occur, and the patient's abdomen may become distended, and vomiting may frequently happen.  If you see this, know that the condition is serious.
Often, death occurs within ten days for those experiencing this condition.
Chapter Twenty-five.
On repositioning the clavicle and shoulder joint.
The clavicle does not dislocate from the inner side due to its connection to the chest.  It may dislocate outward, which is apparent to the touch.  To reduce it, the patient should lie on their back with their arms extended.
Then, press firmly on the affected area with your hand, and it will return to its place.  Then apply a bandage and splints, and secure them tightly.  The portion of the clavicle near the shoulder, where it connects, rarely dislocates.  If it does, it should be repositioned as we have described, using whatever methods seem appropriate to you.
Then apply a bandage, splints, and binding.  Instruct the patient to remain still and quiet until healed.  This same treatment will reposition the shoulder joint if it also dislocates.
Chapter Twenty-six.
In reducing a shoulder dislocation.
Know that the shoulder dislocates in three ways: one, toward the armpit, downwards; two, toward the chest; and three, occasionally, upward, above the shoulder.
It does not dislocate behind the shoulder blade, nor in front of the nerve.  Most often, it dislocates downwards, towards the armpit, especially in those with little muscle, as it moves quickly in and out in them.
However, those with substantial muscle are different, meaning that it comes out with difficulty and goes back with difficulty.  Sometimes, someone might experience a blow or fall, causing the shoulder to swell and become a hot lump.  They might think it is dislocated, but it is necessary to examine this to determine the truth of the matter.
Then, in treating it, if the dislocation is downwards towards the armpit, if you align the dislocated and healthy shoulder, you will find an obvious difference between them. You will find a prominence on the head of the dislocated shoulder and, when palpating under the armpit, the head of the dislocated shoulder will feel like an egg.
The patient cannot raise their arm to their ear or move it in any way.  Similarly, if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards, you will find it obvious upon palpation; it will not be hidden.
This dislocation may be easily reduced if it is fresh or the patient is a child.  To reduce it, have the attendant raise the patient's arm upwards. Then, place the thumb of your hands under their armpit and forcibly raise the joint upwards to its proper position while the attendant raises and extends their arm upwards.
Then, bring it downwards, and it will return quickly. If it does not return as described, and the dislocation has been present for many days, the patient should bathe in hot water and use poultices that relax and soften, such as cooked nose-root, fenugreek, and crown-of-thorns in water and apply them.
Then the patient lies on their back, and a ball of wool of moderate firmness is placed under their armpit.  The physician places their heel on the ball, and holds the patient's hand in their hand, extending it downwards.
And the physician pushes the ball with their foot, and it will return to its place.  Or, if you prefer, you can reduce it in this way: Have a man taller than the patient stand by the side, and place his shoulder under the patient's armpit, raising the armpit upwards, so that the patient is suspended in the air. And another attendant pulls the patient's hand downwards towards their stomach.  If the patient is light, then something else should be hung from them to weigh them down, and the dislocation will return immediately.  It may also be reduced in another way.
Another method is to place a long, round-headed piece of wood on the ground, like the pestle head of a mortar—not thick, nor thin.  A soft cloth is placed on the top of the wood, and then the wood is placed under the patient's armpit. The patient stands along the length of the wood.
Then the patient extends their hand downwards on the other side, and also stretches their body powerfully on the other side. The joint will quickly return to its place. If the dislocation resists all the previously mentioned methods, use this treatment: take a piece of wood about two cubits long. And its width should be about four fingers, and its thickness about two fingers.  It should have a rounded head to easily enter the armpit in this way:
Then a soft cloth is tied to the round head so that the wood does not injure the patient.  It is then placed under the armpit. The entire hand and arm are extended along the wood downwards, and the wood is tied to the upper arm, forearm, and hand.
Then the arm is placed on a crossbar.  The arm is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is left hanging on the other side.  The joint will then enter its place. If it has entered in any way possible, a moderate-sized ball of wool should be placed under the armpit.
Then the prepared dressing, made of mill dust, frankincense, and egg white, is applied to the entire shoulder, moving from top to bottom.  The ball is then firmly tied under the armpit, and the bandage is wrapped around the dressing from above. The patient's hand is tied to the neck.
The hand should not be moved for seven days. The patient should be given little food to strengthen the limb, which promotes quicker healing.  Then, after seven or five days, the bandage is removed, and the limb is exercised. If the limb remains firm and does not relax, the patient is healed.
If the joint frequently dislocates due to moisture or another cause, cauterization with three cauterizing agents should be used, as previously described in the chapter on cauterization. If all this is done, and the bandage is removed after seven days, and the joint does not become stable,
If the dressing and bandage are repeatedly applied without success, and the joint dislocates and relaxes, and the arm cannot be raised, know that the nerve in the shoulder's upper part has been severed, stretched, or relaxed. In this case, know that the joint will never become stable.
As for the dislocation of the jaw, located near the chest and breast and behind it, it is returned by pushing and pulling with the hands until it returns to its place.  All other binding and treatments should be used until healed. If after healing, the limb feels tender and movement is slow,
The patient should use baths repeatedly until the tenderness subsides and returns to its original condition.
Chapter Twenty-seven.
On treating dislocation of the elbow.
The elbow joint dislocates with difficulty, and similarly returns with difficulty.  It dislocates in all directions, especially forward or backward.  Its dislocation is not hidden from you, since it is visible and palpable, in whatever manner it is dislocated.
If you compare the dislocated elbow to the healthy one, the dislocation will be clearly apparent by the depression of the joint. The arm cannot be bent, nor can the shoulder be brought to it.  You should quickly reduce the dislocation before a hot swelling develops.
If a hot swelling develops, reducing the dislocation becomes difficult, and it may never heal, especially if the dislocation is backward.  This is the worst type of dislocation, the most painful, and often leads to death.
If it can be reduced, have an assistant extend his/her arm with both hands, while the physician's hands are positioned above and below the elbow.  The physician pushes the joint back into place using the thumbs of both hands or the base of both palms.
If the dislocation is forward, the hand may be bent once so that the base of the palm strikes the corresponding shoulder.  If this does not return the dislocation, use very strong extension;  two assistants should extend the arm.
And the patient should also be held by two assistants so that he doesn't move during the extension.  Then the arm should be rotated in every direction after wrapping a folded cloth or wide bandage around it.  When the physician begins to manipulate the joint with his hands, he should rub the joint with ointment to facilitate its easy movement.
Then the joint should be pushed forcefully until it returns to its place.  After its reduction, the bandage containing a compress, drying agent, and egg white should be applied tightly, and the arm should be suspended from the patient's neck and left for several days.
Then it is released.  If the joint is firmly in place, release the bandage and leave it.  If you see that the joint is not sufficiently strong, reapply the bandage and bandage and leave it for several days until it strengthens, and then release it.  If the joint experiences discomfort after its return and release.
If movement is difficult, use moist heat in a bath, gentle massage, and gentle prodding until it softens, or apply a thick, fat sheep's fleece to the joint, then bandage it and leave it on for a day and a night.  Then remove it and bathe him.  When he sweats, massage the joint moderately.
Then apply the sheep's fleece again, twice, and three times, in conjunction with bathing, until it softens. If you prefer to apply dampened cow dung mixed with clarified butter and bind it to the joint, do so repeatedly. This will also soften and restore it to its original condition.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
On the treatment of a dislocated wrist.
The wrist frequently dislocates and its reduction is easier than with other joints, but it is necessary to expedite its reduction at the moment of dislocation, before inflammation or a hot swelling appears in the area.
To reduce the dislocation, place the patient's wrist on a board.  A helper should extend the patient's arm. The physician should place their hand on the prominent part of the joint and push it until it returns.  However, one must check if the dislocation has moved to the inside of the hand.
Let the patient place the outside of their hand on the board during extension and reduction. If the dislocation is outward, then the patient's inside hand should be on the board so that the physician's hand can be positioned on the same prominence of the joint. If it returns immediately, well and good. Otherwise, apply a bandage to soothe the swelling.
Leave it alone; do not attempt to repeat the procedure.  After several days, you will not be able to reduce it, and the joint will remain crooked.  This will not harm the patient, unless the hand becomes weak and is unable to grasp anything.
At that point, you know that the nerve is severed or injured, and there is no remedy except to apply cauterization.  Perhaps this will help, perhaps not.  Once the wrist is reduced, apply the bandage we described, then bind tightly and leave it on for five days.
Then it is removed, and the hand is exercised. If its movement is difficult and there is any stiffness, soften it with warm water and rub repeatedly until it softens.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Fingers may dislocate in any direction. When a finger dislocates to the back or palm of the hand, extend the finger and push the dislocation back with your thumb until it returns to its place. Then bandage the knuckle and sling it towards the direction of the dislocation.
Leave it for two days, then release it and extend it until it is straight the following day.  If it is nighttime, bandage it in the same way.  Continue to unbind it during the day and exercise it with movement, then bandage it at night. Do this for several days until it strengthens.
And do likewise if it dislocates to the palm of the hand, bandaging it towards that same direction, and do with it your initial procedure until it heals. And do likewise for any other direction of dislocation.
Chapter Thirty.
In treating the dislocation of the spine.
If a vertebra of the spine or neck dislocates completely, or many vertebrae are displaced from their positions, there is no treatment, for death rapidly comes upon the patient.
A sign of this is that the patient's stool comes out involuntarily; he cannot contain it.  Often, some of his limbs, either his legs or arms or one of them, become relaxed.  If only one vertebra is displaced, it often shifts.
And its displacement can occur in four directions.  The one that moves backward is called a hump.  There is no treatment for a hump that developed from childhood; it will not heal at all.  But for one that developed from a fall, blow, or similar injury...
The ancients have described various treatments with lengthy explanations, most of which are of little benefit.  I have condensed this to what is sufficient, encompassing much of what they proposed by approaching the matter from a different perspective and clarifying it, as well as illustrating the mechanism in a different way from what they have explained and described.
So I say that a hump that forms in the front of the chest cannot be treated or cured, and neither can one that forms to the sides.  However, the one that occurs in the back is treatable, specifically by the method I describe: the patient should be laid on their face.
On a flat surface, near a wall, spread a damp cloth underneath to avoid injuring the chest.  Then place a vertical plank, planted in a hole in the ground, near the head, and another plank near the feet at the opposite end of the platform.
A servant holds one plank, not fixed in the hole, and another servant holds the other in the same manner.  Then, a soft, strong bandage is wrapped around the patient's chest and under the armpits.  The end of the bandage is extended to the plank near the head and tied to it.
Then another bandage is tied around the hips, knees, and ankles.  All the bandages are gathered and tied to the other plank near the feet.  Then each servant pulls on their plank with the bandage, and the planks remain in their fixed positions.
However, as we said, they are not fastened. The physician places both hands firmly on the hump until it straightens, or places a board on it and leans on the board with his feet until it straightens. If this treatment is ineffective, take a board about three cubits long.
A hole is made in the wall, which we said should be near the patient, to accommodate the end of the board.  The middle of the board is placed on the hump, and the physician places his feet on the other end, pulling firmly to compress the hump.
And it returns to its place. If you wish, make it with a hand-wound coil, by driving two planks, each one cubit long, into the ground at the patient's head and at the feet, at the far end of the shop.
Let there be a span between each plank.  A hole is made in each plank for the coil to pass through.  All the planks are firmly anchored to the ground so they cannot move. A twisted stick—the coil—is inserted, into the holes in the planks, and the bandage is wound around the coil in the holes of both planks.
A hole is made at its end, in which a stick, one span long, has been secured by winding, and the same is done on the other two planks.  Then the bandage that was tightened around the patient's chest is tightened onto the coil at the head, and the bandages around the legs are tightened onto the coil at the feet.
Then a servant stands by each coil, twisting the coil with the twisting tool.  The physician adjusts the hump as previously described. This is the image of the coil, the shop, and the patient.
Then, after the hump returns and the area is even, it is necessary to apply a bandage soaked in egg white, followed by a linen dressing. Then, place a splint of wood over the bandage, with a width of about three fingers, and a length that covers the hump.
And apply some correct beads to it, then tie with the necessary bandage. The patient should consume mild foods until recovery. If some protrusion remains at the site after recovery, then use medicines that relax and soften, along with the splint described for a long time.
A lead plate may be used in this case.  A protrusion might appear at the end of the spinal beads, leading to the suspicion of dislocation. This may be an extra bone that has protruded.  Do not apply this treatment to it, for death may result.
Chapter Thirty-One.
In the treatment of the dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the shoulder joint are only subject to dislocation, and not to the slight displacement and subluxation that occur in other joints.  The hip joint dislocates in four ways.
This is because it dislocates inwards, outwards, forwards, and backwards.  It dislocates inwards most frequently, and rarely forwards or backwards.  A sign of dislocation inwards is that if you align the patient's healthy leg with the affected one, the affected leg will appear longer.
The knee will be more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient will be unable to bend their leg at the hip.  The area below the hip will be swollen and distinctly swollen, indicating that the head of the thigh bone has moved there.
The signs of dislocation outwards will be the opposite of these signs.  The sign of dislocation forward is that the patient straightens the leg completely, but cannot bend it without pain, and if they attempt to walk, they cannot walk forward.
The patient retains their urine and their hip is spasmed.  When walking, the patient walks on the heel.  The sign of dislocation backward is that the patient cannot straighten the knee, nor bend it before bending the hip, and the leg is also shorter than the other.
The hip is relaxed, and the head of the thigh bone is visible near the waist.  As for the reduction of these dislocations, if the dislocation has been longstanding and the patient has not attempted reduction, and it remains in its state, there is no cure at all.
Therefore, one should not treat it.  As for a recent dislocation, regardless of which of the four types of dislocation it is, quickly move the joint, extending it inward and outward and moving it left and right, perhaps...
If it returns, no further treatment is needed. If it does not return this way, a strong assistant should be positioned to pull the leg from below, either with their hands or a bandage tied above the knee, and another assistant pulls from above.
By placing their hands under his armpits, then tightening a soft bandage on the thigh's root, a third assistant grips the bandage's end, and pulls, either forward from the hip to the collarbone, or backward to the back.
All of them pull simultaneously until the patient raises his body from the ground and remains suspended.  This type of pulling is common to all four types. If the dislocation returns as described, otherwise, each type requires its own specific treatment as described.
For its specific return, if the dislocation is inward, the patient should lie on his healthy side, then a bandage is placed on the thigh root, between the head of the thigh and the area below the groin.
Then the bandage is pulled upward from the groin towards the upper body, towards the collarbone.  Then a strong assistant with both arms embraces the thick part of the affected thigh and pulls outwards forcefully, and it will return to its place.
This type is easier than other methods of returning this limb.  If this method is unsuccessful and the limb does not return, then the patient's legs should be bound with a strong, soft bandage around the heels and knees.
Each bandage should be a distance of four fingers from the other.  The affected leg should be extended farther than the other, by a distance of two fingers.  Then the patient is suspended from the head by a beam in the house, and this is a distance of two arms from the ground.
Then, a strong boy is instructed to embrace the head of the thigh, while another boy holds the patient and pushes the boy holding the thigh forcefully. The joint will return to its place quickly.  As for its specific return, if the dislocation is outward...
The patient should lie on the bed, as described for the humpback patient.  The bandage should be tightened around the affected leg and chest. Then, two beams are placed, one at the patient's feet and the other at the head.  A further beam is placed firmly in the middle of the bed.
Damp cloths should be wrapped around it to prevent injury to the patient. The beam should be positioned between the patient's thighs to prevent downward displacement during stretching.  Each attendant pulls in their direction, and the physician adjusts the hip with his hands. If it returns to its place...
Otherwise, place the board on him and press it as described for the humpback, except that the patient should lie on his healthy side.  If the dislocation is forward, the patient's leg should be stretched in this same manner on the bed.
The physician places the right hand on the affected groin, and presses it with the other hand.  The pressure should be extended downward toward the knee. If the dislocation is backward, the patient should not be stretched downward.
He is raised from the ground, but should be positioned on a firm surface.  Also, the dislocated hip should be outward, as described in the patient's position on the bed, face down, with bandages tight.
As previously stated, the board should also be used to press the area where the joint has dislocated.  When the hip joint returns properly, the sign of its return is unmistakable:  stretch the patient's legs.
If you see them level, and the patient can flex and extend the leg without difficulty, know that the limb has returned to its proper position.  Then, bring the thighs together, apply the bandage, and tie it tightly with a turban, ensuring the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient must rest for three or four days.  Then, remove the bandages and dressings.  Compare the legs; if they are the same size, know that the joint has set.  Release the patient to walk, unless you see some weakness in him.
Return and bandage it again, tightening it as you did initially. Leave it for three more days, then remove it.  Gradually allow the patient to walk over several days until it strengthens, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-two.
On treating the knee joint.
The knee dislocates in three ways: inward, outward, and downward (i.e., backward).  It does not dislocate forward.  A sign of dislocation is to tell the patient to bring their leg close to their thigh.
If the leg does not adhere to the thigh, know that the knee is dislocated.  To reduce all types of dislocation, the patient should sit down with their legs extended, if they are strong enough to do so.  A helper should sit behind the patient, holding the patient's torso and gently pulling it backward.  Then, you should sit on the patient's thighs.
Place your back against the patient's face, and put the patient's leg between your legs.  Then, grasp the knee with both hands, interlocking your fingers on the knee.  Then, firmly press the sides of the knee with your hands.  A second assistant should extend the leg until the knee returns to its place.
A sign of its return is that the leg will adhere to the thigh gently, without resistance.  Then bandage it, and keep the leg attached to the thigh.  Then, bind them both with a bandage for three or four days, then release them.  Only allow the patient minimal walking for several days until the strength returns.
If you are unable to return it using the method described, then use strong traction with bandages as previously described in the treatment of the hip until it returns.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
On the treatment of a dislocated heel.
The heel may be displaced slightly or completely dislocated.  Dislocation may be inward or outward.  A sign of dislocation is that the heel appears swollen and protruding towards the side to which it has dislocated.
As for treating a slight displacement, it is easy to return it.  Gently pull with the hands and straighten it until it returns.  However, if it is completely dislocated, the patient should sit down, and a strong attendant should hold it from behind in the middle of his back.
Then, with your right hand, grasp his foot from the top, and with your left hand, from the bottom, at the heel. Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and then with your left hand, twice. Then extend it with the left hand, and push the front of the foot towards the leg with the right hand, without force.
Repeat this twice as described. Then, on the third time, push the front of the foot toward the leg while pulling at the heel. If it returns in one or two repetitions with the foot aligned, then stop. Otherwise, repeat the procedure; it will return.
If it does not return using the method described, lay the patient on his back on the ground.  Securely drive a stake into the ground, placing it between his legs, wrapped in cloth to prevent injury to the patient.  Then an attendant should hold his thigh.
Then, another attendant extends the leg, either by hand or with a bandage tied around the patient's leg. Each attendant pulls in a direction opposite the other. The stake remains positioned between the patient's legs, holding the patient to prevent his body from moving downward during the pulling. Then the physician straightens the joint with his hands.
Another attendant holds the healthy leg below.  If the joint returns quickly, then take the bandage and splint, and tie it firmly with bandages. Tie the foot with a bandage downward.  Care must be taken to avoid the nerve which is above the heel from behind.
So that the bandage is not too tight and causes him pain.  Then leave it for two or three days.  If the bandage loosens, tighten it, and then release it on the third or fourth day. The patient should avoid walking for forty days.
If he attempts to walk before this time, you cannot be sure that the joint will not dislocate and become unfixable.  Treatment will not be accepted thereafter. If a hot swelling appears, you should apply the treatments described previously, in other parts of the medical text, for soothing until it subsides.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
On the treatment of the joint of the toes.
One should gently realign any dislocated toes by a slight stretching, without force. This is not difficult but easy. If the dislocation is in one of the toes of the foot, the patient should place their foot on a level surface.
Or, standing like a walker, place your foot on the protruding joint. Then press down firmly with your foot until it returns to its proper position and no protrusion is visible.
Then, place a board under the sole of his foot, covering the entire foot, with two ends.  Secure it tightly for three days. Then, release it and keep him from walking for many days, until it strengthens, and a return is avoided, God willing.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
On the types of dislocation that occur with a wound, or with a fracture, or with both.
When something like this occurs and you attempt treatment and reduction, it often results in death.
Therefore, one should not undertake such treatment unless one is skilled in the craft, experienced, gentle, compassionate, cautious, and not rash or reckless.  Initially, one should use remedies that alleviate only the inflamed swellings and allow the patient to recover.
Except by Your grace, O God, if you hope for the patient's freedom from harm, along with the mildness of the illness, and some hope appears to you, then return it in its first instance, before the inflamed swelling occurs.
If the limb returns to its proper position, use the treatment that relieves inflamed swellings and treat the wound with appropriate drying ointments. If the dislocation is accompanied by a fracture and detached fragments have formed in the bone, remove them. Adhere to what we have previously mentioned regarding similar simple ailments in their respective sections.
Strive diligently and purify yourself from entering the path of recklessness, as I have previously instructed.  This will preserve your reputation and safeguard your standing, God willing.
